# Phase 3 — Local LLM & Inference

> Run → fit → serve. Deferred until Phase 1 foundations make it comprehensible.


---

## 3.1 Ollama & LM Studio

> Run an LLM on your laptop today. After this, "run a model locally" stops being
> `compile llama.cpp and hunt for GGUFs` and becomes *one command* — plus an
> **OpenAI-compatible localhost endpoint**, so any app written for OpenAI points
> at your machine by changing a URL. The keystone artifact is Ollama's
> **Modelfile** (a Dockerfile for models). Concept + run-it; *which* quant to pull
> and *will it fit in VRAM* are 3.5 / 3.6.
> **Prerequisites:** 1.7 (quantization concept). **Pays off in:** 3.5–3.6 (fitting
> models). **Video:** `T2`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `local-llm/OLLAMA_LMSTUDIO.md` (§0 the two-wrapper lineage; §2A the Modelfile + its gold parse; §2D the shared OpenAI-compatible API) · `local-llm/ollama_lmstudio.py` (Section A = the gold Modelfile parser; Section D = the shared `/v1/chat/completions` request body) — re-run it, reproduce the gold parse, then **extend it** with a fixture client (Step 4).
- **Canonical:** Ollama *Modelfile Reference* + *OpenAI compatibility* docs (docs.ollama.com) · LM Studio *OpenAI Compatibility Endpoints* docs (lmstudio.ai/docs/developer) · HuggingFace *GGUF* docs (the shared model format both consume).
- **Goal:** state, from memory, what Ollama & LM Studio do that raw llama.cpp doesn't, write a `Modelfile`, and explain how the OpenAI-compatible `/v1/chat/completions` endpoint turns a cloud→local swap into changing one URL.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **Both are user-friendly wrappers over the *same* engine — they neither write inference kernels nor invent a model format; they consume GGUF (the format built for llama.cpp/GGML) and orchestrate it.** The HF GGUF page lists both side-by-side as GGUF consumers, and GGUF is "designed for use with GGML and other executors," developed by the llama.cpp author. `Verifies:` HuggingFace *GGUF* docs — "GGUF … a binary format that is optimized for quick loading and saving of models … designed for use with GGML and other executors. GGUF was developed by @ggerganov who is also the developer of llama.cpp"; under "Usage with open-source tools" it lists `llama.cpp`, `LM Studio`, `GPT4All`, `Ollama` (https://huggingface.co/docs/hub/en/gguf) + local `local-llm/OLLAMA_LMSTUDIO.md` §0 ("both wrappers around the same engine: llama.cpp (the GGML compute graph). Neither writes inference kernels"), whose architecture cites `ollama/ollama` (Go server spawning a llama.cpp runner subprocess) and `lmstudio.ai` (Electron app over a llama.cpp/MLX binding).
- **Ollama is CLI/server-first (Go, MIT): `ollama pull` + `ollama run`, and an OpenAI-compatible API always-on at `localhost:11434`.** It brings the `docker pull` / `docker run` model to models and runs headless in CI. `Verifies:` Ollama *OpenAI compatibility* docs — example client `base_url='http://localhost:11434/v1/', api_key='ollama', # required but ignored`, with `curl http://localhost:11434/v1/chat/completions` (https://docs.ollama.com/api/openai-compatibility) + Ollama *OpenAI compatibility* blog — "use the same OpenAI format and change the hostname to `http://localhost:11434`" (https://ollama.com/blog/openai-compatibility).
- **LM Studio is GUI-first (Electron): an in-app HuggingFace model browser + chat + side-by-side compare, and a local OpenAI-compatible server at `localhost:1234`.** Its value is discovery/UX, not a new engine. `Verifies:` LM Studio *OpenAI Compatibility Endpoints* docs — "You can reuse existing OpenAI clients (in Python, JS, C#, etc) by switching up the 'base URL' property to point to your LM Studio," "The following examples assume the server port is `1234`," with `curl http://localhost:1234/v1/chat/completions` (https://lmstudio.ai/docs/developer/openai-compat) + Vercel AI SDK *LM Studio* provider — "LM Studio … contains an OpenAI compatible API server … LM Studio uses port `1234` by default" (https://ai-sdk.dev/providers/openai-compatible-providers/lmstudio).
- **The cloud→local swap is changing the `base_url` — nothing else.** Because both expose OpenAI's `/v1/chat/completions` request/response shape, an app written against OpenAI reaches your laptop by repointing one URL (and a throwaway api_key for Ollama). That single property is the whole pitch. `Verifies:` LM Studio OpenAI-compat docs — "reuse existing OpenAI clients … by switching up the 'base URL'" (https://lmstudio.ai/docs/developer/openai-compat) + Ollama OpenAI-compat blog — "change the hostname to `http://localhost:11434`," Python example `OpenAI(base_url='http://localhost:11434/v1', api_key='ollama')` (https://ollama.com/blog/openai-compatibility). *(The OpenAI Chat Completions API at platform.openai.com/docs/api-reference/chat is the canonical shape both comply with.)*
- **Ollama's `Modelfile` is a Dockerfile for models — `FROM` a base, `PARAMETER` overrides, `SYSTEM` prompt, `TEMPLATE`, `ADAPTER`, `LICENSE`, `MESSAGE`.** It pins a base model and overrides its runtime behaviour declaratively. Key params: `temperature` (default `0.8`), `num_ctx` the context window (default `2048`), `top_p`, `stop`, plus `num_gpu` (GPU-layer offload) exposed via the API `options`. `Verifies:` Ollama *Modelfile Reference* — "A Modelfile is the blueprint to create and share customized models using Ollama"; instruction table `FROM`/`PARAMETER`/`TEMPLATE`/`SYSTEM`/`ADAPTER`/`LICENSE`/`MESSAGE`; "`num_ctx` … (Default: 2048)"; "`temperature` … (Default: 0.8)" (https://docs.ollama.com/modelfile) + Ollama *API* docs — the "With options" request lists `temperature`, `num_ctx`, `top_p`, `stop`, `num_gpu`, `num_thread`, `use_mmap`, `main_gpu` under `options` (https://github.com/ollama/ollama/blob/main/docs/api.md). *(The gold parser in `ollama_lmstudio.py` Section A implements these directives and type-coerces the values.)*
- **`num_ctx` is a Modelfile (or `options`) setting, NOT an OpenAI-API field — the OpenAI API has no context-size knob.** To grow the window you bake `num_ctx` into a custom model via a `Modelfile` (`FROM … / PARAMETER num_ctx <size>` → `ollama create`), because the OpenAI request body simply has no field for it. That detail is the bridge to **3.6 VRAM math** (`num_ctx` sizes the KV cache linearly → 1.5). `Verifies:` Ollama OpenAI-compat docs — "The OpenAI API does not have a way of setting the context size for a model. If you need to change the context size, create a `Modelfile` which looks like: `FROM <some model>` / `PARAMETER num_ctx <context size>`" (https://docs.ollama.com/api/openai-compatibility) + local `local-llm/OLLAMA_LMSTUDIO.md` §5 pitfall ("Assuming `num_ctx` is free … `num_ctx` sizes the KV cache linearly. 32K ctx can double VRAM use vs 4K").
- **Both auto-detect the GPU (Metal on Apple Silicon, CUDA on Nvidia, ROCm on AMD) and offload layers; `num_gpu` is the manual override.** Why it matters is **1.4**: decode is **memory-bandwidth-bound**, so running it on GPU (HBM ≈ TB/s-class bandwidth) vs CPU (DRAM ≈ tens-of-GB/s) is a large per-token-speed gap — the same prefill-vs-decode split, now applied to "which device holds the weights." `Verifies:` Ollama API docs — `options` includes `num_gpu` ("GPU layer offload count") and `main_gpu` (https://github.com/ollama/ollama/blob/main/docs/api.md) + the **1.4 carry** — decode "incurs a similar level of I/O to the prefill phase, making it constrained by the GPU's memory bandwidth" (DistServe §2.1, https://arxiv.org/html/2401.09670v3; carried from 1.4). *Flagged: the exact auto-offload heuristic ("how many layers fit") is a llama.cpp/Ollama practitioner detail, not a single canonical citation — the principle (GPU bandwidth >> CPU ⇒ offload speeds decode) is fully verified via 1.4.*
- **The wins are privacy + zero per-token cost + offline** — the prompts never leave the machine and there is no meter. That is inherent to *local* execution, and it is why these tools exist at all. `Verifies:` Ollama OpenAI-compat blog — framed as running models "locally" behind `localhost:11434` (https://ollama.com/blog/openai-compatibility) + LM Studio developer docs — the server is a *local* REST/OpenAI-compatible endpoint on the user's own machine (https://lmstudio.ai/docs/developer/openai-compat). *(The "privacy/offline" payoff is a direct consequence of local execution rather than a quoted sentence — flagged for honesty.)*

> **⚠ Flagged — the local bundle is stale on one point.** `OLLAMA_LMSTUDIO.md` §0/§5 claims **"LM Studio has no CLI / no headless mode."** That is **outdated**: LM Studio 0.4.0+ ships **`llmster`**, a standalone daemon that runs the LLM server *without the GUI* (`lms daemon up`), plus a desktop-headless mode. `Verifies:` LM Studio *Run LM Studio as a service (headless)* docs — "LM Studio can be run as a background service without the GUI. … **llmster** … a standalone daemon, no GUI required … It can run on Linux boxes, cloud servers, GPU rigs, or your local machine without the GUI" (https://lmstudio.ai/docs/developer/core/headless). **Correct framing for the article:** LM Studio is *GUI-first by design* (its headline value is the model browser + compare), and historically was GUI-only; Ollama is *server/CLI-native from day one*. The honest distinction today is default posture and primary UX, not "Ollama can be scripted and LM Studio can't."

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. What do Ollama and LM Studio actually do that raw llama.cpp doesn't — and what do they *not* do (which engine still runs the math)?
2. Write a `Modelfile` from memory. What do `FROM`, `PARAMETER` (e.g. `temperature`, `num_ctx`), `SYSTEM`, and `TEMPLATE` each do — and what are the defaults for `temperature` and `num_ctx`?
3. How does the OpenAI-compatible `/v1/chat/completions` endpoint turn a cloud→local swap into changing *one URL*? What stays identical, and what is the one thing you must supply for Ollama even though it's ignored?
4. Why can't you set the context window (`num_ctx`) through the OpenAI API — and how *do* you grow it? (→ tease 3.6)
5. CPU vs GPU: why does offloading layers to the GPU speed up *decode* specifically? (tie → 1.4: decode is memory-bandwidth-bound)
6. What are the trade-offs of going local vs a frontier cloud model — speed, context, quality — and how should that shape *which* task you point at a local model?
7. Ollama vs LM Studio: given a scripted CI pipeline vs a "browse and compare models" afternoon, which do you reach for and why? (and why is "LM Studio can't be scripted" no longer literally true?)

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `ollama_lmstudio.py` (reuses the `parse_modelfile` parser from the bundle; adds a deterministic fixture client). **Determinism:** the Modelfile text, the fixture JSON, the prices, and the two base URLs are all pinned constants; there is **no `random`/`Date.now()`/network** — the "API call" reads an in-repo **fixture**, so output is byte-stable on re-run. (A live Ollama/LM Studio server is non-deterministic and machine-dependent, so the gold is *defined* by the fixture, exactly like the recorded-response pattern in 2.1.)
- **(a) Parse a pinned `Modelfile`** into `{base, params, system}`:
  ```python
  GOLD_MODELFILE = (
      'FROM llama3.2\n'
      'PARAMETER temperature 0.7\n'
      'SYSTEM "You are helpful"\n'
  )
  parsed = parse_modelfile(GOLD_MODELFILE)
  # -> {"from": "llama3.2", "params": {"temperature": 0.7}, "system": "You are helpful", ...}
  ```
- **(b) A client that calls a *fixture* `/v1/chat/completions` response** (deterministic, offline) and prints the reply + `usage`. Build the request body **once**, then show it targets **both** base URLs — only `host:port` differs:
  ```python
  FIXTURE = {
    "id": "chatcmpl-local-000000000000",
    "object": "chat.completion",
    "model": "llama3.2",
    "choices": [{"index": 0, "finish_reason": "stop",
                 "message": {"role": "assistant",
                             "content": "A Modelfile pins a base model, runtime parameters like temperature, and a system prompt."}}],
    "usage": {"prompt_tokens": 14, "completion_tokens": 18, "total_tokens": 32}
  }
  BASE_URLS = {"Ollama": "http://localhost:11434/v1", "LM Studio": "http://localhost:1234/v1"}
  # the request body is IDENTICAL for both; only base_url changes:
  body = {"model": parsed["from"],
          "messages": [{"role": "system", "content": parsed["system"]},
                       {"role": "user", "content": "In one sentence, what does a Modelfile pin?"}],
          "temperature": parsed["params"]["temperature"]}
  # the client would POST body to each BASE_URLS[*] + "/chat/completions";
  # here we read the fixture so the demo runs offline:
  resp = FIXTURE
  reply = resp["choices"][0]["message"]["content"]; u = resp["usage"]
  ```
- **Gold value (must match on re-run):** parsed Modelfile **`{from: "llama3.2", params: {temperature: 0.7}, system: "You are helpful"}`** · mocked reply **`"A Modelfile pins a base model, runtime parameters like temperature, and a system prompt."`** · `usage` **`total 32 == prompt 14 + completion 18`** · the **same** `body` resolves to both `http://localhost:11434/v1/chat/completions` (Ollama) and `http://localhost:1234/v1/chat/completions` (LM Studio) — the cloud→local swap is one URL.
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: ``[check] Modelfile parsed: from='llama3.2', params={'temperature': 0.7}, system='You are helpful'`` · `[check] fixture reply parsed: choices[0].message.content == gold` · `[check] usage total == prompt + completion (32 == 14 + 18)` · `[check] same body -> both base_urls (11434 / 1234), only host:port differs`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** bridge Modelfile → request — take a richer `Modelfile` (`FROM llama3.2` / `PARAMETER temperature 0.8` / `PARAMETER num_ctx 8192`) and (i) map the parsed `temperature` into the request body's `temperature`, proving the Modelfile param *is* the API knob; (ii) print the one thing the OpenAI API **cannot** carry — `num_ctx` — and emit the `ollama create` recipe (`FROM … / PARAMETER num_ctx 8192`) that bakes it into a custom model (per the Ollama docs). Re-run the fixture at `num_ctx 4096` vs `8192` and print the KV-cache bytes delta with the 1.5 formula (`2·layers·kv_heads·ctx·head_dim·bpe`) — the visible cost of growing the window, and the seed for **3.5** (which quant) and **3.6** (will it fit).

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *Run an LLM on your laptop today (Ollama & LM Studio).*
- **Angle:** one command pulls a model; both tools expose an OpenAI-compatible localhost endpoint, so swapping cloud→local is changing a base URL — and zero per-token cost. The keystone artifact is the Modelfile.
- **Payload:** what the wrappers add over raw llama.cpp (download/registry, the Modelfile, GPU auto-offload, the OpenAI API) · the Modelfile (`FROM`/`PARAMETER`/`SYSTEM`/`TEMPLATE`) and its key params (`temperature`, `num_ctx`) · the OpenAI-compatible swap (one URL; same body for Ollama `:11434` and LM Studio `:1234`) · why GPU offload speeds decode (→ 1.4, memory-bound).
- **Gotcha:** local models are **smaller and slower than frontier cloud models** — shorter effective context, lower peak quality — so point them at tasks they can actually carry (drafting, private Q&A, tool calls) rather than expecting GPT-4-class behaviour. (And `num_ctx` is *not free*: it sizes the KV cache — set the task, then the window.)
- **Video (`T2`):** Hook("one command, an OpenAI-compatible localhost LLM, zero per-token cost") → Mechanism(`ollama pull` → `Modelfile` parse → `/v1/chat/completions` on `:11434` / `:1234`) → Gold(parsed Modelfile + mocked reply + `usage 32`) → Gotcha(smaller/slower than frontier — pick the task).

### 6. Checklist
- [ ] demo runs, exits 0, ≥2 `[check]` lines
- [ ] output byte-stable on re-run
- [ ] gold value reproduced, matches reference
- [ ] ≥2 web sources logged with `Verifies:` lines
- [ ] article centers on one annotated diagram + ≥1 gotcha
- [ ] HyperFrames video renders, 6 beats, gold-check badge green

## 3.2 MLX (Apple Silicon)

> Why a Mac is a serious local-LLM box. After this, "VRAM" stops being a separate
> chip and becomes *your RAM* — on Apple Silicon the CPU and GPU share one physical
> memory pool (unified memory), so the model fits iff your RAM holds it (no separate
> GPU-VRAM ceiling to trip over) — and the single fact that makes a Mac fast at
> decode (generation is memory-bandwidth-bound, tie 1.4) is the same fact that caps
> it below a data-center GPU. Run-it unit; *how the engine fuses Metal kernels* is
> the bundle, *full VRAM budgeting and serving math* is 3.6.
> **Prerequisites:** 1.4 (memory-bound decode), 1.7 (quant). **Pays off in:** 3.6 (VRAM math on Mac). **Video:** `T2`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `local-llm/MLX_INFERENCE.md` (§0 TL;DR's three edges — unified memory, lazy eval, op fusion; §2a unified memory = one pool, zero copy, `stream=` not `.to()`; §4 the decode roofline `tokens/s ≈ bandwidth / weight_bytes`; §5 the `mlx-lm` CLI + `QuantizedLinear`) · `local-llm/mlx_inference.py` (Sections A–E: the zero-copy model, the lazy graph, the K-fold fusion math, the modelled decode roofline). **Read §0/§2/§4; lightly skim §2c–§2d (fusion/transforms) — the *mechanism* is the bundle, this unit is the *fit decision*.**
- **Canonical:** Apple MLX docs (ml-explore.github.io/mlx) — *Unified Memory* + the home/index page · `mlx-lm` README (github.com/ml-explore/mlx-lm) · Apple tech specs (M2 Max memory bandwidth). One-line goal: state, from memory, what unified memory changes about the fit math, why decode being bandwidth-bound makes MLX fast on a Mac, and when a Mac wins vs a CUDA GPU.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **MLX is Apple's array framework purpose-built for Apple Silicon.** It is a NumPy-like array library written by Apple Machine Learning Research specifically for Apple's chips — not a port of a split-RAM framework. `Verifies:` MLX docs, *Home* — "MLX is a NumPy-like array framework designed for efficient and flexible machine learning on Apple silicon, brought to you by Apple machine learning research" (https://ml-explore.github.io/mlx/build/html/index.html) + MLX docs, *Unified Memory* — "Apple silicon has a unified memory architecture. The CPU and GPU have direct access to the same memory pool. MLX is designed to take advantage of that" (https://ml-explore.github.io/mlx/build/html/usage/unified_memory.html).
- **Unified memory = the CPU and GPU share the SAME RAM; no CPU↔GPU copy.** Every other major framework (PyTorch MPS, llama.cpp Metal, TensorFlow Metal) inherits a split CPU-RAM / GPU-VRAM design and bolts a copy path on top. MLX arrays live in one shared pool; you pick the device at *op* time (`stream=mx.gpu`), not array time — there is no `.to('gpu')`, and the GPU's matmul and the CPU's byte-read hit the *same bytes*. `Verifies:` MLX docs, *Unified Memory* — "rather than moving arrays to devices, you specify the device when you run the operation. Any device can perform any operation on `a` and `b` without needing to move them from one memory location to another" (https://ml-explore.github.io/mlx/build/html/usage/unified_memory.html) + MLX docs, *Home* — "the unified memory model. Arrays in MLX live in shared memory. Operations on MLX arrays can be performed on any of the supported device types without performing data copies" (https://ml-explore.github.io/mlx/build/html/index.html).
- **On a Mac, "VRAM = your RAM" — the model fits iff your RAM holds it; there is no separate GPU-VRAM ceiling.** Because the GPU draws from the same unified pool as the CPU, there is no second, smaller VRAM budget to trip (the way a 24 GB RTX card caps a model that your 128 GB of system RAM could otherwise hold). The only budget is total RAM minus what you leave for the OS and your apps. `Verifies:` MLX docs, *Unified Memory* — "Apple silicon has a unified memory architecture. The CPU and GPU have direct access to the same memory pool" (https://ml-explore.github.io/mlx/build/html/usage/unified_memory.html) + `mlx-lm` README, *Large Models* — models "fit in RAM" and run from that same pool, tunable via the macOS wired-memory limit ("If the model fits in RAM then it can often be sped up by increasing the system wired memory limit," https://github.com/ml-explore/mlx-lm). *(Flagged: the punchy phrasing "VRAM = RAM" is a practitioner summary of unified memory, fully grounded by the MLX docs above — there is literally one pool.)*
- **Decode is memory-bandwidth-bound — the tie to 1.4.** Generating one token means streaming essentially the whole model's weights once per token; compute is cheap relative to that memory load, so decode tokens/s is gated by memory *bandwidth*, not FLOPs. That single fact is why a framework that deletes memory traffic (MLX: zero-copy + op fusion + compiled kernels) directly buys tokens/s on a Mac. `Verifies:` DistServe §2.1 — "each decoding step only processes one new token … despite processing only one new token per step, the decoding phase incurs a similar level of I/O to the prefill phase, making it constrained by the GPU's memory bandwidth" (https://arxiv.org/html/2401.09670v3) + Anyscale — "LLM inference is memory-IO bound, not compute bound … it takes more time to load 1MB of data to the GPU's compute cores than it does for those cores to perform LLM computations on 1MB" (https://www.anyscale.com/blog/continuous-batching-llm-inference). *(Both sources already logged in 1.4.)*
- **`mlx-lm` runs inference and fine-tunes on top of MLX.** `pip install mlx-lm` gives a CLI (`mlx_lm.generate` / `mlx_lm.chat`) and Python API that loads thousands of `mlx-community/*` models from Hugging Face (default `Llama-3.2-3B-Instruct-4bit`), quantizes them to 2/4/8-bit native MLX format, and does LoRA + full fine-tuning. `Verifies:` `mlx-lm` README — "MLX LM is a Python package for generating text and fine-tuning large language models on Apple silicon with MLX," with "Low-rank and full model fine-tuning" (https://github.com/ml-explore/mlx-lm) + MLX docs, *LLM inference* example (examples/llama-inference.html, linked from https://ml-explore.github.io/mlx/build/html/index.html).
- **The gotcha — Mac memory bandwidth is far below a data-center GPU, so each token decodes slower even though bigger models fit.** An M2 Max has **400 GB/s** of unified-memory bandwidth; an NVIDIA A100 has **~2.0 TB/s** and an H100 **~3.35 TB/s** — ~5× and ~8× more. Since decode tokens/s ≈ `bandwidth / weight_bytes` (the roofline from 1.4), the same model decodes ~5–8× faster per token on an H100 than on an M2 Max. A Mac *fits* a bigger model (more RAM, one pool); a data-center GPU *generates each token faster* (more bandwidth). `Verifies:` Apple Support, *MacBook Pro (16-inch, 2023) Tech Specs* — "Apple M2 Max chip … 400GB/s memory bandwidth" (https://support.apple.com/en-us/111838) + Apple Support, *Mac Studio (2023) Tech Specs* — "Apple M2 Max chip … 400GB/s memory bandwidth" (https://support.apple.com/en-us/111835). *(NVIDIA A100 ≥2 TB/s and H100 ~3.35 TB/s were verified in 1.4 from the NVIDIA product pages, https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/data-center/a100/.)* *(Flagged, order-of-magnitude: the bundle's "MLX ~2–3× faster than llama.cpp on Apple Silicon for decode" is a widely-reported practitioner figure, not a single canonical citation — it is context, not this unit's gold value; the verified gold is the fit decision, not the end-to-end speedup.)*

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. What does "unified memory" mean on Apple Silicon, and why does it change the fit math for an LLM — what is there *none* of that a CUDA box has?
2. State the "fits if RAM holds it" rule from memory: what do you add up (weights + KV), what do you subtract (OS reserve), and where does each term come from (1.7 / 1.5)?
3. Why is decode memory-bandwidth-bound (tie 1.4), and why does *that* single fact make MLX's design (zero-copy + fusion + compiled kernels) pay off on a Mac?
4. When does a Mac *win* vs a CUDA GPU for local LLMs, and when does it *lose* — what single spec decides decode speed?
5. A 32 GB Mac loads Llama-3-8B at 4-bit @ 8k context: does it fit, and what's the headroom? An 8 GB Mac with the same model — fit or flag, and why does the KV cache tip it over?
6. Why does "more RAM" help a Mac *fit* bigger models, while "more bandwidth" is what makes each *token* faster — and why is an H100 still ~8× faster per-token on the very same model?

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `mlx_inference.py` (the topic's demo — extends the existing bundle with a **unified-memory budget checker**; reproduces the bundle's gold first, then the fit decision is *this* unit's new gold). **Determinism:** every config is a pinned constant; the math is pure arithmetic on those constants — **no RNG, no `Date.now()`, no network**; output is byte-stable on re-run.
- **Reuse the two formulas from Phase 1.** Weights (from 1.7): `weights_bytes = params × bpw / 8`. KV cache (from 1.5): `kv_bytes = 2 × n_layers × n_kv_heads × n_ctx × head_dim × bpe` (the leading `2` is K+V; `bpe=2` for fp16). The budget checker just adds them and compares to the RAM left after the OS reserve.
- **Pinned model — Llama-3-8B** (`params=8,030,261,248`, `n_layers=32`, `n_kv_heads=8`, `head_dim=128`, GQA from 1.5), **4-bit, ctx 8192, fp16 KV**:
  - weights = `8,030,261,248 × 4 / 8 = 4,015,130,624 B` → **`4.02 GB`** (reproduces 1.7's table exactly).
  - KV @ 8k = `2 × 32 × 8 × 8192 × 128 × 2 = 1,073,741,824 B` → **`1.07 GB`** (double 1.5's 4k `512 MiB` → `1024 MiB`).
  - **footprint = `5,088,872,448 B` = `5.09 GB`**.
- **Gold value (must match on re-run) — Mac A (M2 Max, 32 GB, OS reserve 5.0 GB):** available = `32 − 5.0 = 27.0 GB`; `footprint 5.09 GB < 27.0 GB` ⇒ **FITS**, headroom = `27.0 − 5.09 = 21.91 GB`. *(Decision + headroom is the gold.)*
- **The flag — Mac B (base M2, 8 GB, OS reserve 3.5 GB):** available = `8 − 3.5 = 4.5 GB`; `footprint 5.09 GB > 4.5 GB` ⇒ **DOES NOT FIT**, headroom = `−0.59 GB`. The teaching beat: the **weights alone fit** (`4.02 < 4.5`) but **weights + KV tip it** (`5.09 > 4.5`) — the KV cache is exactly what flips a "yes" into a "no" (why the budget is a *cache* question, not just a *weights* question, tie 1.5/3.6).
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] FIT: Llama-3-8B Q4 @8k on 32GB M2 Max -> FITS, headroom 21.91 GB > 0` · `[check] FLAG: Llama-3-8B Q4 @8k on 8GB M2 -> DOES NOT FIT, headroom -0.59 GB < 0 (weights fit, +KV tips it)` · `[check] weights == params*bpw/8 == 4015130624 B (4.02 GB)` · `[check] KV@8k == 2*32*8*8192*128*2 == 1073741824 B (1.07 GB)`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** add the **decode roofline** (tie 1.4) — `tokens/s ≈ bandwidth / weights_GB`: M2 Max `400 / 4.02 ≈ 99.6 tok/s` vs A100 `2000 / 4.02 ≈ 498 tok/s` (5×) vs H100 `3350 / 4.02 ≈ 834 tok/s` (8×). Then print a **fit table** across Mac RAM sizes (`8 / 16 / 32 / 64 / 96 / 128 GB`, reserve 5.0) for this same model and show the 8 GB row is the only one flagged — the visible payoff of "VRAM = RAM," and the seed for **3.6** (VRAM math on Mac).

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *MLX: why Macs punch up at local LLMs.*
- **Angle:** on Apple Silicon, **VRAM *is* RAM** — unified memory erases the separate GPU-VRAM ceiling, so the fit question collapses to one subtraction: `footprint (weights + KV) vs (RAM − OS reserve)`. The same memory-bandwidth-bound decode from 1.4 is what makes a Mac *good* at it (MLX deletes copy + intermediate traffic) and what caps it below a data-center GPU (less raw bandwidth).
- **Payload:** unified memory (one pool, zero copy, `stream=` not `.to()`) · the "fits if RAM holds it" rule with the weights (1.7) + KV (1.5) terms · memory-bandwidth-bound decode (tie 1.4) and why it favors MLX on Mac · the `mlx-lm` CLI (`mlx_lm.generate --model mlx-community/...-4bit`).
- **Gotcha:** Mac memory bandwidth (M2 Max **400 GB/s**) is ~5–8× below an A100/H100, so a Mac **fits** a model an RTX can't (one big pool), yet decodes each **token slower** than an H100 — capacity vs bandwidth are two different axes, and a Mac trades the second for the first.
- **Video (`T2`):** Hook("on a Mac, VRAM *is* your RAM") → Mechanism(unified memory → one pool → no CPU↔GPU copy) → Gold(the fit decision: 8B Q4 @8k → 5.09 GB → FITS on 32 GB with 21.91 GB headroom; FLAGGED on 8 GB) → Gotcha(bandwidth: M2 Max 400 GB/s vs H100 ~3.35 TB/s → ~8× slower per token).

### 6. Checklist
- [ ] demo runs, exits 0, ≥2 `[check]` lines
- [ ] output byte-stable on re-run
- [ ] gold value reproduced, matches reference
- [ ] ≥2 web sources logged with `Verifies:` lines
- [ ] article centers on one annotated diagram + ≥1 gotcha
- [ ] HyperFrames video renders, 6 beats, gold-check badge green
- [ ] all 6 probe questions answered out loud, no notes

## 3.3 Open WebUI

> Your private ChatGPT, on your own hardware. After this, "I want the ChatGPT UI
> but my data never leaves my laptop" is *one Docker command* — a self-hosted
> front-end that talks to the Ollama endpoint from 3.1 and layers on multi-user
> accounts, model management, RAG over your docs, and tool calling. It runs the
> chat experience; the model still comes from Ollama (or any OpenAI-compatible
> endpoint).
> **Prerequisites:** 3.1 (Ollama & the OpenAI-compatible `localhost:11434` endpoint).
> **Pays off in:** self-hosted chat. **Video:** `T3`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundle:** `local-llm/OPEN_WEBUI.md` (§0 TL;DR — it does **not** run models, it connects to backends; §1 the lineage raw → stuff → RAG → hybrid; §1 the backend table `Ollama localhost:11434` / `OpenAI` / `OpenAI-compat <any base_url>`; §3 install + the canonical `docker run` + the RAG env vars; §5 the `host.docker.internal` pitfall; §6 cheat sheet) — focus on the **front-end-over-Ollama** framing and the Docker deploy. The RAG mechanics (chunk → embed → vector search → rerank → inject) are **Phase 2** (2.5–2.9); here we only need "Open WebUI *orchestrates* that pipeline over a backend you point it at."
- **Canonical:** Open WebUI docs — [Getting Started](https://docs.openwebui.com/getting-started/) ("runs anywhere (Docker, Kubernetes, pip, bare metal)... connects to Ollama, OpenAI-compatible... out of the box") · [Features](https://docs.openwebui.com/features/) · [GitHub README](https://github.com/open-webui/open-webui) (the exact `docker run` one-liner) · Ollama [OpenAI compatibility](https://ollama.com/blog/openai-compatibility) (the `/v1/chat/completions` endpoint Open WebUI targets).
- **Goal:** state, from memory, what Open WebUI *is* (a front-end, not a model server), what it adds over raw Ollama, how it connects (an OpenAI-compatible base URL), and the single `docker run` that stands up a private ChatGPT.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **Open WebUI is a self-hosted, ChatGPT-style *front-end* — it does not run the LLM itself; it connects to a model backend (Ollama, or any OpenAI-compatible endpoint) and layers a chat UX + features on top.** The same way a browser is not the web server. `Verifies:` Open WebUI GitHub README — "an extensible, feature-rich, and user-friendly **self-hosted AI platform** designed to operate entirely offline... supports various LLM runners like **Ollama** and **OpenAI-compatible APIs**" (https://github.com/open-webui/open-webui) + Getting Started — "Open WebUI runs anywhere (Docker, Kubernetes, pip, bare metal) and **connects to Ollama, OpenAI-compatible, and Open Responses providers out of the box**" (https://docs.openwebui.com/getting-started/) + local `local-llm/OPEN_WEBUI.md` §0 — "Open WebUI is a **self-hosted web frontend** for LLMs. It does **not** run models — it connects to backends (Ollama, OpenAI, Anthropic, or any OpenAI-compatible API)."
- **It connects via the OpenAI-compatible Chat Completions interface — so "point it at Ollama" or "point it at vLLM/LM Studio/OpenAI" is the *same* config: a base URL.** Ollama itself exposes OpenAI's `/v1/chat/completions` at `http://localhost:11434`; the OpenAI Python/JS libraries reach it by setting `base_url`. Any client that speaks that shape — Open WebUI included — swaps cloud→local by changing one URL. `Verifies:` Ollama *OpenAI compatibility* blog — "use the same OpenAI format and change the hostname to `http://localhost:11434`," with `curl http://localhost:11434/v1/chat/completions` and `OpenAI(base_url='http://localhost:11434/v1', api_key='ollama')` (https://ollama.com/blog/openai-compatibility) + GitHub README — "Effortlessly integrate OpenAI-compatible APIs... Customize the OpenAI API URL to link with **LMStudio, GroqCloud, Mistral, OpenRouter, and more**" (https://github.com/open-webui/open-webui). *(This is the direct payoff of 3.1's OpenAI-compatible endpoint — Open WebUI is one such client.)*
- **What it adds over raw Ollama — the headline features: multi-user accounts + RBAC, model management (a "Model Builder"), RAG over your documents, native tool/function calling, and a polished chat UX.** Raw Ollama is a CLI/server with no accounts and no document chat; Open WebUI is the product layer. `Verifies:` Features docs — "Open WebUI is **multi-user from day one**. Define roles, create user groups... RBAC"; "📚 **Knowledge & RAG**... let the AI retrieve exactly the information it needs"; "🐍 **Tools & Functions**... Python scripts that run in the chat"; "🧩 **Model presets**... pick any base model, bind knowledge, tools, and a system prompt" (https://docs.openwebui.com/features/) + GitHub README — "🛡️ **Granular Permissions and User Groups**," "🔐 **Role-Based Access Control (RBAC)**," "🛠️ **Model Builder:** Easily create Ollama models via the Web UI," "📚 **Local RAG Integration**," "🐍 **Native Python Function Calling Tool**" (https://github.com/open-webui/open-webui).
- **RAG over your docs, orchestrated by the front-end: upload → chunk → embed → vector DB → cosine top-k → optional hybrid (BM25 + vector) with cross-encoder rerank → inject the top-k into the prompt → LLM answers grounded in *your* data.** Open WebUI does not invent this pipeline (that's Phase 2); it ships it as a managed feature, with a choice of vector backends. `Verifies:` Features docs — "📄 **9 vector databases** — ChromaDB and PGVector (officially maintained), plus community options: Qdrant, Milvus, Elasticsearch, and more"; "🔍 **Hybrid search** — BM25 + vector search with cross-encoder reranking"; "Choose between vector search (RAG) for large collections or full-content injection for precision" (https://docs.openwebui.com/features/) + GitHub README — "📚 **Local RAG Integration**... groundbreaking Retrieval Augmented Generation (RAG) support using your choice of **9 vector databases**" (https://github.com/open-webui/open-webui) + local `local-llm/OPEN_WEBUI.md` §2a (the six-stage pipeline diagram) / §2e (hybrid + rerank).
- **Deploy via Docker — the officially recommended path; one command.** The image is `ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main`; it exposes container port `8080` (you map a host port, e.g. `3000:8080`), and you **must** mount `-v open-webui:/app/backend/data` or your DB/chats are lost. `Verifies:` Getting Started — "🐳 **Docker** — One-command deploy, the officially recommended path" (https://docs.openwebui.com/getting-started/) + GitHub README — "When using Docker to install Open WebUI, make sure to include the `-v open-webui:/app/backend/data`... This step is crucial as it ensures your database is properly mounted"; canonical command `docker run -d -p 3000:8080 --add-host=host.docker.internal:host-gateway -v open-webui:/app/backend/data --name open-webui --restart always ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main` (https://github.com/open-webui/open-webui). *(A `pip install open-webui && open-webui serve` path also exists — lightweight, no Docker — serving on `http://localhost:8080` per the GitHub README.)*
- **The value = a private ChatGPT on your own hardware: the prompts and your documents stay on your machine, there is no per-token meter, and it works offline.** That is the entire reason to self-host a front-end instead of using the hosted product. `Verifies:` GitHub README — "self-hosted AI platform designed to operate **entirely offline**" (https://github.com/open-webui/open-webui) + Features docs — "**One interface for every AI model. Private, extensible, and built for teams.**" (https://docs.openwebui.com/features/) + local `local-llm/OPEN_WEBUI.md` §1 — "a ChatGPT-grade interface (RAG, tools, multi-user) over *your own* model on *your own* hardware — private, offline-capable, and free." *(The "data never leaves your machine" payoff is a direct consequence of local execution with a local backend, rather than a single quoted sentence — flagged for honesty.)*
- **Gotcha #1 — it is a *front-end*: the model still runs in Ollama (or your chosen endpoint), so Open WebUI's own footprint is small but the heavy lifting (weights, VRAM, decode) is the backend's problem (→ 1.4 / 3.6).** You have not escaped inference cost; you've moved the *interface* to your side. `Verifies:` local `local-llm/OPEN_WEBUI.md` §0 / §6 cheat sheet — "Open WebUI : SELF-HOSTED FRONTEND (does NOT run models)" + GitHub README — the model runners are Ollama / OpenAI-compatible APIs; Open WebUI is the "AI platform"/interface over them (https://github.com/open-webui/open-webui).
- **Gotcha #2 — the Docker networking trap: when Open WebUI runs *in* Docker but Ollama runs *on the host*, `localhost:11434` from inside the container is the *container*, not your laptop.** Use `host.docker.internal:11434` (with `--add-host=host.docker.internal:host-gateway`) or `--network=host`. This is the single most common "why won't it connect?" failure. `Verifies:` GitHub README — "it's often due to the WebUI docker container not being able to reach the Ollama server at 127.0.0.1:11434 (host.docker.internal:11434) inside the container. Use the `--network=host` flag... or the `--add-host=host.docker.internal:host-gateway` canonical command" (https://github.com/open-webui/open-webui) + local `local-llm/OPEN_WEBUI.md` §5 — "Backend not reachable... Use `host.docker.internal:11434` (not `localhost`) when Open WebUI runs in Docker but Ollama runs on the host."

> **⚠ Flagged — the local bundle's vector-DB roster is stale.** `OPEN_WEBUI.md` §2f lists the 9 backends as *ChromaDB, PGVector, Qdrant, Milvus, Elasticsearch, OpenSearch, Redis, MongoDB Atlas, SurrealDB*. The **current** Open WebUI docs/README list the 9 as *ChromaDB + PGVector (official); Qdrant, Milvus, Elasticsearch, OpenSearch, **Pinecone, S3Vector, Oracle 23ai** (community)* — i.e. Redis/MongoDB/SurrealDB have been replaced by Pinecone/S3Vector/Oracle 23ai. `Verifies:` GitHub README — "9 vector database options including ChromaDB, PGVector, Qdrant, Milvus, Elasticsearch, OpenSearch, **Pinecone, S3Vector, and Oracle 23ai**" (https://github.com/open-webui/open-webui) + Features docs — "📄 **9 vector databases** — ChromaDB and PGVector (officially maintained), plus community options: Qdrant, Milvus, Elasticsearch, and more" (https://docs.openwebui.com/features/). **The count (9) and the official pair (ChromaDB + PGVector) are stable; the specific community backends are a moving target — cite the count, not the full list.**

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. What *is* Open WebUI — a model server, or something else? Does it run the LLM itself, and if not, where does the model actually run?
2. How does Open WebUI connect to Ollama — and why does "OpenAI-compatible endpoint" mean the *same* config also reaches vLLM, LM Studio, or OpenAI itself? (tie → 3.1's `localhost:11434` swap)
3. What does Open WebUI add over raw Ollama — name the headline features (multi-user/RBAC, model management, RAG, tool calling)?
4. Why self-host a chat front-end instead of using ChatGPT — privacy, no per-token cost, your data stays on your hardware?
5. Recite the one `docker run` that stands it up pointing at a host Ollama — which volume mount is *mandatory*, and why `host.docker.internal:11434` instead of `localhost:11434`?
6. Open WebUI is a front-end, so what actually scales (weights, VRAM, decode) as you add concurrent users — and which earlier unit (1.4 / 3.6) owns that cost?

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `open_webui.py`. **Determinism:** every target value is a pinned constant; there is **no `random`/`Date.now()`/network** — the "deploy" is *rendered as a string and validated locally*, never executed, so output is byte-stable on re-run. (Actually running `docker run` is machine/state-dependent and non-deterministic; the gold is *defined* by the pinned target, exactly like the recorded-response pattern in 2.1 / 3.1.)
- **Config/deploy sketch generator:** given a pinned target — `image`, `host:container` port, `OLLAMA_BASE_URL`, `volume`, `name`, `restart`, and the `--add-host` gateway flag — emit (a) a single canonical `docker run` line and (b) its `-e` env-var block. Then **validate** the render:
  - each `-e K=V` has an uppercase `K` matching `^[A-Z][A-Z0-9_]*$` and a non-empty `V`;
  - `-p` is `int:int` (here `3000:8080`);
  - `-v` is `name:path` and the **mandatory** data volume `open-webui:/app/backend/data` is present;
  - the image matches `^[a-z0-9._-]+(:[a-z0-9._-]+)?$`;
  - `OLLAMA_BASE_URL` parses as `scheme://host[:port]`.
- **Gold value (must match on re-run):** the pinned target
  `image=ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main`, `port=3000:8080`,
  `OLLAMA_BASE_URL=http://host.docker.internal:11434`,
  `volume=open-webui:/app/backend/data`, `name=open-webui`, `restart=always`,
  `--add-host=host.docker.internal:host-gateway` renders **exactly**:
  ```
  docker run -d -p 3000:8080 --add-host=host.docker.internal:host-gateway -e OLLAMA_BASE_URL=http://host.docker.internal:11434 -v open-webui:/app/backend/data --name open-webui --restart always ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main
  ```
  with env block `OLLAMA_BASE_URL=http://host.docker.internal:11434` — the one required var for a host-Ollama deploy.
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: ``[check] docker run == gold (well-formed: -d, -p 3000:8080, -v, --name, image) -> OK`` · ``[check] required env present: OLLAMA_BASE_URL=http://host.docker.internal:11434 -> OK`` · ``[check] mandatory data volume mounted: -v open-webui:/app/backend/data -> OK``.
- **Extension (make it yours):** re-render for two more pinned targets and validate each — (i) **OpenAI-API-only** mode: drop `OLLAMA_BASE_URL`/`--add-host`, add `-e OPENAI_API_KEY=$KEY`, keep the volume (the validator should now require `OPENAI_API_KEY` and flag a missing base URL as *expected*, not an error); (ii) the **bundled-Ollama** all-in-one image `ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:ollama` mounting `-v ollama:/root/.ollama` — and observe it needs **no** `OLLAMA_BASE_URL` because the engine lives in the *same* container. Print which of the three configs is the lightest to type vs. the most isolated — that decision (separate engine vs. single container) is the seed for **3.4** (hardware landscape) and **3.11** (a real inference server, vLLM).

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *Your private ChatGPT (Open WebUI, self-hosted).*
- **Angle:** ChatGPT's UI, your hardware, your data stays home. It is a *front-end* — one Docker command, pointed at the Ollama endpoint from 3.1 — that turns a local model server into a multi-user, RAG-capable product.
- **Payload:** what Open WebUI *is* (a front-end over a backend, not a model runner) · what it adds over raw Ollama (multi-user/RBAC, Model Builder, RAG, native tool calling) · the OpenAI-compatible connection (one base URL reaches Ollama, vLLM, LM Studio, or OpenAI) · the one `docker run` + the mandatory `-v open-webui:/app/backend/data` volume.
- **Gotcha:** it is a front-end — the model still runs in Ollama/an endpoint, so the weights, VRAM, and decode cost are the backend's (→ 1.4 / 3.6); and when both run in Docker vs. host, use `host.docker.internal:11434` (not `localhost`) or the connection silently fails.
- **Video (`T3`, interactive `.html` embed):** Hook("ChatGPT's UI, your hardware, your data stays home") → Mechanism(connect Ollama → `docker run` → `localhost:3000`) → Gold(the rendered config + the `OLLAMA_BASE_URL` env) → Gotcha(front-end, not the model — and the `host.docker.internal` trap).

### 6. Checklist
- [ ] demo runs, exits 0, ≥2 `[check]` lines
- [ ] output byte-stable on re-run
- [ ] gold value reproduced, matches reference
- [ ] ≥2 web sources logged with `Verifies:` lines
- [ ] article centers on one annotated diagram + ≥1 gotcha
- [ ] HyperFrames video renders, 6 beats, gold-check badge green

## 3.4 Hardware landscape

> The buying guide. After this, "which computer do I buy to run local LLMs?"
> stops being a spec-sheet firehose and becomes *two numbers* — memory **capacity**
> (will the model + KV cache fit?) and memory **bandwidth** (how fast does decode
> run?) — plus one rule that ranks every box on the market. The split is the direct
> payoff from **1.4** (decode is bandwidth-bound, not compute-bound) and **1.5 /
> 1.7** (the model-weight + KV-cache footprint you must fit). No training math, no
> NVLink/tensor-parallel topology — just the two specs that set a buying decision.
> **Prerequisites:** 1.4, 1.5, 1.7. **Pays off in:** buying decisions, 3.6 (VRAM
> math), 3.8 (GPU offload). **Video:** `T2`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `local-llm/HARDWARE_LANDSCAPE.md` (§0 the two-questions split; §2 the 2026 hardware table; §3 the decode roofline `decode_tps ~= bandwidth / bytes_per_token`; §6 the buying decision tree) · `local-llm/hardware_landscape.py` (Section A = the capacity/bandwidth table; Section B = the bandwidth-bound roofline; Section G = the "will it fit + how fast?" calculator) — re-run it, reproduce the gold (Strix Halo 273/34.13 ≈ 8.00 GB/token), then **extend it** to the four pinned teaching devices + the Llama-3-8B gold (Step 4).
- **Canonical:** NVIDIA **A100** product page (80GB HBM2e, 2,039 GB/s) · NVIDIA **RTX 4090** product page (24GB GDDR6X, 1,008 GB/s) · Apple **M3 Max** tech specs (16-core CPU / 40-core GPU, 400 GB/s, up to 128GB unified) · Baseten, *A guide to LLM inference and performance* (the VRAM-vs-bandwidth framing + the `time/token = bytes / bandwidth` decode equation) · Inference Engineering, *AI Inference Hardware Guide* (the "start with VRAM capacity, then rank by bandwidth" selection framework).
- **Goal:** state, from memory, the *two* hardware specs that dominate LLM running, which question each answers, why decode is bandwidth-bound, and — given "model X at context Y" — which number you check first.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **For LLMs two hardware specs dominate, and they answer two different questions: memory *capacity* (GB — will the model + KV cache fit?) and memory *bandwidth* (GB/s — how fast does decode run?).** Beginners conflate them; the spec sheets list them on separate lines for a reason. `Verifies:` Inference Engineering *AI Inference Hardware Guide* — "start with VRAM capacity (can the model fit?) then rank by memory bandwidth per dollar (which determines decode tokens/second per dollar)" and the numbered framework "1. Memory bandwidth determines tokens/second per chip; 2. VRAM capacity determines the largest model you can serve; 3. FLOP count is nearly irrelevant for single-user latency" (https://inferenceengineering.tech/learn/ai-inference-hardware/) + Baseten *A guide to LLM inference and performance* — lists "GPU Memory" (capacity: "24 GB of VRAM") and "GPU Memory Bandwidth" as two separate specs to read off a datasheet, then computes decode speed from the bandwidth one (https://www.baseten.co/blog/llm-transformer-inference-guide/). *(The local bundle `HARDWARE_LANDSCAPE.md` §0 states the same split: "WILL IT FIT? -> memory CAPACITY (GB)" vs "HOW FAST IS DECODE? -> memory BANDWIDTH (GB/s)".)*
- **Decode is memory-bandwidth-bound, not compute-bound — which is *why* bandwidth (not FLOPS) is the decode-speed spec.** At batch=1 (one user), to emit a single token the engine streams essentially all the active weights out of memory once; the compute units sit idle waiting on data, so tok/s is set by how fast memory can *feed* them. `Verifies:` Baseten — "during the autoregressive part of generation, we are **memory bandwidth bound** if our batch size is 1," with the decode equation `time/token = total number of bytes moved (the model weights) / accelerator memory bandwidth` (https://www.baseten.co/blog/llm-transformer-inference-guide/) + Inference Engineering — "During decode, the GPU re-reads the entire model from memory for every token generated… making bandwidth the binding constraint"; "Rank by bandwidth/$ and VRAM/$ first. Any hardware selection that ranks chips by FLOPS alone is wrong for inference" (https://inferenceengineering.tech/learn/ai-inference-hardware/). *(This is the 1.4 carry: DistServe §2.1 — "each decoding step only processes one new token… the decoding phase… is constrained by the GPU's memory bandwidth," https://arxiv.org/html/2401.09670v3; and the Anyscale roofline framing.)*
- **The decode roofline is one line: `decode_tps ~= bandwidth_GBps / bytes_read_per_token`.** For one *fixed* model the bytes/token is constant, so the decode-speed **ranking across hardware follows the bandwidth ranking exactly** — doubling bandwidth doubles tok/s; doubling FLOPS does ~nothing for decode. (Prefill is the opposite — it is compute-bound, so FLOPS matter there; that asymmetry is 1.4.) `Verifies:` Baseten — the worked `time/token` numbers scale inversely with bandwidth: T4 (300 GB/s) 46 ms/tok, A10 (600) 23 ms, A100 (2039) 6 ms — halve the bandwidth, double the time (https://www.baseten.co/blog/llm-transformer-inference-guide/) + local `HARDWARE_LANDSCAPE.md` §3 + `hardware_landscape.py` Section B — "decode_tps ~= bandwidth_GBps / bytes_read_per_token… Doubling bandwidth doubles tok/s. Doubling FLOPS does ~nothing for decode."
- **NVIDIA GPUs are the throughput/serve default: dedicated VRAM (capacities like 24 / 80 GB) and very high bandwidth HBM (~2 TB/s on the A100) on a mature CUDA stack.** `Verifies:` NVIDIA **A100** product page — "80GB HBM2e" and "GPU Memory Bandwidth, 2,039 GB/s" (SXM4 80GB), "the world's fastest memory bandwidth at over 2 terabytes" (https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/data-center/a100/) + Baseten — uses "A100 SXM 80 GB" at "(2039 GB/s)" directly in its decode-time equation, "6 ms/token" (https://www.baseten.co/blog/llm-transformer-inference-guide/). *(The H100 SXM raises this to 3.35 TB/s over 80GB HBM3 — Inference Engineering, same page. The RTX 4090 consumer card is 24GB GDDR6X at 1,008 GB/s.)*
- **RTX 4090 (consumer CUDA card): 24GB GDDR6X, 1,008 GB/s.** `Verifies:` NVIDIA **RTX 4090** product page — "Standard Memory Config, 24 GB GDDR6X," "Memory Interface Width, 384-bit" (https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/geforce/graphics-cards/40-series/rtx-4090/) + TechPowerUp *GeForce RTX 4090* — "Memory Size: 24 GB… Memory Type: GDDR6X… Bandwidth: 1.01 TB/s" (https://www.techpowerup.com/gpu-specs/geforce-rtx-4090.c3889) + Spheron *RTX 4090 for AI/ML* — "VRAM, 24 GB GDDR6X… Memory Bandwidth, 1,008 GB/s" (https://www.spheron.network/blog/rtx-4090-for-ai-ml/).
- **Apple Silicon uses *unified* memory: the CPU and GPU share one pool, so you get lots of "VRAM" cheaply (up to 128–192 GB on a laptop/desktop chip) but at laptop-tier bandwidth.** The M3 Max (16-core CPU / 40-core GPU) is **400 GB/s**. `Verifies:` Apple *MacBook Pro (M3 Max) Tech Specs* — "M3 Max with 16-core CPU and 40-core GPU (400GB/s memory bandwidth)," configurable to "64GB unified memory" and "128GB unified memory" (https://support.apple.com/en-us/117736) + Apple *newsroom* — "Each chip in the M3 family features a unified memory architecture… This delivers high bandwidth, low latency" (https://www.apple.com/newsroom/2023/10/apple-unveils-m3-m3-pro-and-m3-max-the-most-advanced-chips-for-a-personal-computer/). *(The 14-core M3 Max is 300 GB/s; the 16-core is the full 400 GB/s — the figure this unit pins.)*
- **Unified memory's trade-off: it fits big models cheaply, but decodes them *slower* than HBM.** An A100 (2,039 GB/s) and an M3 Max (400 GB/s) running the *same* model differ by a ~5× bandwidth gap ⇒ a ~5× decode-speed gap — the Mac's capacity advantage and bandwidth disadvantage point in opposite directions. That is *exactly* the two-spec split: capacity decides *what fits*, bandwidth decides *how fast it runs once it fits*. `Verifies:` Inference Engineering — "Memory bandwidth determines tokens/second per chip; VRAM capacity determines the largest model you can serve" (the two axes pull apart; https://inferenceengineering.tech/learn/ai-inference-hardware/) + the bandwidth figures themselves (A100 2,039 GB/s from the NVIDIA page; M3 Max 400 GB/s from Apple) — same task, 5× gap, both verified above. *(The local bundle makes this vivid: a Mac Mini M4 Pro (273 GB/s) "decodes a 70B at the SAME speed as a $4,699 DGX Spark" — identical bandwidth, identical decode, regardless of FLOPS.)*
- **CPU-only is slow because system DRAM has ~tens-of-GB/s bandwidth — an order of magnitude below HBM.** Dual-channel DDR5 is roughly 80–100 GB/s, ~25× less than an A100. `Verifies:` Tom's Hardware forum *How much bandwidth on DDR5 modules?* — "6400 MT/s… with all 4 slots populated = bandwidth up to 102.4 GB/s" (https://forums.tomshardware.com/threads/how-much-bandwidth-on-ddr5-modules.3883762/) + Wikipedia *DDR5 SDRAM* — "8.2 GT/s translates into around 64 GB/s of bandwidth" per channel (https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DDR5_SDRAM). *(This unit pins ~80 GB/s — a representative dual-channel DDR5 figure — and uses it as the "slow" baseline.)*
- **The buying question reduces to one sentence: "What is the biggest model + context I want to run, and how fast do I need it?" → first check *capacity* (does it fit?), then rank the survivors by *bandwidth* (decode speed).** Capacity is a hard gate (it either fits or it doesn't, and there is no partial credit); bandwidth only sets the speed *after* the model fits. `Verifies:` Inference Engineering selection framework — "1. Model size and VRAM requirements… `model_params × bytes_per_param × 1.25`… Fits on [GPU]"; "Rank by bandwidth/$ and VRAM/$ first. Any hardware selection that ranks chips by FLOPS alone is wrong for inference" (https://inferenceengineering.tech/learn/ai-inference-hardware/) + Baseten — first checks whether the model fits in "24 GB of VRAM," *then* ranks the GPUs that fit by `time/token = bytes / bandwidth` (https://www.baseten.co/blog/llm-transformer-inference-guide/). *(The local `HARDWARE_LANDSCAPE.md` §8 calculator and §6 decision tree implement this exact two-step: `fits?` then `est decode`.) `Verifies:` the bundle's own logic.*

> **⚠ Flagged — the pinned teaching numbers are rounded, deliberately.** The A100 SXM4 80GB bandwidth is **2,039 GB/s** on the NVIDIA datasheet (this unit pins it, verified by NVIDIA + Baseten); the bundle's lineage rounds it to "~2 TB/s / ~2000 GB/s." The CPU DDR5 figure of **~80 GB/s** is a representative dual-channel speed (DDR5-5200 ≈ 83 GB/s; DDR5-4800 ≈ 77 GB/s; "up to 102.4 GB/s" at DDR5-6400 with all slots) — a round number, not a single SKU's datasheet line. The *ratios* (≈25× A100-over-CPU, ≈5× A100-over-Mac) are robust to the rounding; the exact ms/token is not the lesson, the **capacity-then-bandwidth ranking** is.

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. For running an LLM, which **two** hardware specs dominate — and what question does each answer? Why is it *not* FLOPS?
2. Why is **decode** memory-bandwidth-bound — exactly what must the device read to emit one token? (tie → 1.4) And which phase is the *opposite* (compute-bound)?
3. NVIDIA GPU (dedicated VRAM + HBM) vs Apple Silicon (unified memory) vs CPU-only — what is the **capacity-vs-bandwidth trade-off** of each, in one line per class?
4. Translate "I want to run model X at context Y" into a hardware requirement: which number do you check **first**, and why? (the capacity gate; tie → 1.5 KV + 1.7 weight footprint)
5. What do you **give up** buying a Mac (lots of cheap unified memory) instead of an NVIDIA GPU (HBM bandwidth)? Why is that not always a bad trade?
6. Given the four pinned devices, why does the A100 **decode faster** than a Mac that holds the exact same model — and why does that *not* mean the Mac is the wrong buy? (capacity gate vs bandwidth speed)

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `hardware_landscape.py` (re-uses the `local-llm/hardware_landscape.py` table + roofline, pinned to four teaching devices + the Llama-3-8B gold). **Determinism:** every device spec, model config, and formula is a pinned constant — **no RNG, no `random`/`Date.now()`/network**; output is byte-stable on re-run. (There is nothing to draw or call — this is a pure arithmetic comparison, so determinism is trivial: no seed is needed because no randomness exists.)
- **The two formulas (carried from 1.5 + 1.7):**
  - `weights_gb = params_b × bpw / 8` — the size term from **1.7** (`bpw` = bits per weight).
  - `kv_gb = 2 × n_layers × n_kv_heads × n_ctx × head_dim × bpe / 1e9` — the running-notebook term from **1.5** (`bpe=2` fp16; the leading `2` is K+V).
  - `footprint_gb = weights_gb + kv_gb` — **this is what must FIT in capacity.**
  - `decode_tps ∝ bandwidth_gbs / footprint_gb` — the **1.4** roofline: decode is bandwidth-bound ⇒ for one fixed model the decode **ranking follows the bandwidth ranking** exactly.
- **The four pinned devices** (capacity `|` bandwidth, each from the vendor spec sheet in Step 2):
  ```python
  DEVICES = {
      "RTX 4090":       {"cap_gb": 24, "bw_gbs": 1008},   # 24GB GDDR6X
      "Mac M3 Max":     {"cap_gb": 64, "bw_gbs":  400},   # 16C/40C, unified, up to 128GB
      "A100 80GB":      {"cap_gb": 80, "bw_gbs": 2039},   # 80GB HBM2e SXM4 (~2 TB/s)
      "CPU + DDR5":     {"cap_gb": 64, "bw_gbs":   80},   # dual-channel system DRAM
  }
  ```
- **The comparison table:** for a pinned `(model, ctx)` footprint, print for each device two columns — `fits?` (`footprint ≤ cap`) and a **relative decode** score (`bw_gbs`, normalized so the slowest = 1.0). Two columns, two questions; that is the whole buying tool.
- **Gold value (must match on re-run):** **Llama-3-8B Q4 @ 8k** (`params = 8,030,261,248`; `n_layers=32, n_kv_heads=8, head_dim=128` from 1.5; `bpw=4.0`; `n_ctx=8192`; `bpe=2`):
  - weights = `8,030,261,248 × 4 / 8` = `4,015,130,624 B` = **4.02 GB**; KV = `2×32×8×8192×128×2` = `1,073,741,824 B` = **1.07 GB**; **footprint = 5,088,872,448 B ≈ 5.09 GB**.
  - **FIT:** all four devices fit (24 / 64 / 80 / 64 GB all ≥ 5.09 GB) — capacity is no constraint at 8B Q4.
  - **Decode ranking (bandwidth-bound):** **A100 (2039) > RTX 4090 (1008) > Mac M3 Max (400) > CPU DDR5 (80)**. Relative to the slowest (CPU = 1.0): **A100 = 25.49, 4090 = 12.60, Mac = 5.00, CPU = 1.00** — the A100 decodes **~25.5×** faster than the CPU and **~5×** faster than the Mac on the *same* model, purely from bandwidth.
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] Llama-3-8B Q4 @8k footprint 5088872448 B (~5.09 GB) fits all 4 pinned devices` · `[check] higher bandwidth => faster decode: ranking A100(2039) > RTX 4090(1008) > Mac M3 Max(400) > CPU DDR5(80)` · `[check] A100:CPU decode ratio == 2039:80 == 25.49 (bandwidth-bound, same model)`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** swap to **Llama-3-70B Q4 @ 8k** (`params ≈ 70B`, `n_layers=80, n_kv_heads=8, head_dim=128`, `bpw=4.0`): weights = 35 GB, KV @8k = `2×80×8×8192×128×2` = `2,684,354,560 B` ≈ 2.68 GB, **footprint ≈ 37.7 GB**. Now the **FIT** gate bites: RTX 4090 (24 GB) → **NO** (must offload, → 3.8); Mac M3 Max / A100 / CPU DDR5 (64/80/64 GB) → yes. The 4090 has the best bandwidth-per-dollar yet *cannot hold* a 70B — **capacity is the hard gate; bandwidth only sets the speed after it fits.** Drop in your own model + context and re-print → seeds **3.6** (VRAM math) and **3.8** (GPU offload).

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *What to actually buy to run LLMs.*
- **Angle:** only two numbers matter — memory (does it fit?) and bandwidth (how fast does it decode?). The whole spec sheet collapses to a 2-column table and one ranking rule. Every "which Mac / which GPU" question is answered by it.
- **Payload:** capacity vs bandwidth (the two questions, the roofline `decode_tps ~= bw / bytes_per_token`) · the NVIDIA-GPU (CUDA + dedicated VRAM + ~2 TB/s HBM) vs Mac (unified memory: lots of cheap "VRAM," ~400 GB/s) vs CPU-only (~80 GB/s DDR, ~25× slower than HBM) trade-offs · the buying decision tree (biggest model + context → check capacity first → rank survivors by bandwidth).
- **Gotcha:** a Mac **fits** a far bigger model than a 24GB GPU — but it **decodes it slower** than an HBM card, because decode speed is governed by **bandwidth, not FLOPS and not capacity**. Buying on "it has lots of memory" or "it has huge FLOPS" alone is the two ways people buy the wrong box.
- **Video (`T2`):** Hook("only two numbers matter — memory (fit) and bandwidth (speed)") → Mechanism(capacity = the fit gate from 1.5/1.7; bandwidth = the decode roofline from 1.4, `decode_tps ~= bw / bytes_per_token`) → Gold(Llama-3-8B Q4 @8k: all 4 fit; decode ranking A100 > 4090 > Mac > CPU, A100 ~25× over CPU) → Gotcha(a Mac fits the model but decodes it ~5× slower than an A100 — bandwidth, not FLOPS, governs decode).

### 6. Checklist
- [ ] demo runs, exits 0, ≥2 `[check]` lines
- [ ] output byte-stable on re-run
- [ ] gold value reproduced, matches reference
- [ ] ≥2 web sources logged with `Verifies:` lines
- [ ] article centers on one annotated diagram + ≥1 gotcha
- [ ] HyperFrames video renders, 6 beats, gold-check badge green
- [ ] all 6 probe questions answered out loud, no notes

## 3.5 Quant types (Q4_K_M)

> The K-quant menu you actually pick from. After this, "which GGUF should I download?"
> stops being a guess and becomes *one column* — **bits per weight (bpw)** — read straight
> off the README, with **Q4_K_M** as the default almost everyone lands on. This is the
> *specific* follow-up to **1.7**: 1.7 said *why* quantization works and the `params × bpw / 8`
> formula; this unit names the real GGUF **K-quant family** you choose between and shows
> why mixed-per-tensor precision (not a uniform ruler) is what makes the default good.
> Don't re-derive `scale = edge/q0` here — 1.7 did. Concept + the size math; *VRAM budgeting*
> (weights + KV + activations together) is 3.6.
> **Prerequisites:** 1.7 (quant concept). **Pays off in:** 3.6 (VRAM math). **Video:** `T2`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `local-llm/QUANT_TYPES.md` (§0 the three-family lineage — **legacy Q4_0 → K-quants → I-quants**; §2 the per-format block mechanics — read the **K-quant super-block layout (§2.D)** and the `_M` mixed-precision note; §3 the full comparison table; §6 the cheat sheet "Default, no thought → Q4_K_M." **Explicitly SKIP** §2.E the I-quant imatrix/codebook math and the per-format dequant formulas in §6 — those are 3.16 / server-side) · `local-llm/quant_types.py` (Sections D–F: the K-quant super-block demo + the struct-verified bpw table + the `_S`/`_M`/`_L` model-level bpw table).
- **Canonical:** llama.cpp **`tools/quantize/README.md`** — the authoritative **Llama-3.1-8B bits-per-weight + file-size + throughput** table (the canonical source the whole community cites) · Kurt 2026, *Which Quantization Should I Use?* (arXiv:2601.14277) — a unified quality / size / perplexity / throughput eval across 3–8-bit K-quant and legacy formats on **Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct**.
- **Goal:** state, from memory, the six K-quant tiers (Q2_K → Q8_0) and their bpw, why `_M` beats a pure/uniform `Q4_K` (mixed precision per tensor — the **1.7 outlier** insight, made concrete), where the quality curve falls off a cliff, and read the size of *any* quant straight off `params × bpw / 8`.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **The K-quant family is a fixed menu of tiers, each with a *bits-per-weight (bpw)* that sets the size.** From Q2_K (smallest) to Q8_0 (largest), every tier packs a 256-weight super-block into a fixed byte budget, so each has a single effective bpw: for **Llama-3.1-8B**, `Q2_K = 3.1593`, `Q3_K_M = 3.9960`, `Q4_K_M = 4.8944`, `Q5_K_M = 5.7036`, `Q6_K = 6.5633`, `Q8_0 = 8.5008`. bpw is the one knob that sets the **weights term** of the size budget — everything else in this unit reads off it. `Verifies:` llama.cpp `tools/quantize/README.md` — the Llama-3.1-8B table lists `bits/weight` for every variant: `Q2_K 3.1593`, `Q3_K_M 3.9960`, `Q4_K_M 4.8944`, `Q5_K_M 5.7036`, `Q6_K 6.5633`, `Q8_0 8.5008` (https://github.com/ggml-org/llama.cpp/blob/master/tools/quantize/README.md) + Kurt 2026, arXiv:2601.14277 §2.2 — "the K-quant family introduces a hierarchical super-block structure… a non-integer effective bits-per-weight (bpw), e.g., ≈3.44 bpw for Q3_K_S and ≈4.5 bpw for Q4_K_M" (https://arxiv.org/html/2601.14277v1). *(The local `QUANT_TYPES.md` §3 carries the same lineage; its older Llama-2-7B bpw table — Q4_K_M 4.84 — is the predecessor of these Llama-3.1 figures.)*
- **bpw maps to size by the 1.7 formula — `size_bytes = params × bpw / 8` — and it reproduces the README's measured file sizes to the rounding.** For an 8B model (`params = 8,030,261,248`): Q4_K_M → `params × 4.8944 / 8 = 4.58 GiB`; Q8_0 → `7.95 GiB`; Q2_K → `2.95 GiB`; F16 (`bpw=16.0005`) → `14.96 GiB`. One column, one formula — a **4.06×** size span from Q2_K to F16. `Verifies:` llama.cpp `tools/quantize/README.md` — the `size (GiB)` column (Q4_K_M 4.58, Q6_K 6.14, Q8_0 7.95, F16 14.96) is exactly `params × bpw / 8 / 2³⁰` with the table's own `bits/weight` column (https://github.com/ggml-org/llama.cpp/blob/master/tools/quantize/README.md) + the 1.7 size formula `size_bytes = params × bits_per_weight / 8`, carried here verbatim (`Verifies:` HF/bitsandbytes *Gentle Introduction to 8-bit* — "To calculate the model size in bytes, one multiplies the number of parameters by the size of the chosen precision in bytes," https://huggingface.co/blog/hf-bitsandbytes-integration). *(Teases 3.6: this `weights_gb = params × bpw / 8` is the dominant term of the full VRAM budget.)*
- **K-quants use *mixed precision per tensor* — and that is exactly why `_M` beats a naive uniform Q4.** A pure `Q4_K` quantizes *every* tensor to the same 4.5 bpw; the `_M` variant is a **layer-mix recipe** that spends more bits on the tensors that drive the output (attention-V → Q6_K; token-embedding + output head → Q6_K / Q8_0) and leaves the bulk MLP at Q4_K. The sensitive tensors are the "outliers" from **1.7**; `_M` keeps them in higher precision exactly as LLM.int8() kept outlier channels in fp16 — just applied per-tensor instead of per-channel. `Verifies:` llama.cpp `tools/quantize/README.md` — the `--pure` flag is documented as "disable k-quant mixtures and quantizes all tensors to the same type" (so `_M` *is* a mixture by default; https://github.com/ggml-org/llama.cpp/blob/master/tools/quantize/README.md) + Kurt 2026 §2.2 — the schemes "leav[e] selected tensors (e.g. layer norms, embeddings) at higher precision" and "some variants also use mixed precision across sub-blocks" (https://arxiv.org/html/2601.14277v1). *(The local `QUANT_TYPES.md` §2.D states it bluntly: "the `_M` suffix means MIXED types across layers, not a single block format… Q4_K_M ≈ 4.84 bpw because the sensitive layers (attention, embedding, output head) use the higher-precision Q6_K, while the bulk MLP layers use Q4_K.")*
- **Why mixed beats uniform — the 1.7 outlier insight, made concrete.** In 1.7 a *single global scale* set by a few large-magnitude outlier weights stretched the ruler so coarse that all the ordinary weights collapsed onto a couple of levels. K-quants fix it on two axes at once: (a) **sub-blocks** — 8 sub-scales per 256-weight super-block so one outlier can't wreck 255 neighbors; (b) **per-tensor mix** — the tensors whose values most affect the final logits get more bits. The `_M` recipe is a budget allocation: it spends the metadata-and-precision budget *where it matters*, not uniformly. That is why Q4_K_M (~4.9 bpw) sits at near-baseline quality while a hypothetical uniform 4-bit-with-one-global-scale would crater. `Verifies:` Kurt 2026 §2.2 — "finer grouping (smaller blocks / more scales) often improves quality at a fixed nominal bit-width," and the super-block "provides additional flexibility in allocating bits and metadata within a tensor" (https://arxiv.org/html/2601.14277v1) + the 1.7 outlier carry (LLM.int8(): outlier features "cause… performance deterioration… extracting all outliers with magnitude 6 or greater… recovers full inference performance," https://huggingface.co/blog/hf-bitsandbytes-integration). *(The mechanism — `weight = (dmin·min6) + (d·scale6)·q` per sub-block with double-quantized scales — is in the bundle §2.D; this unit only needs the *consequence*.)*
- **The practical quality curve: Q8_0 is indistinguishable from FP16, Q5_K_M "transparent," Q4_K_M the sweet spot, Q3_K_M where degradation begins, Q2_K visibly degraded.** On WikiText-2 perplexity for Llama-3.1-8B: F16 `7.32` → Q8_0 `7.33` (effectively identical) → Q6_K `7.35` → Q5_K_M `7.40` → Q4_K_M `7.56` → Q3_K_M `7.96` → Q3_K_S `8.96`. Downstream benchmarks (GSM8K/HellaSwag/MMLU/…) track the same curve: through Q4_K_M the loss is small and noisy (Q4_K_M Avg `69.15` vs F16 `69.47`); it steepens at Q3 (Q3_K_S drops to `65.49`). `Verifies:` Kurt 2026, arXiv:2601.14277 Table 2 — WikiText-2 PPL F16 7.32, Q8_0 7.33, Q6_K 7.35, Q5_K_M 7.40, Q4_K_M 7.56, Q3_K_M 7.96, Q3_K_S 8.96; and the downstream Avg 69.47 → 65.49 across the same sweep (https://arxiv.org/html/2601.14277v1) + llama.cpp `tools/quantize/README.md` — Q8_0 is described as the "reference-ish baseline… very close to FP16," and Q4_K_M is the documented default (https://github.com/ggml-org/llama.cpp/blob/master/tools/quantize/README.md).
- **Q4_K_M is the community + tooling default — the size/quality sweet spot.** It shrinks Llama-3.1-8B from ~32 GB (F32 / ~15 GiB F16) to **~4.9 GB** while keeping benchmark scores within ~0.3 pts of full precision; the official quantize tool names it the default, and almost every pre-quantized model on HuggingFace ships a Q4_K_M build first. `Verifies:` llama.cpp `tools/quantize/README.md` — "naive Q4_K_M quantization using default settings," and the Memory/Disk table "Llama 3.1 8B: Original 32.1 GB → Quantized (Q4_K_M) 4.9 GB" (https://github.com/ggml-org/llama.cpp/blob/master/tools/quantize/README.md) + Kurt 2026 §4.5 + Table 2 — Q4_K_M sits on the accuracy–compression frontier at near-baseline quality (PPL 7.56, Avg 69.15 vs F16 69.47), the practical default (https://arxiv.org/html/2601.14277v1). *(Nuance, kept honest: Kurt's Pareto analysis found Q4_K_S / Q5_0 marginally better on *their specific* benchmark mix; the broader tooling/community default remains Q4_K_M, which is what this unit pins.)*
- **The "cliff": below ~Q3 the quality curve steepens hard, and Q2_K is visibly degraded.** The Kurt eval's most aggressive point — Q3_K_S (3.64 bpw) — already costs +1.64 perplexity and the largest benchmark drop; Q2_K (3.16 bpw) sits *below* that floor and is where users report肉眼可见/visible degradation. `Verifies:` Kurt 2026 §4.1 — "the most aggressive 3-bit configurations deliver the largest space savings, but they also introduce the largest average degradation… Q3_K_S… yields the largest drop… and the largest perplexity increase" (PPL 8.96 vs 7.32; https://arxiv.org/html/2601.14277v1) + llama.cpp `tools/quantize/README.md` — Q2_K is the lowest-bpw entry in the table at `bits/weight 3.1593` / `2.95 GiB` (https://github.com/ggml-org/llama.cpp/blob/master/tools/quantize/README.md). *(Flagged: the Kurt study's floor was Q3_K_S; it did **not** measure Q2_K on Llama-3.1-8B. The long-standing practitioner figure for Q2_K is a ~+3.5 perplexity increase, recorded in the local `QUANT_TYPES.md` §3 ("Q2_K… often degraded") from the llama.cpp quantize notes. The exact Q2_K-on-Llama-3.1 perplexity is **not** a verified single-source number — the principle that the curve steepens below Q3 is.)*
- **The `_S` / `_M` / `_L` suffixes trade size within a tier — same nominal bits, different mix, different bpw.** Within the 3-bit tier: `Q3_K_S 3.6429 < Q3_K_M 3.9960 < Q3_K_L 4.2979` (bpw, hence size, rises S→M→L as more layers get the higher-precision treatment). Same pattern for 4-bit (`Q4_K_S 4.6672 < Q4_K_M 4.8944`) and 5-bit (`Q5_K_S 5.5704 < Q5_K_M 5.7036`). `_S` prioritizes the smallest file / fastest decode; `_L` pushes quality up at a size cost; `_M` is the balanced default. `Verifies:` llama.cpp `tools/quantize/README.md` — the Llama-3.1-8B `bits/weight` column shows the S<M<L ordering for Q3_K_* and the S<M ordering for Q4_K_* / Q5_K_* verbatim (https://github.com/ggml-org/llama.cpp/blob/master/tools/quantize/README.md) + Kurt 2026 §2.2 — "the suffixes `_S`, `_M`, and `_L` denote small/medium/large variants that trade off compression and fidelity" (https://arxiv.org/html/2601.14277v1).

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. Name the six K-quant tiers from Q2_K to Q8_0 and (roughly) their bits-per-weight — which is ~8.5 and which is ~3.2?
2. Write the size formula from memory. For an 8B model, what are the file sizes of **Q4_K_M, Q8_0, and F16** — and what's the span?
3. Why does `_M` (a layer-mix recipe) beat a pure/uniform `Q4_K` at only a small size cost — and how is this the **outlier insight from 1.7** made concrete (which tensors get the extra bits, and why)?
4. Why is **Q4_K_M** the community default — what trade does it nail, and what does the quality curve (PPL 7.56 vs F16's 7.32) tell you?
5. Where does the quality curve fall off a cliff, and how does perplexity betray it (Q3_K_S → 8.96, and Q2_K below it)?
6. What do **`_S` / `_M` / `_L`** trade *within one tier* — name the Q3_K trio's bpw in order.
7. Q8_0 is ~8.5 bpw, not 8 — where do the extra ~0.5 bits/weight go, and why is it "near-lossless" rather than "lossless"? (tie → the per-block fp16 scale; → 1.7)

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `quant_types.py` (re-uses the `local-llm/quant_types.py` K-quant table, re-pinned to the canonical **Llama-3.1-8B** figures). **Determinism:** every bpw, param count, and formula is a pinned constant — **no RNG, no `random`/`Date.now()`/network**; output is byte-stable on re-run. (Pure arithmetic — a size/quality table — so no seed is needed because no randomness exists.)
- **The one formula (carried from 1.7):** `size_GiB = params × bpw / 8 / 2³⁰` (divide by `2³⁰ = 1,073,741,824` for GiB). For bytes, stop at `/ 8`.
- **The pinned model + bpw table** (bpw from the canonical README, Llama-3.1-8B):
  ```python
  PARAMS = 8_030_261_248          # Llama-3-8B (the curriculum's pinned 8B reference)
  QUANTS = [                      # (type, bpw, ppl, tier)  — bpw from tools/quantize/README.md
      ("F16",    16.0005, 7.32, "baseline"),
      ("Q8_0",    8.5008, 7.33, "indistinguishable from FP16"),
      ("Q6_K",    6.5633, 7.35, "near-lossless"),
      ("Q5_K_M",  5.7036, 7.40, "transparent"),
      ("Q4_K_M",  4.8944, 7.56, "sweet spot (default)"),
      ("Q3_K_M",  3.9960, 7.96, "degradation begins"),
      ("Q2_K",    3.1593, None, "visibly degraded*"),   # *not in Kurt eval; floor was Q3_K_S
  ]
  ```
- **The table:** for each row compute `size_GiB = PARAMS × bpw / 8 / 2**30` and print `type | bpw | size_GiB | ppl | tier`. Add a `_S/_M/_L` sub-table for the 3-bit tier (`Q3_K_S 3.6429 / Q3_K_M 3.9960 / Q3_K_L 4.2979`) to show size rising within a tier.
- **Gold value (must match on re-run):** the **size column** — `Q2_K 2.95`, `Q3_K_M 3.74`, `Q4_K_M 4.58`, `Q5_K_M 5.33`, `Q6_K 6.14`, `Q8_0 7.95`, `F16 14.96` (all GiB). These are *exactly* the README's measured `size (GiB)` column — the cross-validation that the formula + bpw are right. Headline spans: **Q4_K_M / F16 ≈ 0.306×** (a 3.27× shrink), **Q2_K → Q8_0 ≈ 2.69×**.
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] size == params*bpw/8 for every row (Q4_K_M: 8030261248*4.8944/8/2**30 == 4.58 GiB)` · `[check] ordering by bpw correct: Q2_K(3.16) < Q3_K_M(4.00) < Q4_K_M(4.89) < Q5_K_M(5.70) < Q6_K(6.56) < Q8_0(8.50)` · `[check] Q4_K_M (4.58 GiB) < Q8_0 (7.95 GiB) < F16 (14.96 GiB)` · `[check] computed GiB == README measured size (Q4_K_M 4.58, Q8_0 7.95, F16 14.96)`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** (a) add the **GPU-offload gate** preview — pick a pinned device's VRAM (e.g. RTX 4090 = 24 GB, M3 Max = 64 GB from 3.4) and print which tiers **fit** in weights alone (`size_GiB ≤ vram_gb`); on a 24 GB card every tier fits, on a 16 GB card F16 (14.96 GiB) barely squeaks by while Q4_K_M leaves 11+ GB for the KV cache (→ 3.6). (b) Swap in **Llama-3-70B** (`params ≈ 70B`): Q4_K_M ≈ `70e9 × 4.8944/8/2³⁰ ≈ 40 GiB` — now F16 (~136 GiB) and even Q8_0 (~70 GiB) don't fit consumer hardware at all, and Q4_K_M is the *only* sane default → seeds **3.6** (VRAM math) and **3.8** (GPU offload).

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *Q4 vs Q8: what you actually lose (the K-quant menu).*
- **Angle:** picking a GGUF quant is choosing a **bpw off a menu**, not sliding a dial — and **Q4_K_M is where almost everyone lands**. One column (bpw), one formula (`params × bpw / 8`), one curve. The whole "which quant should I download?" question collapses to a 6-row table.
- **Payload:** the K-quant family + its bpw (Q2_K→Q8_0) · why `_M` (mixed precision per tensor) beats uniform Q4 — the **1.7 outlier** insight made concrete (sensitive tensors → Q6_K; bulk MLP → Q4_K) · the practical curve (Q8_0 indistinguishable → Q5_K_M transparent → **Q4_K_M sweet spot** → Q3_K_M degradation begins → Q2_K visibly degraded) · `_S`/`_M`/`_L` trade size within a tier · the size table you read off `params × bpw / 8`.
- **Gotcha:** "transparent" / "near-lossless" quality claims are **task-dependent**, not guarantees — Kurt's perplexity numbers are an *average* over benchmarks, and multi-step reasoning (GSM8K) is the first thing to degrade at low bpw. **Always eval on *your* task** (tie → 2.14): a quant that's "fine" on HellaSwag can quietly break your agent's tool-call JSON or your code-gen. (Second gotcha: Q2_K is below the cliff — only reach for it when VRAM is the overriding constraint, and expect visible degradation.)
- **Video (`T2`):** Hook("Q4_K_M is the default almost everyone lands on") → Mechanism(the K-quant tiers + bpw; mixed precision per tensor = the 1.7 outlier fix made concrete) → Gold(the size table for 8B: Q2_K 2.95 / Q4_K_M 4.58 / Q8_0 7.95 / F16 14.96 GiB) → Gotcha("transparent" is task-dependent — eval on *your* task, → 2.14; Q2_K is below the cliff).

- [ ] output byte-stable on re-run
- [ ] gold value reproduced, matches reference
- [ ] ≥2 web sources logged with `Verifies:` lines
- [ ] article centers on one annotated diagram + ≥1 gotcha
