## 3.6 VRAM math

> Make it fit. After this, "will this model run on my GPU?" stops being a guess
> and becomes *one sum of three terms* — `WEIGHTS + KV_CACHE + OVERHEAD` — every
> one of which you can compute from a `config.json` and a quant type. The payoff is
> the **fit decision**: a number, compared to your card's VRAM, with a yes/no. The
> hook is that the famous rule of thumb (`params × bpw / 8`) is *just the weights
> term* — it silently ignores the KV cache, which is why a "3.5 GB Q4 7B" balloons
> past 8 GB once you actually decode 32k tokens. This is the keystone where **1.5**
> (the KV cache), **1.7** (quant), and **3.5** (quant types) all converge into one
> budget you carry for every local-LLM decision after it.
> **Prerequisites:** 1.5 (KV cache), 1.7 (quant), 3.5 (quant types). **Pays off in:**
> every fit decision — 3.8 (GPU offload), 3.9 (context extension), 3.10 (KV-cache
> quant), 3.13 (paged attention). **Video:** `T1` ⭐.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `local-llm/VRAM_ESTIMATOR.md` (§0 the three-term TL;DR; §1 the rule-of-thumb → exact lineage; §2 the term-by-term mechanism; §4 the fit decision matrix) · `local-llm/vram_estimator.py` (Sections A–E + the GOLD block — this is the **single source of truth**: every number in this unit is printed by it, including the **5.55 GB** gold) — re-run it, reproduce the gold, then **extend it** to your own device (Step 4).
- **Sibling bundles you build on:** `llm/KV_CACHE.md` + `llm/kv_cache.py` (the KV term *is* that cache, sized for one concrete model+context — the `[B,H_kv,S,D]` layout and the all-linear byte formula from **1.5**) · `local-llm/QUANT_TYPES.md` (the `bpw` that sets the weights term from **3.5**: Q4_K_M = 4.5, Q8_0 = 8.5, FP16 = 16.0).
- **Canonical:** vLLM-Omni, *GPU Memory Calculation and Configuration* (the weights + KV-cache + activation-memory + system-overhead decomposition, the official serving engine's own memory math) · EleutherAI, *Transformer Math* (the ≤20% runtime-overhead rule of thumb) · Lyceum Technologies, *KV Cache Memory Calculation for LLMs* (the MHA-vs-GQA formula and the linear context growth) · HuggingFace model `config.json`s (the authoritative `num_hidden_layers` / `num_key_value_heads` / `hidden_size` per head) · the independent **apxml** *VRAM & Performance Calculator* (a reputable online estimator that splits weights / KV cache / overhead).
- **Goal:** state, from memory, the three terms, the one-line formula for each, *which* prior concept sets each term (1.7/3.5 set weights; 1.5 sets KV; the engine sets overhead), why the rule of thumb fails silently, and — given `(model, quant, ctx, device)` — produce the fit decision end to end.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **VRAM is a sum of exactly three terms: WEIGHTS + KV_CACHE + OVERHEAD.** The model's resident tensors (the `.gguf`), plus the running key/value notebook from 1.5, plus everything else (activations, the compute workspace, the CUDA/Metal context, OS/display). A serving engine accounts for all three before it can even schedule a request. `Verifies:` vLLM-Omni *GPU Memory Calculation* — "The total memory used by a stage includes: 1. **Model Weights**: The size of the model parameters loaded on the GPU; 2. **KV Cache**: Memory for storing key-value cache during generation; 3. **Activation Memory**: Temporary memory for intermediate computations; 4. **System Overhead**: Memory used by CUDA, PyTorch, and other system components" (https://docs.vllm.ai/projects/vllm-omni/en/latest/configuration/gpu_memory_utilization/) + TechTactician, *LLMs & Their Size In VRAM Explained* — "The final size of the model in VRAM will mainly depend on three things: the model's size in parameters (8B, 12B, 30B), its context window data, and runtime KV cache values" (https://techtactician.com/llm-gpu-vram-requirements-explained/). *(The local `VRAM_ESTIMATOR.md` §0 states the same: `VRAM = WEIGHTS + KV_CACHE + OVERHEAD`.)*
- **The weights term is one line: `params × bits_per_weight / 8`** (decimal GB) — and it is set *entirely* by the quant type (→ 1.7 / 3.5). `bpw` is the single biggest lever on this term (and on the whole budget at short context). `Verifies:` vLLM-Omni *GPU Memory Calculation* — "model_memory ≈ num_parameters × dtype_size. For example: 7B parameters in FP16: ~14GB; 7B parameters in INT8: ~7GB" (https://docs.vllm.ai/projects/vllm-omni/en/latest/configuration/gpu_memory_utilization/) + TechTactician — "a 7B model that required ~14 GB in FP16 can shrink to about ~7 GB at 8-bit or ~3.5 GB at 4-bit with proper quantization" (https://techtactician.com/llm-gpu-vram-requirements-explained/). *(Carried from 1.7: HF/bitsandbytes — "Quantizing a model in 4-bit reduces your memory-usage by 4x," https://huggingface.co/docs/transformers/main/en/quantization/bitsandbytes.)*
- **The KV term is `2 × n_layers × n_ctx × n_kv_heads × head_dim × bytes_per_element`** (→ 1.5). Factor `2` = the K tensor + the V tensor (both stored, every layer); `n_kv_heads` is the **GQA** head count, *not* `n_heads`; `bytes_per_element` is the cache precision (2 for FP16, 1 for Q8). The term is **strictly linear in `n_ctx`** — no squares, so doubling the context doubles the cache. `Verifies:` vLLM-Omni *GPU Memory Calculation* — "kv_cache_memory ≈ batch_size × seq_len × hidden_size × num_layers × 2 × dtype_size… 2 for k & v" (https://docs.vllm.ai/projects/vllm-omni/en/latest/configuration/gpu_memory_utilization/) + Lyceum, *KV Cache Memory Calculation* — the GQA formula "2 * layers * kv_heads * head_dim * seq_len * batch_size * bytes_per_element… Notice that we use 'kv_heads' instead of the total number of attention heads" (https://lyceum.technology/magazine/kv-cache-memory-calculation-llm/). *(Carried from 1.5: Brenndoerfer — "KV Cache Memory = 2 × L × B × T × H_kv × D_h × bytes… every term in this product is linear: there are no squares," https://mbrenndoerfer.com/writing/kv-cache-memory-calculation-llm-inference-gpu.)*
- **The cache grows linearly with context — that is the term that blows up.** Attention *compute* is `O(n²)` (the score matrix from 1.2), but the cache is `O(n)`: exactly one K,V per token. `Verifies:` Lyceum — "As you increase the context window from 4k to 32k tokens, the memory required for the KV cache increases by 8x" (https://lyceum.technology/magazine/kv-cache-memory-calculation-llm/) + Brenndoerfer (1.5) — "Sequence length has a linear effect on KV cache size… cache memory grows strictly linearly," Llama-3-8B table 4K→0.5 GB, 32K→4 GB (https://mbrenndoerfer.com/writing/kv-cache-memory-calculation-llm-inference-gpu). *(Reproduced in `vram_estimator.py` Section B: context x8 (4k→32k) ⇒ KV x8.0.)*
- **The overhead term (activations + workspace + engine context) is a rule of thumb: `max(0.5 GB, ~10% × weights)`.** It is the least-deterministic term — activations and scratch buffers scale with the matmul sizes, the CUDA/Metal launch context + OS/display reservation is a near-fixed floor. `Verifies:` vLLM-Omni *GPU Memory Calculation* — "Activation Memory is typically smaller but varies with: Batch size, Sequence length, Model architecture. It's usually **10-30% of model weight memory** during inference" (https://docs.vllm.ai/projects/vllm-omni/en/latest/configuration/gpu_memory_utilization/) + EleutherAI *Transformer Math* (quoted verbatim in TechTactician) — "In addition to the memory needed to store the model weights, there is also a small amount of additional overhead during the actual forward pass. In our experience **this overhead is ≤ 20%** and is typically irrelevant to determining the largest model that will fit on your GPU" (https://blog.eleuther.ai/transformer-math/#memory-requirements, via https://techtactician.com/llm-gpu-vram-requirements-explained/). *(The local bundle models it as `max(0.5, 0.10 × weights)` — a conservative point inside the verified 10–30% band, with a 0.5 GB floor for the engine context.)*
- **The rule of thumb (`params × bpw / 8`) is JUST the weights term — it silently ignores the KV cache.** It works for a 5-second guess at short context (where weights ≈ 85% of the budget) but is "close enough" only because the cache is small there; the moment you lengthen the context the ignored term balloons. `Verifies:` TechTactician — "The rule of thumb for smaller context models is: take the model size, and add about 20% overhead. **That however, is just half the story.**" and the misconception quote from Maximilian Schwarzmüller — "A common misconception might be that if a model is, say, 7 billion parameters, it 'only' needs 7GB of VRAM. However, besides the parameters themselves, VRAM also needs to hold the input data (prompt, context), intermediate calculations (activations), and the output" (https://techtactician.com/llm-gpu-vram-requirements-explained/) + Lyceum — "LLM weights are only half the story. As sequence lengths grow… the Key-Value (KV) cache often becomes the primary consumer of GPU VRAM, leading to the dreaded Out-of-Memory (OOM) errors" (https://lyceum.technology/magazine/kv-cache-memory-calculation-llm/). *(Concretely, from `vram_estimator.py`: a "3.5 GB Q4 7B" is 3.5 GB of weights; at 32k FP16 KV its real footprint is far larger — the rule of thumb under-counts by the entire cache term.)*
- **At long context the KV cache can exceed the weights — "it fit yesterday" breaks.** The cache is unbounded in `n_ctx`; the weights are fixed. So there is a cross-over where the notebook out-weighs the model, and raising the context past it flips a fit into an OOM with no change to the model. `Verifies:` Lyceum — "the KV cache will eventually dwarf the model weights themselves. For example, a 1-million token KV cache for a small 7B model would require over 100 GB of VRAM, far exceeding the capacity of a single GPU" (https://lyceum.technology/magazine/kv-cache-memory-calculation-llm/) + Brenndoerfer (1.5) — "crossover point… where KV cache exceeds model weights"; for a 7B-class model "beyond this threshold the cache becomes the primary memory consumer" (https://mbrenndoerfer.com/writing/kv-cache-memory-calculation-llm-inference-gpu). *(The practical rule from Lyx, quoted in TechTactician: "You can run big models or long prompts on a consumer GPU (8–16 GB VRAM) — but rarely both," https://techtactician.com/llm-gpu-vram-requirements-explained/.)*
- **GQA is the multiplier that decides how fast the cache grows — and the #1 estimation bug is using `n_heads` instead of `n_kv_heads`.** Grouped-query attention shares one KV head across a group of query heads, so the cache scales with the *small* `n_kv_heads` count (Llama-3-8B: 32 query heads but only **8 KV heads** ⇒ a 4× cut; Llama-3-70B: 64 query heads, 8 KV heads ⇒ 8×). Plugging `n_heads` instead over-counts the cache 4–8× and makes you think a GQA model won't fit when it will. `Verifies:` Lyceum — "if a model has 32 Query heads and 8 KV heads (a group size of 4), the KV cache is reduced by a factor of 4 compared to MHA" (https://lyceum.technology/magazine/kv-cache-memory-calculation-llm/) + Brenndoerfer (1.5) — "Reduction Factor = H / H_kv… a model with 64 query heads and 8 KV heads achieves an 8-fold reduction in cache size" (https://mbrenndoerfer.com/writing/kv-cache-memory-calculation-llm-inference-gpu). *(Reproduced in `vram_estimator.py` Section B: a 13B plain-MHA model caches **more** than a 70B GQA model at the same context — that is why every modern model adopted GQA.)*
- **The fit decision is one inequality: `total ≤ usable_VRAM`.** `total = weights + kv + overhead`; usable VRAM is ~90% of nominal (consumer cards lose 0.5–1 GB to the OS/display + the CUDA/Metal context). When it doesn't fit, the escape hatches are the three terms themselves: shrink the **weights** (smaller quant, → 3.5), shrink the **KV** (Q8 KV-cache quant halving the term, → 3.10; or shorter context), or spill **weights** layers to CPU RAM via GPU offload (→ 3.8). `Verifies:` vLLM-Omni *GPU Memory Calculation* — the engine computes `free_memory ≥ requested_memory` where requested memory is "total_gpu_memory × gpu_memory_utilization," and on OOM recommends "Reduce `gpu_memory_utilization`… Enable quantization if supported" (https://docs.vllm.ai/projects/vllm-omni/en/latest/configuration/gpu_memory_utilization/) + TechTactician — on VRAM shortage "you can offload some or all of the model data to your main system RAM… albeit with much slower performance" (https://techtactician.com/llm-gpu-vram-requirements-explained/). *(The local `VRAM_ESTIMATOR.md` §4 decision matrix + decision tree implement this exact check; the bundle's gold: Llama-3-8B Q4_K_M @4K = 5.55 GB **fits** an 8 GB card, but @32K = 9.31 GB does **not**.)*

> **⚠ Flagged — the overhead term is a rule of thumb, not a constant.** The bundle models overhead as `max(0.5, 0.10 × weights)` GB — a conservative point inside the verified 10–30% band (vLLM-Omni) / ≤20% (EleutherAI). The exact figure is engine- and workload-dependent (batch size, context, flash-attention tiling, CUDA-graph capture). The lesson is the **three-term decomposition and the fit inequality**, not the third decimal of overhead: if you OOM right at the edge, this term is the first place to add slack (or quantize the KV cache). The ~90%-usable-of-nominal VRAM figure is a widely-used practitioner margin (consumer cards reserve VRAM for the display + driver), not a datasheet line.

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. Name the **three terms** of the VRAM budget. What is the one-line formula for each, and *which prior concept* sets each (1.7/3.5 → ?; 1.5 → ?; the engine → ?)?
2. Write the weights term from memory. Why is the quant type (bpw) the single biggest lever on it, and how much smaller is a 4-bit model than an FP16 one?
3. Write the KV-cache term from memory — including the leading `2`, and why you use `n_kv_heads` **not** `n_heads`. Why is it *linear* in context while attention compute is quadratic?
4. Why does the rule of thumb (`params × bpw / 8`) **fail silently** — which term does it ignore, and at what kind of workload does the ignored term finally bite you?
5. Which term **dominates** at short context vs long context? Roughly where does the KV cache cross over the weights for a quantized model — and what does that mean for "it fit yesterday"?
6. End to end: given `(Llama-3-8B, Q4_K_M, 4096 ctx, FP16 KV)` and an 8 GB card, compute the total and the **fit decision**. Then recompute at 32k — what flips, and why?
7. When a model *doesn't* fit, name the three escape hatches and which **term** each attacks. (tease → 3.5 / 3.10 / 3.8)

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `vram_math.py` (reproduces the `local-llm/vram_estimator.py` GOLD, then extends it to the fit decision + your own device). **Determinism:** every config, quant, context, and GPU is a pinned constant; the formulas are pure arithmetic — **no RNG, no `random`/`Date.now()`/network**; output is byte-stable on re-run. (Nothing to draw: this is a pure budget calculation, so no seed is needed because no randomness exists.)
- **The three formulas (built from 1.5 + 1.7 + 3.5):**
  - `weights_gb = params_b × bpw / 8` — the quant-knob term from **1.7/3.5** (`bpw` = bits per weight; `bpw`: Q4_K_M = 4.5, Q8_0 = 8.5, FP16 = 16.0).
  - `kv_gb = 2 × n_layers × n_ctx × n_kv_heads × head_dim × bpe / 1e9` — the running-notebook term from **1.5** (`bpe=2` FP16; the leading `2` is K+V; `n_kv_heads` is the GQA count, *not* `n_heads`).
  - `overhead_gb = max(0.5, 0.10 × weights_gb)` — activations + workspace + engine context.
  - `total = weights + kv + overhead`; `fits = total ≤ 0.90 × nominal_VRAM`.
- **Model configs (verified against each `config.json`):** Llama-3-8B `{params=8.03, n_layers=32, n_kv_heads=8, head_dim=128}` (GQA 4×) · Qwen2.5-7B `{7.62, 28, 4, 128}` (GQA 7×) · Llama-2-13B `{13.02, 40, 40, 128}` (plain MHA, the contrast).
- **Gold value (must match on re-run) — the canonical number `VRAM_ESTIMATOR.html` reproduces:**
  - **Llama-3-8B, Q4_K_M (4.5 bpw), 4096 ctx, FP16 KV:**
    - `weights = 8.03 × 4.5 / 8` = **4.516875 GB** (~4.52)
    - `kv = 32 × 2 × 4096 × 8 × 128 × 2 / 1e9` = **0.536871 GB** (~0.54)
    - `overhead = max(0.5, 0.10 × 4.516875)` = **0.500000 GB**
    - **TOTAL = 5.553746 GB ≈ 5.55 GB → FITS an 8 GB card.**
  - **Balloon at 32k (same model + quant):** `kv = 32 × 2 × 32768 × 8 × 128 × 2 / 1e9` = **4.294967 GB**; **TOTAL = 9.311842 GB ≈ 9.31 GB → does NOT fit 8 GB** (the rule-of-thumb `4.52 GB` under-counts it by **4.79 GB**, the entire KV term it ignored).
- Print the three terms side by side at `4K` vs `32K` so the **dominance flip** is visible: at 4K weights dominate (4.52 vs 0.54 GB); at 32K the cache nearly matches the weights (4.52 vs 4.29 GB). Print the cross-over context where `kv == weights` for the Q4 model (`≈ 34,461 tokens` — at Q4 the weights are already shrunk, so the un-quantizable cache catches up sooner than the fp16 figure from 1.5).
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] total == weights + kv + overhead (5.55 == 4.52 + 0.54 + 0.50)` · `[check] rule-of-thumb (4.52 GB) underestimates total at 32k by 4.79 GB (the ignored KV term)` · `[check] Llama-3-8B Q4_K_M @4K 5.55 GB FITS 8GB; @32K 9.31 GB does NOT fit 8GB` · `[check] KV linear in ctx: 32K/4K == 8.0x (0.5369 -> 4.2950 GB)`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** plug **YOUR actual device** — read its memory (e.g. your Mac's unified memory / your GPU's VRAM) and a model you really run, then print the full budget and the fit decision. E.g. on a **Mac M3 Max (64 GB unified)** running **Qwen2.5-7B Q4_K_M @8k FP16 KV**: weights = 7.62 × 4.5 / 8 = 4.29 GB; KV = 2 × 28 × 8192 × 4 × 128 × 2 / 1e9 = 0.47 GB; overhead = 0.50 GB; **total ≈ 5.26 GB** — fits with enormous headroom (and decode speed is a *bandwidth* question, → 3.4, not a fit question). Then push the same model to **128k**: KV = 2 × 28 × 131072 × 4 × 128 × 2 / 1e9 = 7.54 GB — the cache grows from 0.47 → 7.54 GB (×16), and watch the term that was negligible at 8k become the majority. Swap in your real numbers and re-print → seeds **3.8** (GPU offload, when it still doesn't fit) and **3.10** (KV-cache quant, the cheapest way to halve the cache term).

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *How much VRAM does an LLM really need? (the exact budget).*
- **Angle:** one sum of three terms — `WEIGHTS + KV_CACHE + OVERHEAD` — every term traced to a prior concept (1.7/3.5 → weights, 1.5 → KV, engine → overhead). The hook is that the rule of thumb is *just* the weights term and silently ignores the cache that grows with context — so the same "3.5 GB Q4 7B" balloons past 8 GB once you actually decode long.
- **Payload:** the 3-term formula · the rule-of-thumb failure (weights-only; ignores the linear-in-context cache) · the dominance flip at short vs long context (weights rule at 4K, cache rivals weights at 32K) · the end-to-end fit decision (`total ≤ usable VRAM`) and its three escape hatches.
- **Gotcha:** long context can make the **KV cache exceed the weights** — the budget that fit yesterday OOMs today at higher context, with no change to the model. "It fit" is *never* a property of the model alone; it is `(model, quant, context)`, and the cache term is the one that moves.
- **Video (`T1`, 6 beats):** Hook("the 5.55 GB gold — and why the '3.5 GB' rule of thumb is a lie") → Analogy(a 3-term budget: the furniture you own + the notebooks you fill + the desk workspace) → Mechanism(a bar chart of the three terms, the KV bar growing with context until it overtakes the weights) → Gold(the fit decision: Llama-3-8B Q4_K_M @4K = 5.55 GB fits an 8 GB card) → Gotcha(@32K the cache balloons to 4.29 GB, total 9.31 GB — the same model no longer fits) → Recap.

### 6. Checklist
- [ ] demo runs, exits 0, ≥2 `[check]` lines
- [ ] output byte-stable on re-run
- [ ] gold value reproduced, matches reference
- [ ] ≥2 web sources logged with `Verifies:` lines
- [ ] article centers on one annotated diagram + ≥1 gotcha
- [ ] HyperFrames video renders, 6 beats, gold-check badge green
- [ ] all 6 probe questions answered out loud, no notes

## 3.7 GGUF format

> The model file, unpacked. After this, a `.gguf` stops being a black-box
> download and becomes *one self-describing binary* — metadata + tokenizer +
> quantized tensors in a single file — and you know exactly which keys a loader
> reads first and why the quant type is baked in. Concept only; *the byte-level
> parsing internals* are the `local-llm/GGUF_FORMAT.md` companion.
> **Prerequisites:** 3.5 (quant types). **Pays off in:** understanding model files.
> **Video:** `T3`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `local-llm/GGUF_FORMAT.md` (§0 the four-section layout — header / KV metadata / tensor info / tensor data; §1 the GGML→GGUF lineage; §2 the mechanism; the KV-key table in §3) — **read §0 + §1 + the KV-key table only; SKIP the hex-dump / alignment-math detail in §2, that's the companion demo's job.**
- **Canonical:** the GGUF spec — `ggml/docs/gguf.md` in `ggerganov/ggml` (the prose specification of the layout, the `general.alignment` rule, the `general.architecture`/`general.file_type` KV keys) · the HuggingFace GGUF docs (huggingface.co/docs/hub/en/gguf — GGUF "encodes both the tensors and a standardized set of metadata," the quant-type → bpw table) · `gguf-py/gguf/gguf.py` in `llama.cpp` (the authoritative reader/writer: `GGUF_MAGIC`, `GGUF_VERSION`, the `GGUFValueType` enum).
- **Goal:** state, from memory, what the three things a `.gguf` bundles are, why one self-describing file replaced the old GGML format, which metadata keys a loader reads to know *what* the model is, and why the quant type is baked into the file.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **GGUF (GPT-Generated Unified Format) is a single-file binary that bundles a whole model — metadata + tokenizer + quantized tensors — so one download is fully self-describing.** Where safetensors stores tensors only, a `.gguf` carries the architecture name, the hyperparameters, the vocab, and the chat template alongside the weights, so the loader needs no companion files. `Verifies:` GGUF spec — "Single-file deployment: they can be easily distributed and loaded, and do not require any external files for additional information… Full information: all information needed to load a model is contained in the model file, and no additional information needs to be provided by the user" (https://github.com/ggml-org/ggml/blob/master/docs/gguf.md) + HuggingFace GGUF docs — "unlike tensor-only file formats like safetensors… GGUF encodes both the tensors and a standardized set of metadata" (https://huggingface.co/docs/hub/en/gguf).
- **The "GPT-Generated Unified Format" expansion is the widely-used community name.** `Verifies:` Vicki Boykis, *GGUF, the long way around* — "GGUF (GPT-Generated Unified Format) is the file format used to serve models on Llama.cpp and other local runners like Llamafile, Ollama" (http://vickiboykis.com/test_blog/2024/02/26/gguf-the-long-way-around/) + apxml.com, *LLM GGUF Guide* — "GGUF (GPT-Generated Unified Format) is a big step in this direction, especially for running LLMs on your own computer" (https://apxml.com/posts/gguf-explained-llm-file-format). *(Flagged: the canonical GGUF spec itself does not expand the acronym prominently — the full words are a practitioner convention attested across many independent guides, not a single canonical citation. The *format* is fully specified; only the name's expansion is community-sourced.)*
- **It replaced the older GGML format — the win is extensibility: a typed key-value metadata section, so new architectures need no loader code change.** The old flat GGML format stored hyperparameters as an untyped list hardcoded in the loader, so every new model (Llama, Mistral, Qwen…) meant editing C code and bumping a *breaking* format version. GGUF's typed KV section lets one file declare its own architecture name, dims, and tokenizer — backward compatible across versions. `Verifies:` GGUF spec — "It is a successor file format to GGML, GGMF and GGJT… The key difference between GGJT and GGUF is the use of a key-value structure for the hyperparameters (now referred to as metadata), rather than a list of untyped values. This allows for new metadata to be added without breaking compatibility with existing models" (https://github.com/ggml-org/ggml/blob/master/docs/gguf.md) + local `local-llm/GGUF_FORMAT.md` §1 (the GGML→GGUF lineage: "push model knowledge out of C code and into the file… every new model architecture meant editing the loader and bumping a breaking format version").
- **`general.architecture` is the required key that names the model family and selects the loader path.** That single string (`llama`, `qwen2`, `mistral`, …) tells the loader which `<arch>.context_length` / `<arch>.embedding_length` / `tokenizer.ggml.*` keys to expect — one file self-describes "I am a Llama-style transformer, here are my dims, here is my vocab." `Verifies:` GGUF spec — "**`general.architecture: string`**: describes what architecture this model implements. All lowercase ASCII" listed under "Required" (https://github.com/ggml-org/ggml/blob/master/docs/gguf.md) + local `local-llm/GGUF_FORMAT.md` §2.B ("the `general.architecture` value is the key that names the whole model family — it tells the loader which `llama.*` and `tokenizer.ggml.*` keys to expect").
- **The metadata describes the model: `context_length`, `embedding_length`, `block_count` (dims), plus tokenizer model + chat template.** `Verifies:` GGUF spec — "`[llm].context_length: uint64`… length of the context (in tokens) that the model was trained on"; "`[llm].embedding_length: uint64`… Embedding layer size"; "`[llm].block_count: uint64`… The number of blocks of attention+feed-forward layers" (https://github.com/ggml-org/ggml/blob/master/docs/gguf.md) + GGUF spec tokenizer section — "`tokenizer.ggml.model: string`: The name of the tokenizer model" and "`tokenizer.chat_template: string`: a Jinja template that specifies the input format expected by the model" (https://github.com/ggml-org/ggml/blob/master/docs/gguf.md).
- **The quant type is baked into the file — `general.file_type` (a uint32 enum) names the dominant quant, and each tensor's `type` field (a `ggml_type`) tells the loader how to dequantize.** `MOSTLY_Q4_K_M = 15`, `MOSTLY_F16 = 1`, etc.; per the HF quant table `Q4_K` is `4.5` bits-per-weight, so a `Q4_K_M` file's bpw is derivable straight from the metadata. `Verifies:` GGUF spec — "`general.file_type: uint32`: An enumerated value describing the type of the majority of the tensors in the file" with `MOSTLY_Q4_K_M = 15`, `MOSTLY_F16 = 1` (https://github.com/ggml-org/ggml/blob/master/docs/gguf.md) + HuggingFace GGUF docs quant table — "Q4_K… resulting in 4.5 bits-per-weight"; "Q6_K… resulting in 6.5625 bits-per-weight" (https://huggingface.co/docs/hub/en/gguf). *(Ties back to 3.5: the file carries the quant type, so the loader knows which `w = m + d·q` block layout to unpack per tensor.)*
- **Why single-file + mmap is good for loading: the weight blob is alignment-padded (default 32) so the OS can `mmap` it directly — lazy, page-faulted, shared across processes.** `Verifies:` GGUF spec — "`mmap` compatibility: models can be loaded using `mmap` for fast loading and saving"; "**`general.alignment: uint32`**: the global alignment… If the alignment is not specified, assume it is `32`"; tensor "offset is relative to `tensor_data`… Must be a multiple of `ALIGNMENT`" (https://github.com/ggml-org/ggml/blob/master/docs/gguf.md) + local `local-llm/GGUF_FORMAT.md` §2.D ("because `tensor_data` is alignment-padded, the OS can `mmap` the weight blob directly — lazy, page-faulted loading"). *(Ties to 1.4 / 3.x memory: decode is bandwidth-bound, so a file that maps straight into the address space with no copy is a real win.)*

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. What three things does a `.gguf` bundle in one file — and how is that different from safetensors?
2. Why did GGUF replace the old GGML format — what did the typed key-value metadata section buy?
3. Which single required metadata key names the model family, and how does it tell the loader what to expect?
4. How does the file *carry* its quant type — and where does the loader read bits-per-weight from? (tie → 3.5)
5. Why is single-file + `mmap` good for loading a multi-GB model — what does alignment have to do with it?
6. Why is the quant type "baked in" — can you change a `Q4_K_M` file to `Q6_K` without re-converting? (the gotcha)

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `gguf_format.py`. **Determinism:** the "header" is a **pinned mock metadata dict** (mirrors exactly what a real GGUF loader extracts into its KV dict after parsing the binary); no `random`/`Date.now()`/network/file-I/O; output byte-stable on re-run.
- **The pinned mock header** (the KV a loader would hand you after reading the metadata section):
  ```python
  GGUF_META = {
      "general.architecture": "llama",
      "general.name": "Llama-3-8B-Instruct-Q4_K_M",
      "general.file_type": 15,            # MOSTLY_Q4_K_M
      "general.quantization_version": 2,
      "llama.context_length": 8192,
      "llama.embedding_length": 4096,
      "llama.block_count": 32,
      "tokenizer.ggml.model": "llama",
      "tokenizer.ggml.bos_token_id": 128000,
      "tokenizer.ggml.eos_token_id": 128001,
  }
  ```
- **The pinned file_type → (quant_name, bpw) table** (values straight from the HF GGUF docs quant table):
  ```python
  FILE_TYPE = {
      0:  ("ALL_F32",        32.0),
      1:  ("MOSTLY_F16",     16.0),
      7:  ("MOSTLY_Q8_0",     8.5),
      15: ("MOSTLY_Q4_K_M",   4.5),   # Q4_K block rate
      18: ("MOSTLY_Q6_K",     6.5625),
  }
  ```
- **Validate:** assert the required keys are present — `general.architecture` (selects the loader), `<arch>.context_length`, `<arch>.embedding_length`, `general.file_type` (for bpw) — then read `general.architecture` (`"llama"`) to know the `llama.*` namespace.
- **Report the model's key specs** → the parsed spec dict.
- **Gold value (must match on re-run):** the parsed spec for the pinned header:
  ```python
  {
      "architecture":     "llama",
      "name":             "Llama-3-8B-Instruct-Q4_K_M",
      "file_type":        15,
      "quant_type":       "Q4_K_M",
      "bpw":              4.5,
      "context_length":   8192,
      "embedding_length": 4096,
      "block_count":      32,
      "tokenizer":        "llama",
  }
  ```
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] required keys present (general.architecture, llama.context_length, llama.embedding_length, general.file_type)` · `[check] bpw derivable: general.file_type=15 -> Q4_K_M -> bpw 4.5` · `[check] architecture 'llama' selects the llama.* key namespace`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** swap the mock to a second pinned header — `general.architecture="qwen2"`, `general.file_type=18` (`MOSTLY_Q6_K`), `qwen2.context_length=32768` — re-validate and re-derive: quant_type `Q6_K`, bpw `6.5625`. Then *break* it on purpose: delete `general.file_type` and show the validator flags the missing key — the loader cannot derive bpw, which is exactly why a half-converted file fails to load. *(Seeds 3.8 GPU offload — the loader reads bpw to compute how many layers fit in VRAM.)*

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *What's inside a .gguf (the model file, unpacked).*
- **Angle:** one file, self-describing. A `.gguf` is not magic — it is a typed key-value header (architecture, dims, tokenizer) followed by the quantized weight bytes, and a loader reads the header to know *what* it is holding before it touches a single weight. The headline insight: the metadata makes one loader run every architecture, and the quant type is baked in.
- **Payload:** the three bundled things (metadata / tokenizer / tensors) · the GGML→GGUF lineage (extensibility = typed KV, no loader code change per architecture) · the key fields a loader reads first (`general.architecture` → `context_length` / `embedding_length` / `file_type`) · why single-file + alignment-padded `mmap` loads fast and lazy.
- **Gotcha:** a GGUF's quant type is **baked in** — `general.file_type` and each tensor's `ggml_type` are written when the file is *converted*. You cannot "change the quant" of an existing `.gguf` in place; you re-run the converter/quantizer (`llama-quantize model.f16.gguf model.Q4_K_M.gguf Q4_K_M`), which rewrites the tensor data and sets `general.file_type`. Trying to flip the metadata key alone leaves the bytes mismatched and the loader dequantizes garbage.
- **Video (`T3`):** Hook("one self-describing file — metadata + tokenizer + tensors") → Mechanism(header KV: `general.architecture` → `<arch>.*` dims + tokenizer → the quantized tensor blob) → Gold(the parsed spec dict: `Q4_K_M` / bpw `4.5` / ctx `8192`) → Gotcha(quant is baked in — can't change it without re-converting).

- [ ] demo runs, exits 0, ≥2 `[check]` lines
- [ ] output byte-stable on re-run
- [ ] gold value reproduced, matches reference

- [ ] article centers on one annotated diagram + ≥1 gotcha

## 3.8 GPU offload

> The "make it fit" trick. After this, "the model is bigger than my VRAM"
> stops being a hard wall and becomes *one knob* — `num_gpu_layers` (`-ngl`) — and
> one insight: you don't have to put the whole model on the GPU. You put **N
> transformer layers** on the GPU (decode fast, high bandwidth) and **leave the
> rest on the CPU/RAM** (decode slow, low bandwidth). The model runs either way;
> the question is *how fast*, and the answer is a single formula that ties straight
> back to **1.4** (decode is bandwidth-bound) and **3.6** (the VRAM budget you're
> splitting). This is the pragmatic reason a 70B runs on a 24 GB card at all.
> **Prerequisites:** 3.6 (VRAM math), 1.4 (decode bandwidth), 1.5 (KV cache).
> **Pays off in:** running bigger models than your VRAM, 3.16 (ktransformers /
> CPU-MoE). **Video:** `T2`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `local-llm/GPU_OFFLOAD.md` (§0 the `-ngl` lineage all-CPU → partial → full; §2 the layer split + the one activation that crosses the seam; §4 the `ngl = min(floor((VRAM − overhead − KV)/per_layer), n_layers)` decision recipe) · `local-llm/gpu_offload.py` (Section A the 0/8/16/24/32 split table; Section C why the seam copy is *not* the bottleneck; Section D the decision tree + the linear-then-flat decode curve; Section G the 8B gold) — re-run it, reproduce the gold (`-ngl 32`, 5.52 GiB), then **extend it** to a model that does *not* fit (Step 4: 70B on a 24 GB 4090).
- **Canonical:** llama.cpp `tools/server/README.md` (the `-ngl` / `--n-gpu-layers` flag) · Ollama GPU-offload docs / the `num_gpu` Modelfile parameter · Ollama issue #8850 ("23 of the layers run in system RAM where the CPU does the inference").
- **Goal:** state, from memory, what `-ngl N` does, why a model that doesn't fit can still *run*, why partial offload is bottlenecked by the **CPU layers** (not the copy), and how to compute the offload split + the resulting decode speed from one formula.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **`-ngl N` (a.k.a. `--n-gpu-layers`, Ollama's `num_gpu`) puts the first N transformer layers on the GPU; the remaining L−N stay on the CPU.** `-ngl 0` = all-CPU, `-ngl 999` / `-ngl all` = full offload. It is **layer-based, not byte-based** — you pick a layer count and the engine fits as many as VRAM allows. `Verifies:` llama.cpp tuning guide (dev.to, pat9000) — "`--n-gpu-layers` (or `-ngl`) tells llama.cpp how many of those layers to put on the GPU. The rest run on the CPU. Layers on the GPU run fast" (https://dev.to/pat9000/how-to-tune-n-gpu-layers-for-your-vram-budget-4o79) + bmdpat *llama.cpp n-gpu-layers Explained (2026)* — "The `--n-gpu-layers` flag (or `-ngl` for short) tells llama.cpp how many of those [transformer] layers to move from system RAM onto your GPU" (https://bmdpat.com/blog/llama-cpp-n-gpu-layers-explained-2026).
- **Partial offload exists because a model can be bigger than your VRAM.** A Transformer is a stack of identical layers; if the *whole stack* won't fit in VRAM, you still get a correct answer by running the overflow layers on the CPU — slower, but functional. This is why you can run a 70B on a 24 GB card at all (the 70B Q4_K_M weights alone are ~40 GB; the card holds ~24). `Verifies:` Ollama issue #8850 — "The model doesn't fit in VRAM, 23 of the layers run in system RAM where the CPU does the inference. Inference happens per layer" (https://github.com/ollama/ollama/issues/8850) + Ollama issue #5843 — "ollama offloads as many layers as it thinks will fit in GPU VRAM" (https://github.com/ollama/ollama/issues/5843).
- **The offloaded layers decode fast (GPU HBM bandwidth); the CPU layers decode slow (DDR bandwidth).** Decode is memory-bandwidth-bound (1.4): to emit one token, each layer's weights must be streamed out of memory once. A GPU layer streams from VRAM (~1 TB/s); a CPU layer streams from system DRAM (~80 GB/s) — over an order of magnitude slower per layer. `Verifies:` sergiiob.dev *GPU VRAM, CPU Offload, and llama.cpp: The Real Performance Cliff* — "When part of the model sits in VRAM and the rest has to be pulled from system RAM, the GPU spends more time waiting on transfers. That is the real performance cliff" (https://sergiiob.dev/posts/gpu-vram-cpu-offload-llama-cpp-deep-dive/) + the 1.4 carry (DistServe §2.1: decode "is constrained by the GPU's memory bandwidth"; https://arxiv.org/html/2401.09670v3) applied per-tier.
- **The slowest (CPU) layers bottleneck each token — you are as fast as your slowest tier.** Because the layers run *in sequence* (token flows layer 0 → 1 → … → L−1), every token must pass through *both* the fast GPU block and the slow CPU block; the CPU block's serial decode time dominates the per-token latency. Adding more GPU layers removes CPU layers from that hot path, so decode speed climbs — but only until the last CPU layer is gone. `Verifies:` sergiiob.dev — "the closer you are to full VRAM residency, the better the experience; the more you spill into RAM, the more you pay in TTFT and throughput" (https://sergiiob.dev/posts/gpu-vram-cpu-offload-llama-cpp-deep-dive/) + the local `GPU_OFFLOAD.md` §2c — "partial offload's runtime cost is the CPU COMPUTE on the `L-ngl` non-offloaded layers … More GPU layers ⇒ fewer CPU layers in the hot path ⇒ faster decode" (grounded in ggml's layer-split scheduler).
- **The seam copy is *not* the bottleneck — the CPU compute is.** The only thing that crosses the CPU↔GPU boundary every token is a tiny **activation** (decode: `1 × dim × 2 B` = 8 KiB for an 8B; negligible vs. the ~hundreds-of-MiB of weights that moved *once* at load). So don't optimize the copy — offload more layers. `Verifies:` local `GPU_OFFLOAD.md` §2c + `gpu_offload.py` Section C — decode activation == 8 KiB, "a layer's weights are 18514× bigger than one decode activation" + sergiiob.dev memory-hierarchy table (GPU VRAM "very high" bandwidth vs system RAM "much lower" — the bottleneck is the RAM-resident layers' bandwidth, not the PCIe transfer; https://sergiiob.dev/posts/gpu-vram-cpu-offload-llama-cpp-deep-dive/).
- **The offload count is one formula (tie → 3.6 VRAM math): `ngl = min( floor((VRAM − overhead − KV(ctx)) / weight_per_layer), n_layers )`.** Weights-per-layer × `ngl` + the KV cache (1.5) + runtime overhead must fit in VRAM; the floor is how many whole layers that leaves room for, capped at the model's layer count. `Verifies:` sergiiob.dev — the VRAM budget "`total_vram_needed ≈ (weights_gb × offload_pct) + kv_cache_gb + runtime_overhead` … leave headroom; do not try to fill VRAM to 100%" (https://sergiiob.dev/posts/gpu-vram-cpu-offload-llama-cpp-deep-dive/) + the local `GPU_OFFLOAD.md` §6 cheat-sheet decision recipe (mirrors `gpu_offload.py` `plan_offload`).
- **Decode speed is ~linear in the number of offloaded layers, then FLAT at full offload.** Each extra GPU layer removes a CPU-bound layer from the hot path, so tok/s rises roughly linearly — until `ngl == n_layers`, when no CPU layer remains and decode becomes pure VRAM-bandwidth-bound; beyond that, more GPU memory cannot help (the curve plateaus). That plateau *is* the diminishing-returns ceiling: the last layers give the same per-layer speedup as the first, but you run out of layers to move. `Verifies:` sergiiob.dev — "Each extra offloaded layer pushes more of the hot path into VRAM. That usually helps until you run out of headroom" (https://sergiiob.dev/posts/gpu-vram-cpu-offload-llama-cpp-deep-dive/) + local `GPU_OFFLOAD.md` §4 decode-speed curve ("~linear in `ngl`, then flat: once the last CPU layer is gone, decode is VRAM-bandwidth-bound").
- **The honest trade-off: a smaller model that FULLY fits beats a bigger model that spills.** If your goal is fast interactive chat, a model that stays 100% in VRAM (no CPU layer in the path) is usually better than a larger model that "technically loads" through spillover — because one CPU layer in the hot path drags the whole decode. `Verifies:` sergiiob.dev — "A model that mostly fits in fast memory beats a larger model that technically loads through spillover … if the goal is fast interactive chat, a smaller model that fits well is usually the better engineering decision" (https://sergiiob.dev/posts/gpu-vram-cpu-offload-llama-cpp-deep-dive/) + the local `GPU_OFFLOAD.md` §5 pitfall "Assuming full offload always wins … Prefer fit over size."

> **⚠ Flagged — the decode-speed formula is a teaching model, not a benchmark.** The relative-speed estimate `eff_bw = 1/(gpu_frac/bw_gpu + cpu_frac/bw_cpu)` is a bandwidth-bound per-layer blend (the 1.4 roofline applied per memory tier). It captures the *qualitative* behavior — the slow CPU term dominates, so partial offload's effective bandwidth sits far closer to the CPU than to the GPU — but real tok/s also depends on prefill overhead, batch size, the exact seam-pipelining, and SIMD throughput on the CPU layers. The bundle's own curve (`GPU_OFFLOAD.md` §4) is likewise labelled "illustrative." Treat the **offload split** (`ngl`) as the hard, verified number; treat the **eff_bw** as a teaching-shape, not a datasheet.
>
> **⚠ Flagged — GB vs GiB.** The 70B Q4_K_M GGUF is reported as **42.52 GB** (decimal, file size). VRAM is sized in **GiB** (binary — a "24 GB" GDDR6X card is genuinely 24 GiB ≈ 25.77 GB). This unit converts properly: 42.52 GB = **39.60 GiB**. Using the marketing "GB" figure as-if GiB (the sibling `gpu_offload.py` does this for the 8B) would over-count weights ~7% and shift the boundary from 44→41 layers; this unit converts correctly. (The 8B gold is unaffected — it fits either way.) The *lesson* (the split + the CPU bottleneck) is identical; the exact layer count is the only thing the GB/GiB choice moves.

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. What does `-ngl N` (`num_gpu_layers`) actually do — which layers go where, and what happens at `-ngl 0` vs `-ngl 999`?
2. *Why* does partial offload exist at all — what problem does it solve, and why is it the only way to run a 70B on a 24 GB card?
3. Why do the **CPU layers bottleneck** decode? Tie it to 1.4 — exactly what must each layer read to emit one token, and why is the CPU tier's read ~an order of magnitude slower?
4. Write the offload-count formula from memory: `ngl = min(floor((VRAM − overhead − KV)/per_layer), n_layers)`. Which term comes from 3.6 / 1.5, and why is there a `min`?
5. The seam copy is tiny — why is the bottleneck the **CPU compute**, not the PCIe transfer? (one line)
6. What is the **speed/VRAM trade-off curve**, and why does it go flat (diminishing returns) at full offload? When does it make more sense to *buy more VRAM* (or a smaller model that fully fits) than to keep offloading?

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `gpu_offload.py` (re-uses `local-llm/gpu_offload.py`'s `plan_offload` + `kv_gib`/`weight_per_layer_gib` helpers; extend it with the 70B model + the `eff_bw` formula). **Determinism:** every model config, VRAM size, and bandwidth figure is a pinned constant — **no RNG, no `random`/`Date.now()`/network**; output is byte-stable on re-run. (Pure arithmetic comparison — no seed needed because no randomness exists.)
- **The offload formula (tie → 3.6):** `ngl = min( floor((gpu_vram − overhead − KV(ctx)) / weight_per_layer), n_layers )`, where `weight_per_layer = WEIGHT_GIB[quant] / n_layers` and `KV(ctx) = 2 × n_layers × n_kv_heads × head_dim × ctx × bpe` (the 1.5 term; the leading `2` is K+V).
- **The speed formula (tie → 1.4):** `eff_bw = 1 / (gpu_frac/bw_gpu + cpu_frac/bw_cpu)`, with `gpu_frac = ngl/n_layers`, `cpu_frac = (n_layers−ngl)/n_layers`. This is the bandwidth-bound roofline applied *per memory tier* — when all layers are on GPU, `eff_bw = bw_gpu`; when all on CPU, `eff_bw = bw_cpu`; in between, the slow CPU term dominates (the "you're as fast as your slowest tier" effect).
- **Pinned model (the model that does NOT fit):**
  ```python
  LLAMA70B = {"name":"Llama-3-70B", "n_layers":80, "n_kv_heads":8, "head_dim":128}
  # Q4_K_M GGUF = 42.52 GB file (bartowski/aimodels.fyi) -> 39.60 GiB weights
  WEIGHT_GIB_70B_Q4KM = 42.52e9 / (1024**3)   # = 39.60 GiB
  RTX4090 = {"vram_gib":24.0, "bw_gbs":1008}  # 24GB GDDR6X, 1008 GB/s
  CPU_DDR5 = {"bw_gbs":80.0}                   # dual-channel system DRAM
  OVERHEAD_GIB = 0.50
  ```
- **Gold value (must match on re-run):** **Llama-3-70B Q4_K_M, RTX 4090 (24 GiB), 4096 ctx, f16 KV**:
  - weights = 39.60 GiB (**0.495 GiB/layer**); KV @4096 = `2×80×8×4096×128×2` = 1,342,177,280 B = **1.25 GiB**; overhead = **0.50 GiB**.
  - avail for weights = `24 − 0.50 − 1.25` = **22.25 GiB**; max layers = `floor(22.25 / 0.495)` = **44**; `ngl = min(44, 80)` = **44**.
  - **Split: 44/80 layers on GPU, 36/80 on CPU** (`-ngl 44`). Total VRAM = `0.495×44 + 1.25 + 0.50` = **23.53 GiB** (headroom 0.47 GiB; 45 layers = 24.02 GiB → doesn't fit, clamp is correct).
  - `gpu_frac = 0.55`, `cpu_frac = 0.45` → **`eff_bw = 1/(0.55/1008 + 0.45/80) = 162 GB/s`**.
  - **Relative speed:** partial offload runs at **~16% of full-offload speed** (`162 / 1008`) — **~6.2× slower** than if the 70B fully fit in VRAM — but **~2.0× faster** than all-CPU (`162 / 80`). The effective bandwidth (162) sits far closer to the CPU's 80 than the GPU's 1008: *the CPU layers dominate.*
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] offload count (44) <= n_layers (80): True` · `[check] more VRAM => more offloaded => faster: 24GiB -> 44/80 (eff 162 GB/s); 48GiB -> 80/80 full offload (eff 1008 GB/s, ~6.2x faster)` · `[check] eff_bw(162) closer to CPU(80) than GPU(1008): slowest-tier bottleneck`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** (a) sweep `ngl` 0→80 and print the `eff_bw` curve — show it climbs from 80 (all-CPU) toward 1008 but is *pinned near the CPU floor* until the CPU layers are nearly gone (the diminishing-returns shape); (b) re-run on a **12 GiB** card (`avail = 12−0.50−1.25 = 10.25` → `ngl = floor(10.25/0.495) = 20/80`, `eff_bw = 1/(0.25/1008 + 0.75/80) = 103 GB/s`) — half the VRAM ⇒ roughly half the offloaded layers ⇒ barely above CPU-only, which is *why* people say "if it spills too much, use a smaller model"; (c) drop in your own (model, quant, GPU, ctx) and re-print → seeds **3.10** (quantize the KV term to claw back headroom for more `ngl`) and **3.16** (ktransformers: keep the MoE experts on CPU, shared attention on GPU).

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *Offloading: the partial-GPU trick for big models.*
- **Angle:** can't fit a 70B in 24 GB? Offload *some* layers, run it anyway — slower. One knob (`-ngl`), one formula for the split, one insight (the CPU layers are the bottleneck), one curve (linear gains, then flat). This is the direct payoff of 1.4 (decode is bandwidth-bound) hitting the 3.6 VRAM wall.
- **Payload:** what `-ngl`/`num_gpu` does (first N layers → GPU, rest → CPU) · why it works (layers are independent, the model is correct either way) · the offload-split formula (`min(floor((VRAM − overhead − KV)/per_layer), n_layers)`) · the CPU-bottleneck insight (each token must traverse both the fast GPU block and the slow CPU block; you're as fast as your slowest tier) · the linear-then-flat curve and the "prefer a model that fully fits" rule.
- **Gotcha:** one CPU layer in the hot path drags the *whole* decode — partial offload's effective bandwidth sits near the CPU floor, not the GPU ceiling. A 70B at 44/80 on a 4090 decodes at ~16% of its full-offload speed; a smaller model that fully fits will usually beat it for chat. Offloading is a "run the impossible" tool, not a free speedup.
- **Video (`T2`):** Hook("a 70B won't fit in a 4090's 24 GB — but it'll run anyway") → Mechanism(the layer stack split: first N layers on GPU at ~1 TB/s, the rest on CPU at ~80 GB/s; one tiny activation crosses the seam) → Gold(70B Q4_K_M on 24 GB: 44/80 on GPU, eff_bw 162 GB/s — ~6.2× slower than full offload, ~2× faster than CPU-only) → Gotcha(you're as fast as your slowest tier — one CPU layer in the path drags the whole decode; prefer a model that fully fits for chat).

## 3.9 Context extension

> Fitting more context (and what you lose). After this, "the model accepts 128k"
> stops being a spec sheet you trust and becomes a *hack with a cliff*: you can
> push past the trained context length, but the positions go out-of-distribution
> and quality degrades — fastest at the far end. Concept only; *how servers keep
> that inflated cache from OOMing* is 3.10 / 3.13.
> **Prerequisites:** 1.3 (context window), 1.5 (KV cache). **Pays off in:**
> long-context apps. **Video:** `T2`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `local-llm/CONTEXT_EXTENSION.md` (§0 the four-method TL;DR; §1 the PI → NTK → YaRN → dynamic-NTK lineage; §2 the frequency ladder + the extrapolation problem; the GOLD `θ_new = 10000·4² = 160000` for D=4, scale=4) · `local-llm/context_extension.py` (Sections A–F: the RoPE ladder, linear/PI, NTK-aware, YaRN bands, dynamic NTK, the comparison) · `../llm/ROPE.md` (the base rotation `angle = position·freq` this guide *scales* — skim only).
- **Canonical:** Chen et al., *Extending Context Window of Large Language Models via Positional Interpolation* (arXiv:2306.15595) · Peng et al., *YaRN: Efficient Context Window Extension of Large Language Models* (arXiv:2309.00071) · EleutherAI, *Extending the RoPE* (the lineage walkthrough by the YaRN authors) · Liu et al., *Lost in the Middle: How Language Models Use Long Contexts* (arXiv:2307.03172).
- **Goal:** state, from memory, why a model has a max context at all (RoPE goes OOD), the three scaling methods (PI / NTK-aware / YaRN) in one line each, the length-vs-quality trade-off, and why extending ≠ retraining.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **A model has a *training* context length — the max positions its RoPE angles were ever seen at; past it the model extrapolates and quality collapses.** RoPE rotates each token by `position · freq`; during training the model only ever saw `position` up to `L_orig`, so the rotation angle `i·freq` is in-distribution only up to `i = L_orig`. Beyond that the angle is unseen — out-of-distribution — and the model guesses. `Verifies:` Peng et al., *YaRN* (arXiv:2309.00071) — "Rotary Position Embeddings (RoPE) … these models **fail to generalize past the sequence length they were trained on**" (https://arxiv.org/abs/2309.00071) + Chen et al., *Positional Interpolation* (arXiv:2306.15595) — "rather than extrapolating beyond the trained context length which may lead to **catastrophically high attention scores that completely ruin the self-attention mechanism**" (https://arxiv.org/abs/2306.15595) + EleutherAI, *Extending the RoPE* — "the RoPE is limited by its pretrained context size" (https://blog.eleuther.ai/yarn/).
- **RoPE scaling extends the *effective* context by reshaping the angle function — it is an inference-time hack, not retraining.** The fix never touches the weights: it remaps how positions map to angles so that positions up to a new target `L_target` land back inside the trained angle range. PI needs a little fine-tuning; NTK-aware and dynamic-NTK work zero-shot at load time. `Verifies:` Chen et al., *Positional Interpolation* — "Models extended via Position Interpolation **retain its original architecture and can reuse most pre-existing optimization and infrastructure**" with "**minimal fine-tuning (within 1000 steps)**" (https://arxiv.org/abs/2306.15595) + Peng et al., *YaRN* — "a compute-efficient method to extend the context window … requiring **10x less tokens and 2.5x less training steps** than previous methods" (https://arxiv.org/abs/2309.00071) + local `local-llm/CONTEXT_EXTENSION.md` §3 (the `llama.cpp`/HF `rope_scaling` flags are set at load time, not training).
- **Position Interpolation (PI) compresses positions to fit the trained range — divide every position by `s = L_target/L_orig`.** Equivalent to `freq_eff = freq/s` for *every* dimension. It maps `L_target` back onto the original angle ceiling, but it scales the high-frequency (local) dims too, so adjacent tokens that used to differ by a full rotation now differ by `1/s` — local structure blurs. `Verifies:` Chen et al. — "Position Interpolation **linearly down-scales the input position indices to match the original context window size**" (https://arxiv.org/abs/2306.15595) + EleutherAI — PI is `g(m) = m/s, h(θ_d) = θ_d`; "We simply 'squeeze' the new sequence inside the original context window … The scaling by PI reduces the frequencies `θ_d` uniformly" (https://blog.eleuther.ai/yarn/).
- **NTK-aware scaling preserves the high-frequency (local) dims and stretches only the low-frequency (long-range) ones — by changing the base, not the positions.** `θ_new = θ · s^(D/(D−2))`. Because `θ^(2·0/D) = 1` for any base, the highest-frequency dim (`d=0`) is mathematically immune to the base change — local structure never blurs — while the lowest dim gets the full `/s` stretch. That single recurring trick is why NTK beats linear. `Verifies:` EleutherAI — "`b' = b · s^(|D|/(|D|-2))` … we spread out the interpolation pressure across multiple dimensions by **scaling high frequencies less and low frequencies more**" (https://blog.eleuther.ai/yarn/) + Brenndoerfer, *NTK-aware Scaling* — derives `b' = b·s^(d/(d-2))` from "preserve high frequencies / scale low frequencies," and shows "When i=0 … `scale_0 = 1`. The highest frequencies are *not scaled at all*" (https://mbrenndoerfer.com/writing/ntk-aware-scaling-context-extension) + local `context_extension.py` Section C (the GOLD check: `θ_new = 160000` for D=4, s=4; `freq_0` stays `1.0`).
- **YaRN is a band-wise spectral mix + an attention-temperature factor — best at extreme (8×–32×) extension.** It classifies each frequency dim by how many full rotations its wavelength completes over `L_orig` (extrapolation band = no change; interpolation band = full `/s`; ramp in between) and additionally divides the attention logits by a temperature `t` with `√(1/t) = 0.1·ln(s) + 1` to absorb the distribution shift. `Verifies:` Peng et al., *YaRN* (arXiv:2309.00071, the method + attention factor; https://arxiv.org/abs/2309.00071) + EleutherAI — "YaRN method refers to a combination of this temperature-scaling technique and the 'NTK-by-parts' interpolation … `√(1/t) = 0.1 ln(s) + 1`" with band boundaries `α=1, β=32` (https://blog.eleuther.ai/yarn/).
- **It is a trade-off: you buy length, you pay quality — and the bill is steepest at the far end ("lost in the middle").** Even with YaRN the model does not truly understand 128k the way it understands 8k; performance is highest near the start and end of the context and degrades when the answer sits in the middle. "It accepts 128k" ≠ "it is good at 128k." `Verifies:` Liu et al., *Lost in the Middle* — "performance is often highest when relevant information occurs at the beginning or end of the input context, and **significantly degrades when models must access relevant information in the middle of long contexts**, even for explicitly long-context models" (arXiv:2307.03172, https://arxiv.org/abs/2307.03172) + EleutherAI — PI/NTK/YaRN all trade extrapolation: "After finetuning on longer sequences, **the perplexity slightly increases** … All methods trade some extrapolation ability" (https://blog.eleuther.ai/yarn/) + local `local-llm/CONTEXT_EXTENSION.md` §5 (pitfall: "Expecting no quality loss at all … all methods trade some extrapolation ability").
- **Extending the window multiplies the KV cache, not the weights — and it scales linearly with context.** RoPE scaling itself is free (it only remaps positions to angles); the cost lands on the KV cache from 1.5: `kv_bytes = 2·n_layers·n_kv_heads·ctx·head_dim·bpe`, all linear, so `s×` context = `s×` cache bytes, forever. Long windows OOM through the cache, not the weights. `Verifies:` Brenndoerfer, *KV Cache Memory* — "every term in this product is linear: there are no squares … Sequence length has a linear effect on KV cache size" (https://mbrenndoerfer.com/writing/kv-cache-memory-calculation-llm-inference-gpu) + Marques, *KV cache memory calculator* — Llama-3-8B: 4K→0.5 GB, 32K→4 GB (each 8× context = 8× bytes; https://dev.to/jagmarques/kv-cache-memory-calculator-how-much-does-your-llm-actually-use-85n) + local `local-llm/CONTEXT_EXTENSION.md` §3 ("extending context multiplies the KV cache, not the weights. A 4× window costs ~4× more KV-cache VRAM … The RoPE scaling itself is free"). *(Ties 1.5 → seeds 3.6 VRAM math, 3.10 KV-cache quant.)*

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. Why does a model have a *maximum* context length at all — what exactly is out-of-distribution past the training window?
2. What breaks past the trained length if you do *nothing* (raw extrapolation), and why is it catastrophic for attention?
3. Name the three RoPE-scaling methods (PI / NTK-aware / YaRN) and explain each in one line — what does each scale, and which dims does it leave alone?
4. Why does NTK-aware scaling leave the highest-frequency (local) dim untouched — what is the mathematical reason?
5. What is the length-vs-quality trade-off, and why does "it accepts 128k" NOT mean "it is good at 128k" (lost in the middle)?
6. Why is context extension an inference-time hack rather than retraining — and which term of the VRAM budget actually grows when you extend (tie → 1.5)?

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `context_extension.py`. **Determinism:** the trained length, scale factor, model config, and the quality-curve functions are all pinned constants; no `random`/`Date.now()`/network; output byte-stable on re-run. (The RoPE math is pure closed form; the quality curve is a *pinned toy model* of degradation, clearly labelled as such — not a measured perplexity.)
- **Pinned config:** trained context `L_orig = 4096`, scale `s = 8` → effective context `L_target = L_orig · s = 32768` (the canonical 4K→32K extension, same `L_orig` as `local-llm/context_extension.py`'s hero). Model = **Llama-3-8B** (`n_layers=32, n_kv_heads=8, head_dim=128`, fp16 `bpe=2`) — the same config as 1.5, so the KV line is a direct tie.
- **(a) Effective context:** `effective_ctx = L_orig · s`. Re-print the NTK-aware base for the real head_dim `D=128`: `θ_new = 10000·8^(128/126) ≈ 82684.6` (carried from `context_extension.py` Section C's `ntk_theta()`; the bundle's toy `D=4` gold `160000` stays the bundle's reference).
- **(b) KV-cost increase (tie 1.5):** `kv_bytes(ctx) = 2·n_layers·n_kv_heads·ctx·head_dim·bpe`. Print it at `L_orig` and `L_target`: **512 MiB → 4 GiB**, an exactly **8×** linear increase — the RoPE scaling is free, the cache is what you pay.
- **(c) Toy quality-degradation curve:** pin two deterministic functions sampled at `L_orig, 2·L_orig, 4·L_orig, L_target`:
  - **unscaled (raw extrapolation, the cliff):** `1.0` within `[0, L_orig]`, then `exp(−2·(pos−L_orig)/L_orig)` → `1.0000 / 0.1353 / 0.0025 / 0.0000` (catastrophic — the PI paper's "ruin the self-attention mechanism").
  - **NTK-scaled (the hack, partial recovery):** `1.0` within `[0, L_orig]`, then `max(0, 1 − 0.08·(pos/L_orig − 1))` → `1.0000 / 0.9200 / 0.7600 / 0.4400` (gradual — "good but not perfect," and still losing at the far end = lost in the middle).
- **Gold value (must match on re-run):** effective context **`32768`**; KV **`512 MiB @4096 → 4 GiB @32768` (8× linear)**; quality at `L_target`: unscaled **`0.0000`** (cliff) vs NTK **`0.4400`** (partial recovery).
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] effective_ctx == L_orig * s == 32768` · `[check] KV scales linearly: ctx x8 -> KV x8 (512 MiB -> 4 GiB)` · `[check] NTK recovers quality at L_target (0.4400) >> unscaled (0.0000)`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** swap the scale to `s=32` (4K→128K, the extreme YaRN regime) and re-print — effective ctx `131072`, KV **`16 GiB`** (32× the 512 MiB), NTK quality at the far end now **`0.0400`** (the cliff returns even for the hack). That visible re-collapse at 32× is exactly why YaRN (band-wise + attention factor) exists and why "128k on the box" is a spec, not a promise — the seed for long-context RAG chunking and 3.10 KV-cache quant.

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *Fitting more context (and what you lose).*
- **Angle:** you can push past the training length — but it is a hack with a cliff. One recurring trick (never blur the high-frequency local dims), three methods that apply it (PI → NTK → YaRN), and one bill that lands on the KV cache (→ 1.5), not the weights.
- **Payload:** why a model has a max context (RoPE goes OOD past `L_orig`) · the three scaling methods in one line each (PI compresses positions uniformly; NTK-aware stretches the base to spare the local dims; YaRN mixes per-band + an attention temperature) · the length-vs-quality trade-off and "lost in the middle" · extending ≠ retraining, and the cache grows linearly (`512 MiB → 4 GiB` at 8×).
- **Gotcha:** quality degrades at the far end of extended context — **"it accepts 128k" ≠ "it is good at 128k."** Even YaRN only *partially* recovers the far-end quality (the toy curve's `0.44` at 8×, re-collapsing to `0.04` at 32×), and the inflated KV cache is what OOMs your GPU first.
- **Video (`T2`):** Hook("128k on the box ≠ good at 128k") → Mechanism(RoPE OOD → scale the angles: PI/NTK/YaRN, one line each) → Gold(effective ctx `32768` + KV `512 MiB → 4 GiB`) → Gotcha(the quality cliff / lost in the middle).

- [ ] article centers on one annotated diagram + ≥1 gotcha
