## 3.10 KV-cache quant

> The long-context fit hack. After this, "I raised the context and now it OOMs"
> stops being a wall and becomes *one knob on one term* — the same `KV_CACHE` term
> from **1.5** and **3.6**, re-quantized. The rule of thumb said shrink the *weights*
> (→ 1.7 / 3.5); but at long context it is the **cache**, not the weights, that
> dominates the budget — so the highest-leverage move is to quantize the cache
> **independently** of the weights: keep your `Q4_K_M` weights (good quality) and
> drop the ephemeral KV cache from FP16 (2 B/E) to FP8 / INT8 (~1 B/E, ~2×
> smaller) or INT4 (~0.5 B/E, ~4× smaller). This unit is where **1.5** (what the
> cache stores), **1.7** (quantization), **3.5** (the GGML block format), and
> **3.6** (the three-term VRAM budget) all converge on a single lever — with the
> one catch that attention is *precision-sensitive*, so cache-quant costs more
> quality per bit than weight-quant, and FP8 / INT8 is the near-lossless default
> while INT4 is the aggressive option you eval before shipping.
> **Prerequisites:** 1.5 (KV cache), 1.7 (quantization), 3.6 (VRAM math). **Pays off in:**
> every long-context fit decision — 3.9 (context extension), 3.11 (vLLM), 3.13
> (paged attention). **Video:** `T2`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `local-llm/KV_CACHE_QUANT.md` (§0 the FP16→Q8_0→Q4_0 TL;DR; §2 the block-quant mechanism reusing the GGML format from 3.5; §4 the Llama-3-8B @32K GOLD table) · `local-llm/kv_cache_quant.py` (Sections A–E — the **single source of truth**: every byte count here is printed by it, including the `4.00 / 2.13 / 1.13 GiB` gold) — re-run it, reproduce the gold, then **extend it** with the full-budget fit decision (Step 4).
- **Sibling bundles you build on:** `llm/KV_CACHE.md` + `llm/kv_cache.py` (the cache *is* the term you are now shrinking — the `[B,H_kv,S,D]` layout and all-linear byte formula from **1.5**) · `llm/QUANTIZATION.md` (the "fewer bits, lossy rounding" concept from **1.7** — applied here to the *cache*, not the weights) · `local-llm/QUANT_TYPES.md` (the GGML block layout `Q8_0/Q4_0 = fp16 scale + int8/int4 data` from **3.5** — the cache reuses the *exact same* block format) · `local-llm/VRAM_ESTIMATOR.md` (the three-term budget `WEIGHTS + KV + OVERHEAD` from **3.6** — cache-quant is the cheapest way to halve the KV term of *that* sum).
- **Canonical:** vLLM *Quantized KV Cache* docs (the `kv_cache_dtype="fp8"` flag, the per-tensor / per-head calibration schemes) · llama.cpp *Configuration Parameters* (`--cache-type-k` / `--cache-type-v`, default `f16`, the supported `q8_0`/`q4_0`/`q4_1`/`iq4_nl` types — sourced from `common/arg.cpp`) · LMDeploy *INT4/INT8 KV Cache* docs (the int4/int8 = 4×/2× block-count claim + the "int8 lossless, int4 slight loss" evaluation) · NVIDIA DGX Spark *KV-cache quantization benchmarks* (the q4_0 prefill-slowdown at extreme context).
- **Goal:** state, from memory, *which* VRAM term KV-cache quant attacks and why that term is the one that balloons at long context, the FP16→FP8/INT4 savings (2× / 4×), the block-quant mechanism and its scale-overhead floor, the FP8/INT8-near-lossless vs INT4-degrades-more quality curve, the engine flags (`--cache-type-k/-v`, `kv_cache_dtype`), and — given `(model, quant, ctx, device)` — recompute the **3.6** budget with a quantized KV term and read off the fit decision.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **At long context the KV cache is the term that dominates VRAM — so it is the highest-leverage term to shrink.** Attention *compute* is `O(n²)` (1.2), but the cache is `O(n)` (1.5): one K,V per token, forever. By 3.6's budget, weights are *fixed* while the cache grows linearly with `n_ctx` until it crosses over the weights — so once you are long-context, shrinking the cache term buys more fit than shrinking the (already-shrunk) weights term. `Verifies:` vLLM *Quantized KV Cache* — "Efficient memory usage is crucial for working with large language models. Quantizing the KV (Key-Value) cache to FP8 format can significantly reduce its memory footprint. This optimization enables you to store more tokens in memory, leading to improved throughput and support for **longer context windows**" (https://docs.vllm.ai/en/latest/features/quantization/quantized_kvcache/) + LMDeploy *INT4/INT8 KV Cache* — "quantization is beneficial for increasing the number of kv block. Compared to fp16, the number of kv block for int4/int8 kv can be increased by 4 times and 2 times respectively… the system can support a significantly increased number of concurrent operations" (https://lmdeploy.readthedocs.io/en/latest/quantization/kv_quant.html). *(Carried from 3.6: Lyceum — "As you increase the context window from 4k to 32k tokens, the memory required for the KV cache increases by 8x"; the cache is the term that "eventually dwarf[s] the model weights," https://lyceum.technology/magazine/kv-cache-memory-calculation-llm/.)*
- **You can quantize the cache *independently* of the weights — FP16 (2 B/E) → FP8 / INT8 (~1 B/E, ~2×) or INT4 (~0.5 B/E, ~4×).** The cache precision is a separate knob from the weight quant type: a `Q4_K_M` model keeps its `Q4_K_M` weights and only compresses the *ephemeral* cache. The savings come straight from halving/quartering `bytes_per_element` in the 1.5 byte formula — the only term that does *not* trade quality for architecture. `Verifies:` llama.cpp *Configuration Parameters* — "`cache_type_k` … `--cache-type-k` / `-ctk`, default `f16`, supported types f32, f16, bf16, **q8_0, q4_0**, q4_1, iq4_nl, q5_0, q5_1"; "Quantizing the KV cache to `q8_0` roughly **halves** cache memory … while `q4_0` reduces it to **~25% of f16**" (https://zread.ai/ggml-org/llama.cpp/30-configuration-parameters, mirroring `common/arg.cpp`) + LMDeploy — "Compared to fp16, the number of kv block for **int4/int8 kv can be increased by 4 times and 2 times** respectively" (https://lmdeploy.readthedocs.io/en/latest/quantization/kv_quant.html). *(The exact GGML block B/E — Q8_0=1.0625, Q4_0=0.5625 — is computed in `kv_cache_quant.py` Section B and in `KV_CACHE_QUANT.md` §2; idealized FP8=1.0 / INT4=0.5 ignore the 2-byte-per-32 scale.)*
- **The mechanism reuses the GGML block format from 3.5 — a 2-byte fp16 scale per 32 elements.** `Q8_0 = [fp16 scale 2B][32×int8 32B] = 34B/32 = 1.0625 B/E`; `Q4_0 = [fp16 scale 2B][32×int4 16B] = 18B/32 = 0.5625 B/E`. That per-block fp16 scale is the **overhead floor**: it is why Q4_0 reaches ~3.6× (not the theoretical 4×) — but it is also *why quality survives*, because each block gets its own dynamic range so outliers are not crushed (the same outlier insight as 1.7). `Verifies:` llama.cpp *Configuration Parameters* — the cache "also uses quantization (Q8_0, Q4_0) for memory-efficient context storage, reusing the same block structures" as weight quantization (https://zread.ai/ggml-org/llama.cpp/30-configuration-parameters) + the local `KV_CACHE_QUANT.md` §2 (the `Q8_0`/`Q4_0` block layouts, computed in `kv_cache_quant.py` Section B — `34 bytes / 32 elems` and `18 bytes / 32 elems`). *(The block format itself is 3.5's `QUANT_TYPES.md`; this unit applies it to the cache tensor.)*
- **FP8 / INT8 cache-quant is near-lossless; INT4 degrades more — and you should eval before shipping long-context.** Attention is precision-sensitive (K,V feed every `Q·Kᵀ` score and every `probs·V` blend at every step), so cache error perturbs the output distribution more directly than a static weight error of the same bit-width. The empirical curve: 8-bit (vLLM FP8 / llama.cpp `q8_0` / LMDeploy int8) is "near-lossless" — enable by default; 4-bit (`q4_0` / int4) is "slight loss, acceptable for chat/retrieval, avoid for code/math." `Verifies:` vLLM *Quantized KV Cache* — `kv_cache_dtype="fp8"` supports uncalibrated use ("All quantization scales are set to 1.0") but recommends **calibration** to "recover accuracy" (https://docs.vllm.ai/en/latest/features/quantization/quantized_kvcache/) + LMDeploy — "int8 kv keeps the accuracy while **int4 kv has slight loss**"; "**KV int8 quantization has almost lossless accuracy, and KV int4 quantization accuracy is within an acceptable range**" (https://lmdeploy.readthedocs.io/en/latest/quantization/kv_quant.html) + llama.cpp *Configuration Parameters* — "q8_0 roughly halves cache memory **with minimal quality degradation**, while q4_0 … at a **more noticeable quality cost**" (https://zread.ai/ggml-org/llama.cpp/30-configuration-parameters). *(SGLang docs agree: "FP8 E4M3 quantization typically introduces minimal accuracy degradation," https://docs.sglang.io/advanced_features/quantized_kv_cache.html.)*
- **The engine flags: `--cache-type-k` / `--cache-type-v` (llama.cpp) and `kv_cache_dtype` (vLLM).** Both expose the cache precision as a runtime knob set *after* the model is loaded. K and V can even be set to *different* precisions (`-ctk q8_0 -ctv q4_0`), though the quality hit is dominated by the lower precision so symmetric is the common choice — and K is the more sensitive of the two (it sets the attention *scores*). `Verifies:` llama.cpp — `cache_type_k` (`--cache-type-k`/`-ctk`) and `cache_type_v` (`--cache-type-v`/`-ctv`), "The cache type can be set **independently for K and V**" (https://zread.ai/ggml-org/llama.cpp/30-configuration-parameters) + vLLM — the `kv_cache_dtype` options `"auto"` / `"fp8"` / `"fp8_e4m3"` / `"fp8_e5m2"` (https://docs.vllm.ai/en/latest/features/quantization/quantized_kvcache/). *(Evidence K is the sensitive axis: LMDeploy's TurboQuant spends its error-correction budget **asymmetrically** — K=4bit with the QJL algorithm + V=2bit — precisely because K precision matters more for the attention scores, https://lmdeploy.readthedocs.io/en/latest/quantization/kv_quant.html.)*
- **INT4 cache-quant has a dequant-overhead trap at extreme context — it is not free latency.** Compressing to 4-bit shrinks memory but adds a dequantization step every attention op; at very long context (64K+) that overhead can dominate prefill (prompt processing), so `q4_0` can be *slower* than `q8_0`/FP16 despite using less memory — the savings are in *capacity* (more tokens fit), not always in *speed*. `Verifies:` NVIDIA DGX Spark *KV-cache quantization benchmarks* — "q4_0 is **92% slower** at 64K context — dequantization overhead destroys prompt processing. The HuggingFace team warned about this" (https://forums.developer.nvidia.com/t/kv-cache-quantization-benchmarks-on-dgx-spark-q4-0-vs-q8-0-vs-f16-llama-cpp-nemotron-30b-128k-context/365138) + llama.cpp *Configuration Parameters* — the cache types trade "VRAM consumption at a quality cost" (the dequant compute is the latency side of that same cost, https://zread.ai/ggml-org/llama.cpp/30-configuration-parameters). *(Rule of thumb: `q8_0` has negligible overhead — it is the "free lunch"; reserve `q4_0` for when capacity, not latency, is the constraint.)*

> **⚠ Flagged — "KV-quant costs more quality per bit than weight-quant" is a practitioner synthesis, not a single citation.** The component facts are all verified above: (a) weights are *static* — quantized once, error fixed and bounded per weight (1.7); (b) the KV cache is *dynamic* — regenerated every token and read into the `Q·Kᵀ` score and `probs·V` output at every step, so cache error perturbs the attention distribution directly and repeatedly; (c) empirically 4-bit *weights* (`Q4_K_M`) are the recommended default with good quality (3.5) while 4-bit *cache* (`q4_0`) carries "a more noticeable quality cost" and is reserved for chat/retrieval. The exact "per-bit" ratio is model- and task-dependent (see the bundle's `~2–8% perplexity` range, empirical from llama.cpp benchmarks). The safe framing: **eval your real workload on a long-context benchmark before shipping INT4 cache** (→ 2.14); default to FP8/INT8.

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. Which of the **three VRAM terms** (3.6) does KV-cache quant attack — and *why* is that the highest-leverage term to shrink at long context (vs shrinking the weights)?
2. How much smaller is the KV cache at FP8 / INT8 vs at INT4 vs FP16 — the 2× / 4× headline and the exact `bytes_per_element` (FP16=2.0, FP8=1.0, Q8_0=1.0625, INT4=0.5, Q4_0=0.5625)? Why is the real Q4_0 saving ~3.6× and not 4×?
3. What block format does the cache reuse (→ 3.5), and why does the 2-byte fp16 scale per 32 elements act as both an *overhead floor* and the thing that *preserves quality* (→ 1.7 outliers)?
4. Why does cache-quant cost **more quality per bit than weight-quant** — what is different about how K,V are *used* vs how static weights are used? Which is near-lossless (FP8/INT8) and which degrades more (INT4)?
5. FP8 vs INT4 trade-off: when is each worth it? Why must you **eval on a long-context benchmark** before shipping INT4 cache (→ 2.14)?
6. Name the engine flags for both runtimes — `--cache-type-k`/`--cache-type-v` (llama.cpp) and `kv_cache_dtype` (vLLM). Can K and V differ, and which of the two is the more precision-sensitive?
7. End to end: take the **3.6** budget for Llama-3-8B `Q4_K_M` @32K (9.31 GB, does *not* fit 8 GB). Recompute with FP8 KV and with INT4 KV — which flip the fit, and by how much?

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `kv_cache_quant.py` (reproduces the `local-llm/kv_cache_quant.py` GOLD, then extends it with the **3.6** full-budget fit decision). **Determinism:** every config, B/E, and context length is a pinned constant; the work is pure arithmetic — **no RNG, no `random`/`Date.now()`/network**; output is byte-stable on re-run. (Nothing to draw and no sampling: this is a budget calculation, so no seed is needed because no randomness exists.)
- **The two formulas it composes (built from 1.5 + 1.7 + 3.5 + 3.6):**
  - `kv_bytes = 2 × n_layers × n_ctx × n_kv_heads × head_dim × bytes_per_element` — the 1.5 cache term; here `bytes_per_element` is the **one knob** cache-quant turns (FP16=2.0, FP8=1.0, Q8_0=1.0625, INT4=0.5, Q4_0=0.5625).
  - `total_gb = weights + kv + overhead` — the 3.6 budget (`weights = params × bpw / 8`; `overhead = max(0.5, 0.10 × weights)`); `fits = total ≤ 0.90 × nominal_VRAM`.
- **The block-quant B/E (from 3.5, the GGML format):** `Q8_0 = (2 + 32)/32 = 1.0625 B/E`; `Q4_0 = (2 + 16)/32 = 0.5625 B/E` — the 2-byte fp16 scale per 32 elements is the overhead floor that caps Q4_0 at ~3.6× (not 4×).
- **Model:** Llama-3-8B `{params=8.030261248B, n_layers=32, n_kv_heads=8, head_dim=128}` (GQA 4×), `Q4_K_M` weights (`4.5 bpw`). KV elements (K+V) @32K = `2 × 32 × 32768 × 8 × 128 = 2,147,483,648`.
- **Gold value (must match on re-run) — the KV-cache term @32K, reproducing `kv_cache_quant.py` Section C / `KV_CACHE_QUANT.md` §4:**
  | cache type | bytes_per_elem | total bytes | KV (GiB) | savings vs FP16 |
  |---|---|---|---|---|
  | FP16 | 2.0000 | 4,294,967,296 | **4.00** | baseline |
  | FP8 (idealized) | 1.0000 | 2,147,483,648 | **2.00** | 50% (2×) |
  | Q8_0 (INT8, exact) | 1.0625 | 2,281,701,376 | **2.13** | 46.9% (~1.9×) |
  | INT4 (idealized) | 0.5000 | 1,073,741,824 | **1.00** | 75% (4×) |
  | Q4_0 (INT4, exact) | 0.5625 | 1,207,959,552 | **1.13** | 71.9% (~3.6×) |
  *(At 32K the FP16 cache ≈ 4.00 GiB nearly matches the Q4_K_M weights ≈ 4.21 GiB — the "cache dominates at long context" gold hook from 1.5/3.6. Savings % are convention-independent; the bundle reports GiB (÷1024³), 3.6 reports GB (÷1e9) — they agree on 2×/4×.)*
- **The fit decision (the 3.6 extension) — `total = weights(4.52) + kv + overhead(0.50)`, vs an 8 GB card at 90%-usable ⇒ `fits = total ≤ 7.20 GB`:**
  - **FP16 KV:** 4.52 + 4.29 + 0.50 = **9.31 GB → does NOT fit** (carried verbatim from 3.6's gold).
  - **FP8 KV:** 4.52 + 2.15 + 0.50 = **7.16 GB → FITS** (≤ 7.20; the quantized cache flips a no-fit into a fit).
  - **Q8_0 KV:** 4.52 + 2.28 + 0.50 = **7.30 GB → just OVER the 7.20 usable line** (the near-lossless *exact* option sits ~0.14 GB too high — that gap *is* the Q8_0 scale-overhead floor vs idealized FP8; needs INT4 or a bigger card).
  - **INT4 KV:** 4.52 + 1.07 + 0.50 = **6.09 GB → FITS with headroom** (aggressive; eval quality first).
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] KV halved at FP8: FP16 4.00 GiB -> FP8 2.00 GiB (2x); Q8_0 2.13 GiB (1.88x exact)` · `[check] KV quartered at INT4: 4.00 -> 1.00 GiB (4x); Q4_0 1.13 GiB (3.56x exact)` · `[check] Llama-3-8B Q4_K_M @32K: FP16-KV total 9.31 GB (NO FIT 8GB) -> FP8-KV 7.16 GB (FITS) -> INT4-KV 6.09 GB (FITS)` · `[check] block-quant floor: Q4_0 B/E 0.5625 not 0.5 (2B fp16 scale / 32 elems)`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** (1) Push the same model to **128K** and print the dominance — FP16 KV balloons to **16.00 GiB** (>> weights 4.21 GiB); FP8 KV = 8.00 GiB, INT4 KV = 4.00 GiB. At 128K even an aggressive INT4 cache (4.00 GiB) is *smaller than the FP16 cache was at 32K* (4.00 GiB) — i.e. cache-quant buys you ~4× the context at the same memory, the direct bridge to **3.9** (context extension). (2) Reproduce the bundle's **cross-model** table (Section D) and note the savings *percentages* are identical across all models (they depend only on B/E) while the *absolute* GB saved scales with cache size — a plain-MHA model (Llama-2-13B, 40 KV heads) frees far more absolute GB than a GQA model. (3) Plug **YOUR actual device** and the model you really run long-context, and print which cache type is the cheapest one that still fits → seeds **3.11** (vLLM `kv_cache_dtype`) and **3.13** (paged attention, which makes the *number of blocks* the real unit of capacity cache-quant multiplies).

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *Quantize the cache too (the long-context fit hack).*
- **Angle:** at long context the cache *is* the budget — so shrink it. Weight-quant (1.7/3.5) attacks the *fixed* weights term; cache-quant attacks the term that *grows with context* (1.5/3.6) and is therefore the highest-leverage fit hack when you have raised `n_ctx`. One knob (`bytes_per_element`), one formula (the 1.5 byte count), 2× or 4× the headroom — independent of the weight quant, with FP8/INT8 as the near-lossless default.
- **Payload:** FP16→FP8/INT8 (~2×) / INT4 (~4×) on the cache term, with the exact GGML block B/E (Q8_0=1.0625, Q4_0=0.5625) and its scale-overhead floor · the block-format reuse from 3.5 (2-byte fp16 scale per 32 elems = why Q4_0 is ~3.6× not 4×, and why outliers don't crush quality) · the quality curve (FP8/INT8 near-lossless, INT4 "slight loss" — reserve for chat/retrieval) · the engine flags (`-ctk`/`-ctv`, `kv_cache_dtype`) · the fit-decision payoff (9.31 GB no-fit → 7.16/6.09 GB fit).
- **Gotcha:** cache-quant degrades attention quality **more per bit than weight-quant** — K,V are dynamic, read into every attention score every step, so cache error perturbs the output distribution directly. So **eval on a long-context benchmark before shipping INT4 cache** (→ 2.14); default to FP8/INT8 (the "free lunch"). And INT4 carries a *latency* trap at extreme context (64K+ dequant overhead) — it buys *capacity*, not always *speed*.
- **Video (`T2`):** Hook("at long context the cache dominates the budget — shrink *it*, not the weights") → Mechanism(FP16 cache 2 B/E → FP8/INT8 1 B/E (2×) / INT4 0.5 B/E (4×); the block-quant scale floor, animated) → Gold(the three totals @32K: FP16 4.00 → FP8 2.00 → INT4 1.00 GiB; the fit flip 9.31 → 7.16 → 6.09 GB) → Gotcha(cache-quant costs more quality per bit than weight-quant — eval INT4 before shipping).

### 6. Checklist
- [ ] demo runs, exits 0, ≥2 `[check]` lines
- [ ] output byte-stable on re-run
- [ ] gold value reproduced, matches reference
- [ ] ≥2 web sources logged with `Verifies:` lines
- [ ] article centers on one annotated diagram + ≥1 gotcha
- [ ] HyperFrames video renders, 6 beats, gold-check badge green
- [ ] all 6 probe questions answered out loud, no notes

## 3.11 vLLM basics

> Serve a model to *many* users at once. After this, "production serving" stops
> being one user's laptop and becomes *one command* — `vllm serve <model>` — that
> mounts an **OpenAI-compatible API** behind which **PagedAttention + continuous
> batching** turn Ollama's flat sequential line into a throughput curve that
> *grows* with concurrency. This unit is the *what/why*; the *how* of the two
> techniques is 3.12 (continuous batching) and 3.13 (paged attention). It rests
> directly on **1.4** (decode is memory-bandwidth-bound — that is the deep reason
> batching wins at all) and **3.6** (VRAM is the shared pool that bounds the
> batch). Concept + deploy-it; *tuning* the scheduler is 3.12–3.15.
> **Prerequisites:** 1.4, 3.6. **Pays off in:** production serving, 3.12–3.15.
> **Video:** `T2`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `local-llm/VLLM_SERVING.md` (§0 the two-engine TL;DR and the headline speedup table; §1 the single→paged→continuous→vLLM lineage; §4 the two throughput curves + the GOLD) · `local-llm/vllm_serving.py` (Section D = the `vllm_total(N) = 900·N/(N+10)` saturating curve and the `llama.cpp total(N) = 60` flat line; `section_gold()` = the 7.5x / 750 tok/s reference) — re-run it, reproduce the GOLD, then **extend it** with a config-builder + throughput estimator (Step 4). Read `local-llm/OLLAMA_LMSTUDIO.md` §0 for the *contrast*: Ollama/LM Studio wrap llama.cpp for **one** user; vLLM is the opposite design point — **many** users, an API.
- **Canonical:** vLLM blog, *Easy, Fast, and Cheap LLM Serving with PagedAttention* (blog.vllm.ai) · vLLM README (github.com/vllm-project/vllm) · Anyscale, *How continuous batching enables 23x throughput in LLM inference* (2023) · Red Hat, *vLLM or llama.cpp: Choosing the right LLM inference engine* (2025). *(The vLLM docs at docs.vllm.ai were rate-limited (HTTP 429) during verification — same failure as the 1.4 metrics fetch; facts below are confirmed from the four sources above, never invented.)*
- **Goal:** state, from memory, *what* vLLM is, *when* you reach for it vs Ollama, name the **two** techniques that make it fast under concurrency (and defer their mechanism to 3.12/3.13), deploy it with `vllm serve`, and explain why **throughput** (tokens/sec across many requests) is the metric vLLM optimizes — not single-request latency.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **vLLM is a high-throughput LLM *serving* engine — it serves a model behind an OpenAI-compatible API so any app built for OpenAI points at it unchanged.** It is "a fast and easy-to-use library for LLM inference and serving," originally from UC Berkeley, and its serving path is explicitly an "OpenAI-compatible API server." `Verifies:` vLLM README — "vLLM is a fast and easy-to-use library for LLM inference and serving," and under flexibility "OpenAI-compatible API server" (https://github.com/vllm-project/vllm) + vLLM launch blog — "To use vLLM for online serving, you can start an OpenAI API-compatible server … You can query the server with the same format as OpenAI API" (https://blog.vllm.ai/2023/06/20/vllm.html).
- **The OpenAI-compatible server means the cloud→local-vLLM swap is one `base_url` — nothing else.** Because vLLM exposes OpenAI's `/v1/chat/completions` (and `/v1/completions`) request/response shape, the *same* client code that calls OpenAI reaches your vLLM box by repointing one URL — the exact property 3.1 found in Ollama/LM Studio, now at production scale. `Verifies:` vLLM launch blog — serves over the OpenAI format; example `curl http://localhost:8000/v1/completions … "the same format as OpenAI API"` (https://blog.vllm.ai/2023/06/20/vllm.html) + vLLM README — "OpenAI-compatible API server" (https://github.com/vllm-project/vllm). *(The OpenAI Chat Completions API at platform.openai.com/docs/api-reference/chat is the canonical shape vLLM complies with.)*
- **vLLM is dramatically faster than Ollama/llama.cpp *under concurrent load* — because its throughput grows with concurrency while llama.cpp's is flat.** At peak concurrency Red Hat measured vLLM at "more than 35 times the request throughput (RPS) and more than 44 times the total output tokens per second (TPS) compared to llama.cpp," while "llama.cpp's throughput remains almost perfectly flat." `Verifies:` Red Hat *vLLM or llama.cpp* — "vLLM's throughput scales impressively as concurrency increases … llama.cpp's throughput remains almost perfectly flat"; "vLLM delivered more than 35 times the request throughput (RPS) and more than 44 times the total output tokens per second (TPS)" (https://developers.redhat.com/articles/2025/09/30/vllm-or-llamacpp-choosing-right-llm-inference-engine-your-use-case) + Anyscale *continuous batching* — "Up to 23x throughput improvement using continuous batching … (using vLLM)" (https://www.anyscale.com/blog/continuous-batching-llm-inference). *(The local bundle's pinned, simulated numbers — 7.5x per-user at 10 users, 12.5x at 50 — are the same effect on a single RTX 4090.)*
- **Two named techniques drive the win — PagedAttention and continuous batching (the *how* is 3.12/3.13, teased here).** (1) **PagedAttention** stores the KV cache in fixed-size non-contiguous **blocks** (like OS virtual-memory pages) instead of one contiguous buffer per request; existing systems "waste 60% – 80% of memory," paging cuts it "under 4%," so far more concurrent requests fit on the same GPU. (2) **Continuous batching** (iteration-level scheduling) admits a queued request into the running batch the very next iteration a slot frees — no head-of-line blocking. `Verifies:` vLLM launch blog — "we introduce PagedAttention … existing systems waste 60% – 80% of memory due to fragmentation and over-reservation … waste of under 4%" (https://blog.vllm.ai/2023/06/20/vllm.html) + Anyscale *continuous batching* — "iteration-level scheduling where the batch size is determined per iteration … once a sequence … has completed generation, a new sequence can be inserted in its place, yielding higher GPU utilization" (https://www.anyscale.com/blog/continuous-batching-llm-inference). *(The vLLM README lists both as the headline features: "Efficient management of attention key and value memory with PagedAttention" + "Continuous batching of incoming requests.")*
- **The deep reason batching wins at all is 1.4 — decode is memory-bandwidth-bound.** The dominant cost of a decode pass is streaming the weights from VRAM once, and that cost is *~the same* whether the batch holds 1 sequence or 32 (the extra activations are negligible). So running N sequences together produces ~N× the tokens for barely-more time — *until* the memory bus saturates. That is why vLLM's curve *grows then flattens* at the bandwidth ceiling, and why llama.cpp (one request at a time, batch ≈ 1) is *flat* regardless of how many users queue. `Verifies:` Anyscale *continuous batching* — "LLM inference is memory-IO bound, not compute bound … throughput is largely determined by how large a batch you can fit into high-bandwidth GPU memory" (https://www.anyscale.com/blog/continuous-batching-llm-inference) + the **1.4 carry** — decode "incurs a similar level of I/O to the prefill phase, making it constrained by the GPU's memory bandwidth" (DistServe §2.1, https://arxiv.org/html/2401.09670v3; carried from 1.4).
- **The metric vLLM optimizes is *throughput* (tokens/sec across many requests), not single-request latency — and the two trade off.** To handle many requests at once vLLM builds large batches, which *slightly* raises the time to compute any single next token; Red Hat calls this "a classic throughput-latency trade-off." Ollama/llama.cpp, by contrast, optimize single-stream latency and stay flat. `Verifies:` Red Hat *vLLM or llama.cpp* — "vLLM's ITL, while still very fast, increases with concurrency. This is a classic throughput-latency trade-off: to handle many requests at once, vLLM creates large batches, which slightly increases the time to compute the next token for any single request" (https://developers.redhat.com/articles/2025/09/30/vllm-or-llamacpp-choosing-right-llm-inference-engine-your-use-case) + Anyscale *continuous batching* — "Live-inference endpoints often face latency-throughput tradeoffs that must be optimized based on user needs" (https://www.anyscale.com/blog/continuous-batching-llm-inference). *(Per-user tok/s still degrades *gently* under vLLM — 15 tok/s even at 50 users in the bundle — vs llama.cpp's hard 1/N collapse.)*
- **Deploy = `vllm serve <model>` — and it needs a CUDA GPU.** `vllm serve` is the current CLI entry point that boots the OpenAI-compatible server; the three flags an operator tunes are `--tensor-parallel-size` (GPUs to shard across), `--max-model-len` (cap each request's KV), and `--gpu-memory-utilization` (VRAM fraction → block-pool size). vLLM is GPU-bound: every cited benchmark runs on NVIDIA CUDA GPUs. `Verifies:` vLLM launch blog — online serving is started as "an OpenAI API-compatible server," run on "an NVIDIA A10G GPU" / "an NVIDIA A100 GPU" (https://blog.vllm.ai/2023/06/20/vllm.html) + Red Hat *vLLM or llama.cpp* — benchmarked "Single NVIDIA H200-PCIe-141GB GPU … CUDA version: 12.8" (https://developers.redhat.com/articles/2025/09/30/vllm-or-llamacpp-choosing-right-llm-inference-engine-your-use-case). *(Flagged: the precise `vllm serve` CLI is the current documented entry per the vLLM docs, which returned HTTP 429 during verification; the 2023 launch blog used the longer `python -m vllm.entrypoints.openai.api_server` form. The local bundle cites docs.vllm.ai for the `vllm serve` invocation.)*
- **The crossover: below ~4–5 concurrent users it barely matters; above it, there is no contest.** vLLM is comparable to llama.cpp at concurrency 1 (both near the batch-1 decode rate) and pulls away hard as users pile up — so vLLM's VRAM overhead and startup cost only *pay* once you serve many users. `Verifies:` Red Hat *vLLM or llama.cpp* — at "a concurrency of 1" the two are comparable, but "As the load increases, vLLM's total generative power rapidly exceeds llama.cpp's"; "vLLM is unequivocally the superior choice for production deployment in a multi-user environment" (https://developers.redhat.com/articles/2025/09/30/vllm-or-llamacpp-choosing-right-llm-inference-engine-your-use-case) + local `local-llm/VLLM_SERVING.md` §5 — the crossover sits around ~4–5 concurrent users (community + Red Hat benchmark). *(Flagged: the exact "~4–5 user" threshold is a widely-used practitioner heuristic, not a single canonical citation — the *trend* (comparable at 1, dominant at scale) is fully verified above.)*

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. What is vLLM, and *when* do you reach for it vs Ollama/llama.cpp? (the decision collapses to one variable — name it)
2. vLLM serves an OpenAI-compatible API — so what does turning a cloud model into a vLLM-backed one actually change in your client code? (tie → 3.1)
3. Why is vLLM faster *under concurrent load* — name the two techniques, and where is their *mechanism* covered? (name only; tease → 3.12 continuous batching, 3.13 paged attention)
4. What is the *deep* reason batching wins at all — why does packing N sequences into one decode pass cost barely more than 1? (tie → 1.4: decode is memory-bandwidth-bound)
5. Throughput vs latency: which one does vLLM optimize, why does it trade the other away, and why does llama.cpp stay *flat* while vLLM's curve *grows then flattens*?
6. Write the `vllm serve` deploy command from memory, name the three capacity/throughput flags, and state the one hardware requirement (and why no Mac/CPU).

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `vllm_basics.py`. **Determinism:** the model string, every flag, and the two throughput constants (`T1 = 60.0`, `T_SAT = 900.0`, `N_HALF = 10.0`) are pinned; there is **no `random`/`Date.now()`/network** — the "throughput" is computed from the identical saturating-curve formula the local bundle uses, so output is byte-stable on re-run. (A live vLLM server is non-deterministic and hardware-dependent, so the gold is *defined* by the pinned formulas, exactly like the recorded-response pattern in 2.1.)
- **(a) A server-launcher config builder.** Take a pinned config dict and render/validate the `vllm serve` command:
  ```python
  CONFIG = {
    "model": "meta-llama/Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct",
    "port": 8000,
    "tensor_parallel_size": 1,
    "max_model_len": 8192,
    "gpu_memory_utilization": 0.9,
    "quantization": None,            # None | "awq" | "gptq" | "fp8" ...
  }
  def render_serve(cfg):
      parts = ["vllm", "serve", cfg["model"],
               f"--tensor-parallel-size {cfg['tensor_parallel_size']}",
               f"--max-model-len {cfg['max_model_len']}",
               f"--gpu-memory-utilization {cfg['gpu_memory_utilization']}"]
      if cfg["quantization"]:
          parts.append(f"--quantization {cfg['quantization']}")
      return " ".join(parts)
  def is_valid(cfg):
      return (bool(cfg["model"])
              and 0.0 < cfg["gpu_memory_utilization"] < 1.0
              and cfg["max_model_len"] > 0)
  ```
- **(b) A throughput estimator** — the two curves from the bundle, evaluated for a pinned load (N = 1, 10, 50 concurrent users):
  ```python
  LLAMACPP_T1 = 60.0      # tok/s, FLAT (one request served at a time)
  VLLM_T_SAT  = 900.0     # tok/s, the memory-bandwidth ceiling
  VLLM_N_HALF = 10.0      # concurrency at which vLLM total = T_SAT / 2

  def llamacpp_per_user(n): return LLAMACPP_T1 / n          # 1/N collapse
  def vllm_total(n):        return VLLM_T_SAT * n / (n + VLLM_N_HALF)  # saturating
  def vllm_per_user(n):     return VLLM_T_SAT / (n + VLLM_N_HALF)
  ```
- **Gold value (must match on re-run):** rendered serve command **`vllm serve meta-llama/Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct --tensor-parallel-size 1 --max-model-len 8192 --gpu-memory-utilization 0.9`** (`quantization=None` ⇒ no `--quantization` flag emitted) · @10 users **llama.cpp `6.0` tok/s/user vs vLLM `45.0` tok/s/user → `7.5x`** · @50 users **vLLM `15.0` tok/s/user, total `750` tok/s** · vLLM total grows with N (`450 → 750`) while llama.cpp stays pinned at `60`.
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] serve command valid: model set + util in [0,1) + max-model-len>0` · `[check] throughput-with-batching (45.0) > serial (6.0) @10 users` · `[check] vLLM total grows w/ N (450->750) while llama.cpp flat at 60` · `[check] quantization=None emits no --quantization flag`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** (i) flip `quantization = "awq"` and confirm the rendered command gains `--quantization awq` (and stays valid); (ii) add a 4th knob `--max-num-seqs` and explain it caps the running batch = the concurrency vLLM will pack (the bridge to 3.12); (iii) recompute the curve on a *weaker* GPU by lowering `VLLM_T_SAT` to `450` (half the bandwidth) and re-print the 10-user speedup — it drops from `7.5x` because the bus saturates sooner, the visible cost of a smaller GPU, and the seed for **3.6** (the VRAM/bandwidth budget sets the ceiling).

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *vLLM in 10 minutes (serve a model to many users).*
- **Angle:** when one user becomes many, Ollama stalls — vLLM is the throughput engine. One command mounts an OpenAI-compatible API; behind it, PagedAttention + continuous batching (the *how* is 3.12/3.13) turn the flat line into a curve that *grows* with concurrency. The whole decision is one variable: how many users?
- **Payload:** what vLLM is + when you reach for it vs Ollama (the ~4–5 user crossover) · the OpenAI-compatible serve (cloud→vLLM is one `base_url`) · the two techniques, *named* only (PagedAttention kills the 60–80% KV-cache waste; continuous batching kills head-of-line blocking) · why batching wins at all (→ 1.4, decode is memory-bandwidth-bound) · throughput vs latency (vLLM optimizes aggregate tok/s, trading a sliver of single-request latency; llama.cpp optimizes single-stream latency and stays flat) · `vllm serve` + the three capacity flags + the CUDA requirement.
- **Gotcha:** vLLM **needs a CUDA GPU** (VRAM is the shared pool the batch lives in — no Mac/CPU path; on Apple Silicon use MLX/Ollama per 3.2/3.1), and it optimizes **throughput, not single-request latency** — at low concurrency it is barely faster than llama.cpp, so deploying it for a 2-user dev box is pure overhead.
- **Video (`T2`):** Hook("one user becomes many → Ollama stalls; vLLM is the throughput engine") → Mechanism(`vllm serve` → OpenAI API → batch N sequences into one memory-bound decode pass) → Gold(7.5x at 10 users; vLLM total 450→750 while llama.cpp flat at 60) → Gotcha(needs CUDA VRAM; throughput-not-latency, crossover ~4–5 users).

## 3.12 Continuous batching

> Why batching is free throughput. After this, "the GPU is idle while one slow
> request finishes" stops being an unavoidable tax and becomes a scheduling choice:
> shuffle requests in and out every single token step instead of waiting for the
> slowest. Continuous batching (Orca's *iteration-level scheduling*) is half of
> why vLLM is fast — the other half is paged attention (3.13).
> **Prerequisites:** 1.4 (prefill vs decode), 3.11 (vLLM basics). **Pays off in:**
> serving throughput, 3.13 (paged attention). **Video:** `T2`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `llm/SCHEDULER.md` §0–§1 (the static-vs-continuous lineage; the bus analogy; the 75%→92% toy) · `llm/SCHEDULER.md` §3 (what `schedule()` does each iteration — prefill priority, token budget) · `llm/BLOCK_MANAGER.md` §0 + §6 (intuition only: why on-demand page allocation and the `can_append` page-boundary rule are the memory half that makes continuous batching's full win possible).
- **Canonical:** Anyscale, *How continuous batching enables 23× throughput in LLM inference* (Daniel, Shen, Liang, Liaw, 2023) · vLLM launch blog, *Easy, Fast, and Cheap LLM Serving with PagedAttention* (Kwon et al., 2023) · Orca (Yu et al., OSDI 2022) — the paper that introduced iteration-level scheduling.
- **Goal:** state, from memory, why static batching leaves the GPU idle, what the scheduler does at every iteration in a continuous-batching system, why decode being one-token-per-step (1.4) is *exactly* what makes per-step join/leave possible, and why the full throughput win needs a pageable KV cache (→ 3.13).

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **Static (request-level) batching forms a fixed batch and runs it to completion before admitting anyone new — finished requests stall, their GPU slots padded idle, while the batch waits on its slowest/longest member.** Because the batch size is frozen at request admission, a request that emits EOS early cannot return to the client and cannot hand its slot to a waiting request; the GPU does dummy/padded work in that slot until the longest sequence in the batch finishes. `Verifies:` Anyscale blog — "We call this traditional approach to batching *static batching*, because the size of the batch remains constant until the inference is complete… the GPU will be underutilized until the last sequence in the batch finishes generation" (https://www.anyscale.com/blog/continuous-batching-llm-inference) + Orca (Yu et al., OSDI 2022) — "requests that have finished earlier than other requests in a batch cannot return to the client, while newly arrived requests have to wait until the current batch completely finishes" (https://www.usenix.org/conference/osdi22/presentation/yu, quoted verbatim in `llm/SCHEDULER.md` Sources).
- **Continuous (iteration-level) batching schedules at the granularity of one iteration — one token step — not one request: after every step, finished requests leave and waiting requests join, so the batch is re-formed each iteration.** Orca's core idea is to move the scheduling unit from the *request* (run N sequences, then stop) to the *iteration* (run one forward pass on whoever is active, then re-decide). A request finishing mid-batch frees its slot *that same step*, and a new request takes it immediately. `Verifies:` Anyscale blog — "Orca implements *iteration-level* scheduling where the batch size is determined per iteration. The result is that once a sequence in a batch has completed generation, a new sequence can be inserted in its place, yielding higher GPU utilization than static batching" (https://www.anyscale.com/blog/continuous-batching-llm-inference) + Orca (Yu et al., OSDI 2022) — "we propose **iteration-level scheduling**, a new scheduling mechanism that schedules execution at the **granularity of iteration** (instead of request) where the scheduler invokes the execution engine to run only a single iteration of the model on the batch" (https://www.usenix.org/conference/osdi22/presentation/yu, quoted verbatim in `llm/SCHEDULER.md` Sources).
- **This works precisely because decode is one-token-per-step (tie 1.4) — every active request advances by exactly one token each iteration, so a request reaching EOS or `max_tokens` is detectable at a clean iteration boundary and its slot is reclaimable the very next step.** There is no partial token, no mid-matmul finish: the scheduler inspects each sequence's length after the forward pass, removes the finished ones, and fills the freed seats before the next pass. (Prefill, which processes `L>1` tokens, is a different, longer step — and is why vLLM separates prefill and decode per iteration; see 3.11.) `Verifies:` Anyscale blog — "You get one additional completion token for each new forward pass of the model" and "once a sequence in a batch has completed generation, a new sequence can be inserted in its place" (https://www.anyscale.com/blog/continuous-batching-llm-inference) + vLLM paper (Kwon et al., SOSP 2023) — "at each iteration, completed requests are removed from the batch, and new ones are added. Therefore, a new request can be processed after waiting for a single iteration, not waiting for the entire batch to complete" (arXiv:2309.06180, https://arxiv.org/abs/2309.06180, quoted in `llm/SCHEDULER.md` Sources).
- **The throughput gain is large and well-measured: continuous batching alone delivers ~8× over naive static batching, and with vLLM's memory optimizations the combined system hits 23×.** The static regime gets catastrophically worse as request-length variance grows (a chatbot workload), because every batch pays the cost of its longest member; continuous batching keeps the GPU saturated regardless. `Verifies:` Anyscale blog — "8x throughput over naive batching by using continuous batching (both on Ray Serve and Hugging Face's text-generation-inference)" and "Up to 23x throughput improvement using continuous batching and continuous batching-specific memory optimizations (using vLLM)" (https://www.anyscale.com/blog/continuous-batching-llm-inference) + vLLM blog — "vLLM equipped with PagedAttention… delivers up to 24x higher throughput than HuggingFace Transformers" (https://blog.vllm.ai/2023/06/20/vllm.html). *(Flagged: the "5–10× vs static" practitioner shorthand for continuous batching *alone* is the Anyscale 8× figure rounded; the 23–24× figure is the full vLLM stack — continuous batching + PagedAttention together — and should not be attributed to batching alone.)*
- **Continuous batching is half of why vLLM is fast — the other half is paged attention (3.13).** Continuous batching (Orca's scheduling innovation) keeps every decode seat full; PagedAttention (vLLM's memory innovation) lets the system *fit* many more concurrent seats by allocating KV cache on-demand in non-contiguous blocks instead of reserving a contiguous max-length buffer per request. Without paged KV, each request still hoards a `max_context_length`-sized buffer, so the batch size is bounded by pre-reserved memory and the continuous-batching win is capped. `Verifies:` Anyscale blog — "buffer allocation can happen just-in-time instead of ahead-of-time: when starting a new generation, the framework does not need to allocate a contiguous buffer of size maximum_context_length… vLLM's ability to reserve space dynamically instead of ahead-of-time allows vLLM to dramatically increase the batch size" (https://www.anyscale.com/blog/continuous-batching-llm-inference) + vLLM blog — "PagedAttention allows storing continuous keys and values in non-contiguous memory space… The physical blocks are allocated on demand as new tokens are generated… It allows the system to batch more sequences together, increase GPU utilization, and thereby significantly increase the throughput" (https://blog.vllm.ai/2023/06/20/vllm.html).
- **Existing systems waste 60–80% of KV-cache memory to fragmentation and over-reservation — the exact waste that paged allocation eliminates and that caps static batch sizes.** This is the number that motivates tying continuous batching (scheduling) to paged attention (memory): you can schedule perfectly per-iteration, but if each seat pre-reserves max-length KV, you cannot fill enough seats. `Verifies:` vLLM blog — "We find that existing systems waste **60% – 80%** of memory due to fragmentation and over-reservation" (https://blog.vllm.ai/2023/06/20/vllm.html) + Anyscale blog — "vLLM's ability to reserve space dynamically instead of ahead-of-time allows vLLM to dramatically increase the batch size" and "These memory savings translate directly into a higher batch size, which means higher throughput" (https://www.anyscale.com/blog/continuous-batching-llm-inference).

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. What is the difference between **static (request-level) batching** and **continuous (iteration-level) batching** — at what granularity does each one schedule?
2. Why does letting requests **join/leave at every token step** boost throughput — what is the GPU doing in the static case that it isn't in the continuous case?
3. Why is decode being **one token per step** (1.4) the *exact* property that makes per-step join/leave clean — what would break if a request finished mid-matmul?
4. Walk through what the **scheduler does each iteration**: who runs, who leaves, who joins, and what is the GPU doing the whole time?
5. Static batching wastes GPU cycles when request lengths vary — where exactly does the waste come from, and why is a chatbot workload the worst case?
6. Why does continuous batching's full throughput benefit **require a pageable KV cache** — what limits the batch size if each request reserves a fixed max-length buffer up front? (tease → 3.13)

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `continuous_batching.py`. A tiny **discrete-event simulation** of a decode loop: `N` requests each need a random number of output tokens; the GPU processes at most `B` sequences per step (one token each, tie 1.4). **Determinism:** all request lengths come from a fixed-seed `mulberry32` LCG (no `random`/`Date.now()`/network); output is byte-stable on re-run.
- **Two policies on the same workload:**
  - **Static (request-level):** form batches of `B` from the queue, run each batch for `max(lengths_in_batch)` steps (short requests pad idle), then start the next batch — GPU processes one batch at a time.
  - **Continuous (iteration-level):** at each step, all active requests generate one token; when a request finishes it is removed and the next queued request takes its slot *before the next step* — seats stay full while work remains.
- **Workload A (ties `SCHEDULER.md` §1 lineage):** `B=2`, two requests `R0=3, R1=6` tokens. Static: `6` steps, `9/12` slots useful (**75%**). Continuous with a third request `R2=2` arriving to fill `R0`'s freed seat: still `6` steps but `11/12` useful (**92%**) — same makespan, more useful work per step.
- **Workload B (GOLD, must match on re-run):** `B=4`, `N=12`, `mulberry32(seed=66)` → lengths = `[8, 3, 11, 30, 25, 35, 4, 3, 23, 13, 3, 3]`, total useful work = `161` tokens.
  - **Static makespan = `88` steps** (batch maxes `30 + 35 + 23`), idle = `191` padded token-steps, **utilization = `45.7%`**.
  - **Continuous makespan = `43` steps**, ramp-down idle = `11` token-steps, **utilization = `93.6%`**.
  - **Speedup = `88 / 43 = 2.05×`** (makespan); throughput `1.83 → 3.74` useful tok/step.
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] continuous makespan (43) < static makespan (88) → 2.05x faster` · `[check] no idle slots while work remains (cont util 93.6% >> static 45.7%)` · `[check] same useful work: 161 tokens in both (fair comparison)`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** (1) **Variance sweep** — re-run with lengths drawn from `[3, 40]` vs `[15, 25]` (low variance) and print the speedup curve: at zero variance static == continuous (no padding to reclaim); at high variance the gap widens — the whole effect is variance-driven. (2) **Steady arrival** — instead of burst (all queued at `t=0`), have requests arrive one per step (Poisson-ish) and show the continuous advantage *grows*: static must wait to fill a batch *and* wait for the prior batch, while continuous admits each request after a single iteration. (3) **The paged-KV cap (tease 3.13)** — add a `max_kv_blocks` budget; in the static policy each admitted request reserves `max_len` blocks (so batch size ≤ `budget / max_len`), while in continuous + paged each request grows one block at a time (batch size ≤ `budget / avg_len`) — print the batch-size ratio and show it is the *other* half of vLLM's 23×. *(The Anyscale benchmark's 8× is continuous batching alone; the jump to 23× is this memory effect — exactly what 3.13 covers.)*

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *Why batching is free throughput (continuous batching).*
- **Angle:** don't make requests wait for the slowest one — shuffle them in and out every token step. The whole story is one scheduling-granularity change (request → iteration) and one utilization number (45% → 94%) that you can simulate in 50 lines.
- **Payload:** static vs continuous batching (request-level vs iteration-level scheduling) · why decode being one-token-per-step (1.4) makes per-step join/leave clean · what the scheduler does each iteration (remove finished, fill freed seats, run one forward pass) · the throughput jump (2× makespan in the toy; ~8× over naive static in production; 23× with paged KV in vLLM) · the tie to paged KV (continuous batching keeps seats full; paged attention lets you *fit* enough seats).
- **Gotcha:** continuous batching's *full* throughput win requires the KV cache to be **pageable** — if each request pre-reserves a contiguous `max_context_length` buffer (the static-era default), the batch size is memory-bound and you cannot fill enough seats to matter. Existing systems waste 60–80% of KV memory this way. That pageable, on-demand KV is exactly what paged attention (3.13) provides — the two are co-designed halves of vLLM's speed.
- **Video (`T2`):** Hook("static batching makes the whole batch wait for its slowest request — the GPU runs half-empty") → Mechanism(per-step join/leave: finished request leaves, new one takes the seat, same iteration) → Gold(makespan `88 → 43` steps, utilization `45.7% → 93.6%`) → Gotcha(needs a pageable KV cache or the batch is memory-capped → 3.13).

- [ ] demo runs, exits 0, ≥2 `[check]` lines
- [ ] output byte-stable on re-run
- [ ] gold value reproduced, matches reference
- [ ] ≥2 web sources logged with `Verifies:` lines
- [ ] article centers on one annotated diagram + ≥1 gotcha
- [ ] HyperFrames video renders, 6 beats, gold-check badge green

## 3.13 Paged attention

> The trick that made vLLM famous. After this, "why is vLLM so fast?" stops being a
> mystery and becomes *one memory-management change* — the dense KV cache from 1.5
> reserves a giant contiguous slab per request (up to **93.75%** wasted), and
> PagedAttention replaces it with OS-style **virtual memory**: a pool of fixed-size
> pages, a per-request **block table**, and on-demand allocation from a **free list**.
> Fragmentation drops from ≤93.75% to **<1/page_size**, and that single change is
> what *unlocks* continuous batching (3.12) and prefix caching (3.14).
> **Prerequisites:** 1.4 (prefill vs decode), 1.5 (the dense KV cache), 3.12
> (continuous batching). **Pays off in:** understanding why vLLM is fast — and every
> downstream serving optimization. **Video:** `T1` ⭐.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `llm/PAGED_ATTENTION.md` (§0 the dense→paged lineage in one picture; §1 the 93.75% dense-waste example; §2 the page pool + free list; §3 the block table GOLD example; the glossary) · `llm/KV_CACHE.md` — re-read the **paged sections only** (§4 fragmentation math, §5 block table, §8 rewind); §0–§3 are 1.5's dense concept, now the thing paged *replaces* · `llm/paged_attention.py` (Sections A–G: dense waste → pool/free-list lifecycle → block table → append → rewind/release → indirect-gather kernel → the dense==paged invariant) · `llm/paged_attention.html` (live animation: allocate → write → release, block-table arrows, indirect K/V gather).
- **Canonical:** W. Kwon et al., *"Efficient Memory Management for Large Language Model Serving with PagedAttention,"* SOSP 2023 (arXiv:2309.06180) — the paper that introduced PagedAttention · vLLM launch blog, *Easy, Fast, and Cheap LLM Serving with PagedAttention* (Jun 2023) — the "blocks as pages, tokens as bytes, sequences as processes" one-liner + the 60–80%→<4% waste numbers · Anyscale, *How continuous batching enables 23× throughput* (2023) — the bridge between PagedAttention and continuous batching.
- **Goal:** state, from memory, *why* the dense KV cache wastes up to 93.75% per request, *how* PagedAttention borrows OS virtual memory (pages + block table + free list) to drop that to <1/page_size, and *why* that memory-management change is what makes continuous batching (3.12) and prefix caching (3.14) possible — and why it is a memory-MANAGEMENT change, not a faster attention *math*.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **The dense KV cache reserves a contiguous slab sized for `max_seq_len` PER request, up front — so a request that uses 512 of 8192 slots wastes 93.75%.** The dense cache from 1.5 pre-allocates `[B, H_kv, max_seq_len, d]` the moment a request arrives, sized for the *worst case*. If the request only generates 512 tokens, the other 7680 slots sit dead forever: `1 − 512/8192 = 0.9375 = 93.75%` wasted. Multiply by many concurrent requests and the dead VRAM caps your batch size — directly limiting throughput. `Verifies:` vLLM blog — "The KV cache is Large: Takes up to 1.7GB for a single sequence in LLaMA-13B… Dynamic: Its size depends on the sequence length, which is highly variable and unpredictable… We find that existing systems waste 60% – 80% of memory due to fragmentation and over-reservation" (https://vllm.ai/blog/2023-06-20-vllm) + RunPod — "The standard approach is to pre-allocate a contiguous memory region per request sized to `max_sequence_length`… GPU utilization at realistic mixed-length workloads can drop to the 20-40% range" (https://www.runpod.io/articles/guides/vllm-pagedattention-continuous-batching). *(The 93.75% figure itself is clean per-request arithmetic `1 − 512/8192`, computed and asserted in `paged_attention.py` Section A — it is distinct from vLLM's measured 60–80% system-wide aggregate. Both are correct; they measure different things: one-request arithmetic vs the aggregate across heterogeneous requests.)*
- **PagedAttention = OS virtual memory for the KV cache — "blocks as pages, tokens as bytes, sequences as processes."** The paper's central analogy: just as an OS maps a process's *logical* (virtual) pages to *non-contiguous physical* frames via a page table, PagedAttention maps each sequence's contiguous *logical blocks* to scattered *physical blocks* via a **block table**. The KV cache is no longer one private slab per request — it is a shared pool of fixed-size pages that any sequence can draw from. `Verifies:` vLLM blog — "we can manage the keys and values in a more flexible way as in OS's virtual memory: one can think of **blocks as pages, tokens as bytes, and sequences as processes**. The contiguous *logical blocks* of a sequence are mapped to non-contiguous *physical blocks* via a **block table**" (https://vllm.ai/blog/2023-06-20-vllm) + arXiv abstract — "we propose PagedAttention, an attention algorithm inspired by the **classical virtual memory and paging techniques** in operating systems" (https://arxiv.org/abs/2309.06180) + RunPod — "applies the core idea of OS virtual memory to KV cache management… each active sequence in vLLM addresses its KV cache through a **logical block table** that maps to **non-contiguous physical blocks**" (https://www.runpod.io/articles/guides/vllm-pagedattention-continuous-batching).
- **The pool is carved into fixed-size pages (vLLM default 16 tokens); physical blocks are allocated on demand from a free list.** A physical block holds the K,V tensors for a fixed number of token slots (vLLM: 16). When a sequence generates its 17th token, the kernel claims a *new* physical block from the pool's free list — it can live anywhere, regardless of where the previous block sits. `Verifies:` vLLM blog — "The physical blocks are **allocated on demand** as new tokens are generated… PagedAttention partitions the KV cache of each sequence into **blocks, each block containing the keys and values for a fixed number of tokens**" (https://vllm.ai/blog/2023-06-20-vllm) + RunPod — "A physical block in vLLM's KV cache pool holds the key and value tensors for **16 contiguous tokens (the default block size, configurable via `--block-size`)**. Blocks are **allocated on-demand** as tokens are generated… vLLM claims one physical block **from the free pool**" (https://www.runpod.io/articles/guides/vllm-pagedattention-continuous-batching) + vLLM docs — "if the **block size is 16** and the head size is 128, then for one head, one block…" (https://docs.vllm.ai/en/latest/design/paged_attention/).
- **Waste drops from ≤93.75% to <1/page_size (<4% in practice) — only the last partial page per request carries empty slots.** Because pages are allocated on demand, a sequence that uses N tokens gets exactly `ceil(N/page_size)` pages. Only the *last* page can be partial (at most `page_size − 1` empty slots). With page_size 16, worst-case per-sequence waste is 15 slots; system-wide across realistic workloads vLLM measured **<4%**. `Verifies:` vLLM blog — "memory waste only happens in the **last block** of a sequence. In practice, this results in near-optimal memory usage, with a mere waste of **under 4%**" (https://vllm.ai/blog/2023-06-20-vllm) + RunPod — "Fragmentation shrinks to a maximum of **15 wasted token slots per sequence** (the partially filled last block), dropping the measured memory waste to approximately **4%**, compared to the **60-80%** waste under contiguous pre-allocation" (https://www.runpod.io/articles/guides/vllm-pagedattention-continuous-batching) + arXiv abstract — vLLM achieves "**near-zero waste** in KV cache memory" (https://arxiv.org/abs/2309.06180). *(The <1/page_size bound is arithmetic: for page_size 16, 1/16 = 6.25%; for page_size 128, 1/128 = 0.78%. Computed in `paged_attention.py` Section A.)*
- **This memory-management change is what UNLOCKS continuous batching (3.12) and prefix caching (3.14).** Continuous batching needs to admit and evict requests at *every* iteration — impossible when each request owns a fixed-size pre-allocated slab you can't move or free mid-flight. PagedAttention's on-demand pages mean a finished request just returns its blocks to the free pool (instantly reusable by the next request), and a new request grabs blocks from the same pool with zero fragmentation. That is why vLLM "builds upon Orca's continuous batching design by taking full control of dynamic memory allocations." The same block-table indirection also enables **prefix caching**: two requests sharing a system prompt map their logical blocks to the *same* physical blocks (ref-counted, Copy-on-Write) — free deduplication. `Verifies:` Anyscale — "vLLM… **builds upon Orca's continuous batching design** by taking full control of dynamic memory allocations, allowing it to significantly reduce different forms of GPU memory fragmentation… **vLLM's ability to reserve space dynamically instead of ahead-of-time allows vLLM to dramatically increase the batch size**" (https://www.anyscale.com/blog/continuous-batching-llm-inference) + vLLM blog — "PagedAttention keeps track of the **reference counts** of the physical blocks and implements the **Copy-on-Write** mechanism… **memory sharing** greatly reduces the memory overhead" (https://vllm.ai/blog/2023-06-20-vllm) + RunPod — "vLLM's **prefix caching** stores KV blocks for shared prompt prefixes… across requests. When two requests share the first 512 tokens of a system prompt, vLLM **re-uses the pre-computed KV blocks**" (https://www.runpod.io/articles/guides/vllm-pagedattention-continuous-batching).
- **The result: 2–4× throughput over prior SOTA, with no model-architecture change.** By removing the dense-waste ceiling, more sequences fit in the same GPU → larger effective batch → higher throughput. The paper reports 2–4× over FasterTransformer and Orca; the launch blog reports up to 24× over vanilla HuggingFace Transformers (which had no memory management at all). `Verifies:` arXiv abstract — "vLLM improves the throughput of popular LLMs by **2-4×** with the same level of latency compared to the state-of-the-art systems, such as **FasterTransformer and Orca**. The improvement is more pronounced with longer sequences, larger models, and more complex decoding algorithms" (https://arxiv.org/abs/2309.06180) + vLLM blog — "vLLM achieves up to **24x** higher throughput compared to HF and up to **3.5x** higher throughput than TGI" (https://vllm.ai/blog/2023-06-20-vllm) + Anyscale — "Up to **23x** throughput improvement using continuous batching and continuous batching-specific memory optimizations (using vLLM)" (https://www.anyscale.com/blog/continuous-batching-llm-inference).
- **Gotcha — PagedAttention is a memory-MANAGEMENT change, NOT a faster attention *math*.** The paged attention kernel computes the exact same `softmax(Q·Kᵀ/√d)·V` as ordinary attention; it just *fetches* each K,V through the block table (`k_idx = ((page_id·H_kv + kv_head)·page_size + slot)·D + c`) instead of from one contiguous slab. The indirection is invisible to the math — `paged K == dense K == raw K` (proven in `paged_attention.py` Section G). The throughput wins come from *fitting more sequences per GPU* (memory efficiency), not from computing attention faster. `Verifies:` vLLM blog — "the PagedAttention kernel **identifies and fetches these blocks efficiently**" during attention computation — the blocks "do not need to be contiguous in memory" (https://vllm.ai/blog/2023-06-20-vllm) + RunPod — "vLLM ships a custom CUDA kernel that **gathers non-contiguous blocks via the block table** at each attention step… the memory savings from paging outweigh the gather cost" (https://www.runpod.io/articles/guides/vllm-pagedattention-continuous-batching) + local `llm/PAGED_ATTENTION.md` §6/§7 — the indirect-lookup formula produces byte-identical K,V to the dense path; "the indirection is invisible to the math — it only changes *where* the bytes live, never *what* they are."
- **Tie to 1.5 — the dense KV cache you learned there is EXACTLY what PagedAttention replaces.** In 1.5 the cache was one contiguous `[B, H_kv, max_seq_len, d]` slab, and the byte formula was `2 × n_layers × n_ctx × n_kv_heads × head_dim × bpe`. PagedAttention keeps *what* is stored (K,V per token per layer, never Q) and *how much* (the same linear formula) — it only changes *where* the bytes live (scattered pages from a shared pool vs one private slab) and *when* they're allocated (on demand vs up-front for `max_seq_len`). The unmanaged growth that 1.5 flagged as "a memory problem" is precisely the problem PagedAttention solves. `Verifies:` carried from 1.5 — the dense byte formula and the "grows forever → OOM → a memory problem" gotcha (Raschka + Brenndoerfer, https://sebastianraschka.com/faq/docs/kv-cache.html) + local `llm/PAGED_ATTENTION.md` §0 — "Dense KV caching made decoding `O(S)` per step… but it reserved a giant `[B, H_kv, max_seq_len, d]` slab per request **up front** — PagedAttention borrows the OS virtual-memory trick to kill that waste." *(The curriculum-level link — "1.5's dense slab = what 3.13 replaces" — is a synthesis grounded in the two bundles' shared invariant: `no-cache == dense == paged`, proven with RoPE in `kv_cache.py` Section E and without in `paged_attention.py` Section G.)*

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. The dense KV cache from 1.5 reserves memory per request sized for `max_seq_len` — if a request uses 512 of 8192 slots, what fraction is wasted (compute it), and why can't you *move or free* that dead slab mid-flight?
2. PagedAttention borrows OS virtual memory — state the analogy in one line ("blocks as pages, tokens as bytes, sequences as processes") and name its three load-bearing parts (the page pool, the block table, the free list). What does each map to in the OS?
3. How does paging drop fragmentation to <1/page_size — what is the *only* page that can be partial, and why is it bounded by `page_size − 1` empty slots?
4. Why does PagedAttention *unlock* continuous batching (3.12) — what can the scheduler do with on-demand paged blocks that it *cannot* do with fixed dense slabs?
5. How does the same block-table mechanism enable prefix caching (3.14) — what does "sharing a physical block by reference" mean, and what OS technique (ref counting + Copy-on-Write) keeps it safe?
6. PagedAttention is a memory-MANAGEMENT change, not a faster attention *math* — what stays identical (the `softmax(Q·K)·V` computation and the K,V bytes) and what changes (only *where* the bytes live and *when* they're allocated)? Why are the throughput wins a *memory-efficiency* win, not a *compute-speed* win?

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `paged_attention.py`. **Determinism:** every config is a pinned constant (`page_size`, `max_seq_len`, the batch of sequence lengths, the model shape); no `random`/`Math.random()`/`Date.now()`/network; output byte-stable on re-run.
- **Section A — the dense-waste problem (tie to 1.5).** Pin `max_seq_len = 8192`, `used = 512`. Compute `waste_fraction = 1 − used/max_seq_len = 1 − 512/8192 = 0.9375 = 93.75%` — the single-request worst case. Then print the worst-case internal-waste bound for paged: `1/page_size` at `page_size ∈ {16, 128}` → **6.25%, 0.78%**.
- **Section B — dense vs paged for a batch of variable-length sequences (the GOLD example).** Pin a batch of 6 sequences with *non*-page-aligned lengths (so paged waste is visible):
  ```
  seq_lens  = [513, 2050, 129, 4097, 1025, 257]
  max_seq_len = 8192
  page_size   = 16
  ```
  - **Dense:** each sequence reserves `max_seq_len = 8192` slots. Dense total = `6 × 8192 = 49152` slots; used = `8071`; wasted = `41081`; **waste = 83.58%**.
  - **Paged:** each sequence gets `ceil(len/16)` pages → `[33, 129, 9, 257, 65, 17]` = 510 pages = `8160` slots; used = `8071`; wasted = `89` (only the last partial page per sequence); **waste = 1.09%**.
  - Paged total `8160 < dense total 49152` → **6.02× less** memory. Paged waste `1.09% < 1/page_size 6.25%`.
- **Byte-level (LLaMA-7B shape: 32 layers, 32 KV heads, head_dim 128, fp16).** `bytes_per_token = 2 × 32 × 32 × 128 × 2 = 524288 B (512 KiB)`. Dense batch = `49152 × 524288 = 24.000 GiB`; paged batch = `8160 × 524288 = 3.984 GiB` → **20.016 GiB freed** (the dead slab is real VRAM you get back).
- **Gold value (must match on re-run):** dense batch total **49152** slots (83.58% waste) vs paged batch total **8160** slots (1.09% waste) — paged uses **6.02×** less; single-request worst case **1 − 512/8192 = 0.9375 (93.75%)**.
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] dense worst case: 1 - 512/8192 == 0.9375 (93.75%)` · `[check] paged batch waste 1.09% < 1/page_size 6.25%` · `[check] paged total 8160 < dense total 49152 (6.02x less)` · `[check] dense batch 24.000 GiB vs paged 3.984 GiB (20.016 GiB freed)`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** (1) Scale the batch to 100 concurrent requests (the vLLM-blog scenario) at `used=512` each: dense reserves `100 × 8192 = 819200` slots = **400.0 GiB**, paged reserves `100 × 32 = 3200` pages = **25.000 GiB** → **375 GiB of dead VRAM recovered** (93.75% of dense was waste) — the visible reason vLLM fits 2–4× more sequences per GPU. (2) Reproduce the existing Section C block-table GOLD (`A.page_ids = [0, 2]`, scattered, non-contiguous) and Section G invariant (`paged K == dense K == raw K`) — the proof that paging changes *where* bytes live, never *what* they are. (3) Swap `page_size` from 16 → 128 and re-print the waste table: paged waste drops further (1/128 = 0.78% bound) but the gather granularity coarsens — the page-size dial that 3.10 (KV-cache quant) and the kernel tiling must respect. *(Seeds 3.14 prefix caching — the same block-table + ref-counting that enables sharing.)*

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *The trick that made vLLM famous (PagedAttention).*
- **Angle:** the dense KV cache from 1.5 reserves memory like an OS without virtual memory — a giant fixed slab per request, up to **93.75%** dead. PagedAttention borrows the *one* OS idea that kills that waste: virtual memory (pages + a block table + a free list). One memory-management change, fragmentation drops from ≤93.75% to <1/page_size, and that is what *unlocks* everything downstream.
- **Payload:** the dense-waste problem (93.75% per request; 60–80% system-wide, per vLLM's measurement) · the OS-virtual-memory analogy ("blocks as pages, tokens as bytes, sequences as processes") · the three parts — the **page pool** (`[num_pages, H_kv, page_size, d]`, one shared slab), the **block table** (per-request logical→physical index card), the **free list** (on-demand `allocate()`/`free_page()`) · the waste bound (<1/page_size; only the last partial page) · what it unlocks — continuous batching (3.12: free/allocate pages at every iteration) and prefix caching (3.14: ref-counted shared blocks + Copy-on-Write) · the 2–4× throughput.
- **Gotcha:** PagedAttention is **not** a faster attention *math* — the kernel computes the exact same `softmax(Q·K)·V`, just gathering K,V through the block table instead of from one slab (`paged K == dense K`, proven byte-identical). The throughput wins are a *memory-efficiency* win (more sequences fit per GPU), not a *compute-speed* win. Confusing the two leads you to look for the speedup in the wrong place.
- **Video (`T1`, 6 beats):** Hook("the dense KV cache wastes up to 93.75% per request — that's why serving is slow") → Analogy(OS virtual memory: pages, page table, free-frame list — "blocks as pages, tokens as bytes, sequences as processes") → Mechanism(the page pool + block table + free list, animated: a request grabs pages on demand, storage scatters non-contiguously, the block table gathers it back) → Gold(dense 49152 slots / 83.58% waste vs paged 8160 slots / 1.09% waste — 6.02× less) → Gotcha(memory management, not math — same attention, same K,V bytes; the win is fitting more sequences) → Recap.

### 6. Checklist
- [ ] demo runs, exits 0, ≥2 `[check]` lines
- [ ] output byte-stable on re-run
- [ ] gold value reproduced, matches reference
- [ ] ≥2 web sources logged with `Verifies:` lines
- [ ] article centers on one annotated diagram + ≥1 gotcha
- [ ] HyperFrames video renders, 6 beats, gold-check badge green
- [ ] all 6 probe questions answered out loud, no notes
