## 3.14 Prefix cache / LMCache

> Don't recompute the system prompt. After this, "every request starts from
> scratch" stops being a law of physics and becomes *one optimization* — store the
> KV of the shared prefix, reuse it, skip its prefill. The serving-cost lever that
> ties prefill (1.4), the KV cache (1.5), and paged attention (3.13) into one move.
> **Prerequisites:** 1.4 (prefill cost), 1.5 (KV cache), 3.13 (paged attention). **Pays off in:** serving cost. **Video:** `T2`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `llm/PREFIX_CACHE.md` (§0 the three-generations lineage — naive per-request KV → flat block-aligned hash → radix tree; §4 insert/longest-prefix-match; the `9`-tokens-saved gold; the `[100,101,102]` backbone `ref_count=3`) · `llm/LMCACHE.md` (§0 the single-node → multi-node lineage; §4 the global chunk-hash index; §6 the latency budget `transfer ≪ prefill`; the `12.00 MiB` / `13.5×` gold) — **read both fully; this unit is their curriculum synthesis.**
- **Canonical:** vLLM, *Automatic Prefix Caching* (docs.vllm.ai/.../automatic_prefix_caching) + *Prefix caching design* doc · SGLang launch blog, *Fast and Expressive LLM Inference with RadixAttention* (lmsys.org/blog/2024-01-17-sglang) + Zheng et al., arXiv:2312.07104 · Liu et al., *LMCache*, arXiv:2510.09665.
- **Goal:** state, from memory, what a shared prefix is, why recomputing its prefill is waste, what prefix caching stores and reuses, how LMCache extends it across nodes, why paged attention makes the sharing clean, and the cost/TTFT win.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **Real workloads share a prefix — the system prompt, few-shot examples, tool schemas, or a long document queried repeatedly.** Chat apps re-send the same system prompt every turn; few-shot chains share N examples across queries; multi-turn conversations re-send the whole history; document-QA re-sends the doc with each new question. That shared leading run of tokens is *the prefix*, and it is identical across many requests. `Verifies:` SGLang blog — "KV cache reuse means different prompts with the same prefix can share the intermediate KV cache... Shareable parts include few-shot learning examples, questions in self-consistency, chat history in multi-turn chat, and search history in tree-of-thought" (https://lmsys.org/blog/2024-01-17-sglang/) + vLLM *Automatic Prefix Caching* — the two example workloads are "Long document query, where the user repeatedly queries the same long document... with different queries" and "Multi-round conversation, where the user may chat with the application multiple times... reusing the processing results of the chat history" (https://docs.vllm.ai/en/stable/features/automatic_prefix_caching.html).
- **Recomputing that prefix's prefill on every request is pure waste (tie 1.4).** Prefill is the compute-bound phase that processes the whole prompt at once (→ 1.4); if 100 requests all start with the same 1,000-token system prompt, the server re-runs those 1,000 tokens through every weight 100 times — `N × P` prefix-prefill token-computations — when the result (the KV) was already computed on the very first request. That redundancy scales with request count × prefix length, and it is the single biggest serving-cost waste on system-prompt-heavy workloads. `Verifies:` vLLM *Automatic Prefix Caching* — "instead of processing the long document again and again, APC allows vLLM to process this long document *only once*, and all future requests can avoid recomputing" (https://docs.vllm.ai/en/stable/features/automatic_prefix_caching.html) + OpenAI *Prompt caching* — "Model prompts often contain repetitive content, like system prompts and common instructions... making it cheaper and faster than processing a prompt from scratch" (https://platform.openai.com/docs/guides/prompt-caching). *(Prefill is compute-bound — carried from 1.4: DistServe §2.1, a 13B model prefilling 512 tokens saturates an A100.)*
- **Prefix caching stores the KV tensors of the shared prefix and reuses them — skipping the prefill for that span.** When a request finishes, its KV is *not* discarded; the server keeps the K,V tensors (→ 1.5) for the prefix keyed on the token sequence. The next request that shares that prefix looks it up, points its attention at the cached pages, and only prefills the genuinely-new suffix — the shared prefix's prefill is skipped entirely. So `N × P` prefix-prefills collapse to `1 × P` (computed once, cold) + `N × suffix` (each request's unique tail). `Verifies:` vLLM *Automatic Prefix Caching* — "Automatic Prefix Caching (APC) caches the KV cache of existing queries, so that a new query can directly reuse the KV cache if it shares the same prefix... allowing the new query to skip the computation of the shared part" (https://docs.vllm.ai/en/stable/features/automatic_prefix_caching.html) + OpenAI *Prompt caching* — "key/value tensors are the intermediate representation from the model's attention layers produced during prefill" are persisted and replayed; "the system uses the cached result. This significantly decreases latency and reduces costs" (https://platform.openai.com/docs/guides/prompt-caching).
- **The cache key is an EXACT prefix match — a one-token difference at the start voids it (and, under vLLM's flat hash, block-alignment rounds the reuse down).** Only an identical leading run of tokens, from position 0, is a hit; prepend a single different token (a timestamp, a per-user greeting) and the whole prefix misses. vLLM's flat chained-hash compounds this: it can only reuse *whole blocks* (`block_size` tokens), so a shared prefix whose length isn't a multiple of `block_size` has its trailing tokens trapped in a block whose remaining slots belong to the divergent suffix. SGLang's radix tree removes that rounding by sharing at token granularity. Either way: **put static content first, variable content last** (→ 2.15). `Verifies:` OpenAI *Prompt caching* — "Cache hits are only possible for *exact prefix matches* within a prompt. To realize caching benefits, place static content like instructions and examples at the beginning of your prompt, and put variable content, such as user-specific information, at the end" (https://platform.openai.com/docs/guides/prompt-caching) + vLLM *Prefix caching design* doc — vLLM achieves prefix caching "without the need of maintaining a tree structure among the KV blocks"; each block is "uniquely identified by the tokens within the block *and the tokens in the prefix before the block*" (the chained block hash that can only reuse whole blocks — the flat-hash baseline in `llm/PREFIX_CACHE.md` §1) + SGLang blog — "node b from (3) is *split* into two nodes to allow the two chat sessions to share the system prompt" (the radix-tree fork-point sharing the flat hash cannot express; https://lmsys.org/blog/2024-01-17-sglang/).
- **LMCache generalizes prefix caching across requests AND across nodes — turning every node's memory into one global, hierarchical pool.** Single-node prefix caching (vLLM APC / SGLang RadixAttention) reuses KV only on the *same* GPU; in a cluster, a repeat prompt routed to a cold node triggers a full recompute even though the KV already exists on a sibling. LMCache extends the cache off GPU VRAM into a tiered hierarchy (CPU DRAM → local NVMe → remote S3/Redis) with a global chunk-hash index queried across nodes — on a local miss it looks up where the KV lives cluster-wide and streams it over RDMA straight into the local block table. So "recompute because I'm on the wrong node" becomes "pull the pages and carry on." `Verifies:` LMCache paper (arXiv:2510.09665) — "the first and so far the most efficient open-source KV caching solution, which extracts and stores KV caches... out of the GPU memory and shares them *across engines and queries*... supports both cache offloading (prefix reuse across queries) and prefill-decode (PD) disaggregation (cross-engine/GPU cache transfer)... up to 15× improvement in throughput" (https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.09665) + LMCache GitHub README — "Persistent, tiered KV cache offloading and reuse: Move KV caches out of GPU memory into a tiered storage hierarchy spanning CPU memory, local storage, and remote backends, enabling reuse *across requests, sessions, and engine instances*"; "PD disaggregation and KV transfer: Support KV cache transfer from prefill workers to decode workers over NVLink, RDMA, or TCP" (https://github.com/LMCache/LMCache).
- **Paged attention (3.13) is what makes KV sharing clean — shared physical pages referenced by multiple block tables.** Because paged attention stores KV in non-contiguous pages indexed by a per-request block table (`logical page → physical page`), a cached prefix is simply a set of *physical pages* already filled with the right KV. A new request that shares the prefix just *references* those existing physical pages from its own block table (`ref_count++`) — no copy, no contiguous reservation. That is why vLLM can reuse whole blocks (the block table *is* the sharing mechanism) and SGLang can store KV "in a paged layout, where the size of each page is equivalent to one token." Without paging (→ 3.13's pre-paging fragmentation problem), sharing a prefix would require a contiguous slice that diverging requests couldn't both reference. `Verifies:` SGLang blog — "KV cache tensors are stored on the GPU in a *paged layout*, where the size of each page is equivalent to one token... RadixAttention is *compatible with* existing techniques like continuous batching and paged attention" (https://lmsys.org/blog/2024-01-17-sglang/) + vLLM *Automatic Prefix Caching* — the block-level reuse ("reuse the KV cache if it shares the same prefix") is built directly on vLLM's paged-attention block manager, where blocks are the unit of reference counting and sharing (https://docs.vllm.ai/en/stable/features/automatic_prefix_caching.html). *(The paged-attention block-table mechanism is carried from 3.13; the pre-paging fragmentation it solves is why sharing was hard before.)*
- **The payoff: huge TTFT and cost wins on system-prompt-heavy workloads (tie 2.15).** Prefix caching only cuts the *prefill* (TTFT) — it does nothing for decode (each request still generates its own tokens) — but on a workload where the prompt dominates, that is exactly the term that matters. Skipping the prefix prefill slashes TTFT and the GPU-seconds per request; providers bill cached input at a fraction of fresh input (OpenAI up to 90% off, Anthropic 0.1×), and open servers report up to 5× (SGLang) to 15× (LMCache) throughput on chat/few-shot/RAG workloads. `Verifies:` OpenAI *Prompt caching* — "Prompt Caching can reduce latency by up to 80% and input token costs by up to 90%"; "APC only reduces the time of processing the queries (the prefilling phase) and does not reduce the time of generating new tokens (the decoding phase)" (the decoding caveat is from vLLM, https://docs.vllm.ai/en/stable/features/automatic_prefix_caching.html; https://platform.openai.com/docs/guides/prompt-caching) + SGLang blog — "achieving up to 5 times higher throughput... particularly for the first token latency, where a prefix cache hit can be significantly beneficial" (https://lmsys.org/blog/2024-01-17-sglang/) + LMCache paper — "up to 15× improvement in throughput across workloads such as multi-round question answering and document analysis" (https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.09665). *(The cached-input pricing tie to 2.15: there the cost formula gained a cache term — `cached_in·price_read` ≈ 0.1× fresh input — here we see the server-side mechanism that makes it cheap.)*

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. What is a *shared prefix* — name three things in a real workload that are the same leading tokens across many requests. Why are they "leading"?
2. Why is recomputing the prefix's prefill on every request pure waste — which phase of inference (→ 1.4) is it, and how does the redundant work scale with request count × prefix length?
3. What exactly does prefix caching store and reuse, and which inference phase does it *skip*? Write the prefill-token counts without caching (`N×P`) vs with (`1×P + N×suffix`).
4. Why is the cache key an *exact* prefix match — and what does "put static first, variable last" (→ 2.15) have to do with it? What does vLLM's block-alignment add on top of that?
5. How does **LMCache** extend prefix caching beyond one node — what does "cross-request, cross-instance" mean, and why does a wrong-node hit force a recompute without it?
6. Why does **paged attention** (→ 3.13) make KV sharing clean — what is a cached prefix physically, and how do multiple block tables reference it without copying?

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `prefix_cache.py`. A pure prefill-cost calculator: given a pinned workload (N requests, shared prefix P tokens, unique suffix S tokens each), compute the prefill token-computations WITHOUT caching vs WITH prefix caching → the savings. **Determinism:** every value is a pinned constant; **no RNG, no `Date.now()`, no network**; output byte-stable on re-run.
- **The cost model (the whole job, two formulas):**
  - `prefill_no_cache = N * (P + S)` — every request prefills the full prompt (shared prefix + its suffix).
  - `prefill_cached   = P + N * S` — the shared prefix is prefilled *once* (cold miss on request 1); every request still prefills its own unique suffix. *(The first request pays `P + S`; requests 2..N pay only `S` each: `(P + S) + (N−1)·S = P + N·S`.)*
  - `savings = prefill_no_cache − prefill_cached = (N − 1) * P` — the prefix is computed `N` times without caching, `1` time with; that `(N−1)` redundant computations are what caching deletes.
- **Pinned workload (the gold):** `P = 1000` tokens (a system prompt + tool schemas), `N = 100` requests, `S = 50` tokens (the unique user query each).
- **Gold values (must match on re-run):**
  - **Without caching:** `100 × (1000 + 50) = 100 × 1050 =` **`105,000`** prefill token-computations.
  - **With caching:** `1000 + 100 × 50 = 1000 + 5000 =` **`6,000`** prefill token-computations.
  - **Savings:** `105,000 − 6,000 =` **`99,000`** prefill token-computations eliminated = **`(N−1)·P = 99 × 1000 = 99,000`** (**`94.3%`** of the uncached prefill).
  - **Prefix recompute count:** the shared 1,000-token prefix is prefilled **`100×`** without caching → **`1×`** with caching — a **`100×`** reduction on the prefix portion specifically.
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] cached prefill total (6000) < uncached prefill total (105000)` · `[check] savings == (N-1)*P == 99*1000 == 99000 (94.3% of uncached)` · `[check] savings grow with prefix length: P=500 -> 49500; P=1000 -> 99000 (2x P -> 2x savings)` · `[check] savings grow with request count: N=10 -> 9000; N=100 -> 99000 (each new req saves P=1000)`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** (1) **Exact-prefix-miss simulation** — inject a 1-token divergence at position 0 of the suffix (a per-user greeting before the shared system prompt) and show the cache hit collapses to `0` (the whole `P` must be re-prefilled: `prefill_cached = P + N*S` reverts to `N*(P+S)`). That single token voids the cache — the gotcha. (2) **LMCache cross-node reach** — pin a cluster of `K=4` nodes, assume the KV is cached on one node uniformly, and show local-only hit rate `≈ 1/K = 25%` vs LMCache global hit rate `≈ 100%` (a request on a cold node pulls the KV over RDMA instead of recomputing) — the cluster-scale amplification that single-node caching cannot capture. *(Teases 3.15 speculative decoding, which attacks the decode term prefix caching leaves untouched.)*

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *Don't recompute the system prompt (prefix caching).*
- **Angle:** if every request starts with the same 1,000 tokens, you're paying for that prefill 100×. Prefix caching stores the KV once and reuses it — `N×P` → `1×P` — and the whole mechanism (KV reuse, the radix/hash index, paged-attention sharing, LMCache's global pool) is one idea at increasing scale.
- **Payload:** the shared-prefix problem (system prompts, few-shot, tool schemas, document-QA — the leading tokens identical across requests) · what prefix caching stores and skips (the KV of the prefix → skip its prefill; `105,000 → 6,000` token-prefills, a 94.3% cut) · the exact-prefix-match requirement (static first, variable last; block-alignment rounding) · how LMCache extends it cross-node (global hierarchical pool: VRAM → DRAM → NVMe → S3; RDMA pull instead of recompute; local `~1/N` → global `~1` hit rate) · why paged attention (3.13) is the enabler (shared physical pages referenced by multiple block tables — no copy, no contiguous slice).
- **Gotcha:** prefix caching requires an **exact prefix match** (and block alignment) — a one-token difference at the start (a timestamp, a per-user name) voids the cache for the *entire* prefix. And the deeper gotcha: prefix caching only cuts **prefill** (TTFT), never **decode** — on a workload that generates long outputs, it barely dents total latency (→ 2.15); pair it with a decode-side win (→ 3.15) for the full picture.
- **Video (`T2`):** Hook("100 requests, same 1k-token system prompt → you pay for that prefill 100×") → Mechanism(cache the shared KV, reuse it via the block table; the radix-tree / flat-hash index) → Gold(`105,000 → 6,000` prefill token-computations; the prefix `100× → 1×`) → Gotcha(one token at the start voids the whole cache; and caching ≠ decode latency).

### 6. Checklist
- [ ] demo runs, exits 0, ≥2 `[check]` lines
- [ ] output byte-stable on re-run
- [ ] gold value reproduced, matches reference
- [ ] ≥2 web sources logged with `Verifies:` lines
- [ ] article centers on one annotated diagram + ≥1 gotcha
- [ ] HyperFrames video renders, 6 beats, gold-check badge green
- [ ] all 6 probe questions answered out loud, no notes

## 3.15 Speculative decoding

> Guessing several tokens at once — for free. After this, "make decode faster"
> stops being wishful thinking and becomes *one trick*: a tiny draft model
> guesses K tokens, the big model verifies all K in a **single** forward pass,
> and a rejection-sampling rule makes the output *mathematically identical* to
> the big model alone. This is the direct payoff of 1.4 (decode is
> memory-bound) — and the reason decode's wasted bandwidth can be turned into
> useful tokens with **zero quality loss**.
> **Prerequisites:** 1.4 (prefill vs decode), 1.6 (sampling), 3.11 (vLLM).
> **Pays off in:** latency reduction. **Video:** `T2`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `llm/SPECULATIVE_DECODING.md` (§0 the assembly-line analogy + the memory-wall table; §2 the draft→verify pipeline; §3 the accept condition `u ≤ min(1, p/q)` + the adjusted resample `max(0,p−q)/Z`; §4 the algebra that proves the output == target; §5 the speedup bound `S(α,K,γ)`) · `llm/speculative_decoding.py` (Sections A–H: the intensity table, the causal-mask verify, the rejection-sampling trace, the K=4 worked round) · `local-llm/SPECULATIVE_LOCAL.md` (§2a why batch(γ+1) ≈ batch(1); §4 the gold `γ=4, α=0.75 → 3.05 tokens/step, 2.43× net`; §5 the draft-model-selection pitfalls) · `llm/SAMPLING.md` (the softmax/categorical draw that feeds `q` and `p` — the draft is just a smaller version of the same pipeline).
- **Canonical:** Leviathan, Kalman & Matias, *Fast Inference from Transformers via Speculative Decoding*, ICML 2023 Oral (arXiv:2211.17192) · Chen et al. (DeepMind), *Accelerating Large Language Model Decoding with Speculative Sampling* (arXiv:2302.01318) · NVIDIA Technical Blog, *An Introduction to Speculative Decoding for Reducing Latency in AI Inference* · BentoML *LLM Inference Handbook → Speculative decoding*.
- **Goal:** state, from memory, *why* decode is the bottleneck spec decoding targets, the draft+verify dance, why verifying K tokens costs ~one forward pass, what happens on a mismatch (resample, tie 1.6), why the output is provably identical to the big model, and what makes a good draft model.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **Decode is the bottleneck spec decoding targets: autoregressive generation emits ONE token per step but must load essentially every weight to do it — so the GPU's compute sits idle waiting on memory bandwidth.** This is exactly the memory-bound decode of 1.4: a single forward pass re-reads the whole weight matrix for one token of math. `Verifies:` NVIDIA Technical Blog — "GPUs offer massive compute, yet much of that power sits idle because autoregressive generation is inherently sequential: each token requires a full forward pass, reloading weights, and synchronizing memory at every step. This combination of memory access and step-by-step dependency raises latency, underutilizes hardware" (https://developer.nvidia.com/blog/an-introduction-to-speculative-decoding-for-reducing-latency-in-ai-inference/) + BentoML *LLM Inference Handbook* — "LLM inference is memory-bound. GPUs have massive compute capacity but limited memory bandwidth. Much of their compute sits unused while waiting on memory access" (https://bentoml.com/llm/inference-optimization/speculative-decoding). *(Ties directly to 1.4's decode intensity ≈ 1 FLOP/byte.)*
- **Speculative decoding runs a small *draft model* that proposes K tokens quickly, then the *big model verifies all K in ONE forward pass*.** Two models: a small, fast draft proposes a short continuation (K tokens, typically 3–12), then the target processes the input + all K draft tokens together in a single batched forward pass. `Verifies:` Leviathan et al. 2023 — "we introduce speculative decoding - an algorithm to sample from autoregressive models faster without any changes to the outputs, by computing several tokens in parallel... by running them in parallel on the outputs of the approximation models, potentially generating several tokens concurrently" (arXiv:2211.17192, ICML 2023 Oral, https://arxiv.org/abs/2211.17192) + NVIDIA Technical Blog — "Speculative decoding is an inference optimization technique that pairs a target model with a lightweight draft mechanism that quickly proposes several next tokens. The target model verifies those proposals in a single forward pass" (https://developer.nvidia.com/blog/an-introduction-to-speculative-decoding-for-reducing-latency-in-ai-inference/).
- **Verifying K tokens in one parallel pass costs ≈ the price of one token's forward — so K serial decodes collapse into ~1 amortized pass.** Because decode is memory-bound, the dominant cost is loading the weights once; computing K positions instead of 1 multiplies the cheap compute but reuses the one expensive weight read. The latency of scoring K short continuations is comparable to sampling a single token from the target. `Verifies:` Chen et al. (DeepMind) 2023 — "Our algorithm relies on the observation that the latency of parallel scoring of short continuations, generated by a faster but less powerful draft model, is comparable to that of sampling a single token from the larger target model" (arXiv:2302.01318, https://arxiv.org/abs/2302.01318) + NVIDIA Technical Blog — "By using a fast draft mechanism to speculate two candidate tokens then verifying them all in a single 250 ms forward pass, the model can generate three tokens (two speculations plus one base model generation) in 250 ms versus 600 ms" (https://developer.nvidia.com/blog/an-introduction-to-speculative-decoding-for-reducing-latency-in-ai-inference/). *(The intensity ≈ K FLOP/byte figure is derived in `llm/SPECULATIVE_DECODING.md` §1 / `speculative_decoding.py` Section A, building on 1.4's intensity ≈ L.)*
- **The verifier accepts the longest prefix of draft tokens it agrees with, rejects the first mismatch, and resamples a corrected token there (rejection sampling).** For each draft position, compare the draft's distribution `q` and the target's `p`: accept while they agree, and at the first mismatch discard the rest and draw the corrected token from the target — the output distribution is then *exactly* the target's. (Tie 1.6: the corrected token is the same softmax/categorical draw the sampler already does.) `Verifies:` Leviathan et al. 2023 — the modified rejection-sampling scheme accepts/rejects per position "without changing the distribution" (arXiv:2211.17192, https://arxiv.org/abs/2211.17192) + BentoML *LLM Inference Handbook* — "The target model accepts the longest prefix of these K tokens that it agrees with. If it accepts h tokens, it then generates the (h+1)th token itself (so that generation remains on track)" (https://bentoml.com/llm/inference-optimization/speculative-decoding). *(The per-token accept rule `u ≤ min(1, p(t)/q(t))` and the adjusted resample `P_adj = max(0, p−q)/Z` are in `llm/SPECULATIVE_DECODING.md` §3.)*
- **Because the draft is usually right, you get K tokens for ~1 forward → lower latency, ZERO quality loss — the output is mathematically equivalent to sampling from the big model.** The rejection-and-resample step cancels the draft's bias exactly (`min(q,p) + max(0,p−q) == p`), so the final distribution equals the target's, not an approximation of it. This is *not* a quality/speed trade-off; it is a pure speedup. `Verifies:` Leviathan et al. 2023 — "an algorithm to sample from autoregressive models faster without any changes to the outputs... without changing the distribution... a 2X-3X acceleration compared to the standard T5X implementation, with identical outputs" (arXiv:2211.17192, https://arxiv.org/abs/2211.17192) + Chen et al. 2023 — "a novel modified rejection sampling scheme which preserves the distribution of the target model within hardware numerics... without compromising the sample quality or making modifications to the model" (arXiv:2302.01318, https://arxiv.org/abs/2302.01318). *(The algebra + a 5000-sample empirical proof are in `llm/SPECULATIVE_DECODING.md` §4.)*
- **A good draft model = high acceptance rate (α), and α is what drives the speedup.** The draft must be small (so its own K forwards are cheap) *and* closely match the target's distribution (so most draft tokens are accepted). Same-family drafts (e.g. a 0.5B/1B from the same model family) reach α ≈ 0.65–0.75; mismatched families drop to ≈ 0.4–0.5 and the speedup largely evaporates. A bad draft model gives few accepts → little gain — but still **no quality loss** (rejection sampling holds regardless). `Verifies:` BentoML *LLM Inference Handbook* — "How closely your draft model's distribution matches with the target model determines the acceptance rate... At α ≥ 0.6 and γ ≥ 5, speculative decoding achieved 2–3× speedups over baseline decoding" (https://bentoml.com/llm/inference-optimization/speculative-decoding) + NVIDIA Technical Blog — "The number of accepted tokens compared to the total generations is the acceptance rate. Higher acceptance rates equate to more significant speedups and at worst, if all draft tokens are rejected, then only the single target model token is generated" (https://developer.nvidia.com/blog/an-introduction-to-speculative-decoding-for-reducing-latency-in-ai-inference/). *(Published: Leviathan 2–3× on T5-XXL, Chen 2–2.5× on Chinchilla-70B — both with high α.)*

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. Why is **decode** the bottleneck speculative decoding targets — and how does this tie back to 1.4 (one token per huge weight-read)?
2. Walk the **draft+verify dance**: who proposes K tokens, who verifies them, and in how many forward passes?
3. Why does **verifying K tokens cost ≈ one forward pass** — what is it about decode being memory-bound that makes the parallel verify nearly free?
4. On the **first mismatch**, what happens — which tokens are kept, which are discarded, and where does the corrected token come from? (tie 1.6 sampling)
5. Why is the output **provably identical** to sampling from the big model — and why is this *not* a quality/speed trade-off?
6. What makes a **good draft model** — and what happens to the speedup (but *not* the quality) when the draft is bad?
7. How does this connect to 3.11 (vLLM) — why is speculative decoding a serving-layer optimization you switch on, not a model change?

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `speculative_decoding.py`. **Determinism:** every input is a pinned constant — the proposed draft tokens, the acceptance pattern, and the sampler's seed. No `random`/`Math.random()`/`Date.now()`/network; output byte-stable on re-run. (A tiny fixed-seed LCG backs the resample draw, though the gold pattern's resample is argmax-stable.)
- **Simulate one speculative round.** The draft model proposes `K = 4` tokens from a pinned sequence:
  `PINNED_DRAFT = ["The", "quick", "brown", "xyz"]` (ids `[464, 2068, 6281, 622]`-style stand-ins — use `DRAFT_IDS = [11, 42, 7, 99]`).
- The big model's **pinned acceptance pattern** is `[True, True, False, False]` — it accepts the first **A = 2** draft tokens and **rejects** the 3rd. The rejected position resamples a corrected token (pinned, argmax-stable → id `5`).
- **Compute effective tokens-per-forward** (tokens committed per *target* forward pass): accepted draft tokens `A` **+ 1** corrected/bonus token from the verify pass = `1 + A`.
- **Gold value (must match on re-run):** with `K = 4`, pinned accept `A = 2` → **effective tokens-per-forward = `1 + 2 = 3`**; naive decode commits `1` token per forward, so **speedup vs naive = `3 / 1 = 3.0×`** (in tokens per target forward). Committed tokens this round = `["The", "quick", "<corrected>"]` = 3.
- Print the **acceptance-rate sweep** (Leviathan's expected tokens/round, `E = (1 − α^{K+1}) / (1 − α)` at `K = 4`): `α = 0.3 → 1.4251`, `0.5 → 1.9375`, `0.7 → 2.7731`, `0.9 → 4.0951` — speedup grows monotonically with α.
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] effective tokens/forward == 3 (1+A, K=4 A=2) > naive 1` · `[check] speedup(α) monotonic increasing: 1.4251 < 1.9375 < 2.7731 < 4.0951` · `[check] committed tokens == 3 (2 accepted + 1 corrected)` · `[check] output distribution == target (rejection sampling, 0 quality loss)`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** account for the **draft model's own cost** to get the *net* speedup (the honest number). With draft/target size ratio `γ_draft` (e.g. 0.5B draft for an 8B target ⇒ `0.0625`) and the batch overhead `≈ 1.004` (from `local-llm/SPECULATIVE_LOCAL.md` §2a), the net speedup = `effective / (1 + K·γ_draft + batch_overhead)`. At `α = 0.75, K = 4, γ_draft = 0.0625`: expected tokens/step `= (1−0.75^5)/(1−0.75) = 3.0508`, net `= 3.0508 / (1 + 4·0.0625 + 0.004) ≈ 2.43×` — matches the bundle's measured gold and shows the *effective* 3.05 eroded toward the *net* 2.43 by the draft's own passes. Sweep `γ_draft ∈ {0.03125, 0.0625, 0.125, 0.25}` and watch the net speedup fall (2.70× → 2.43× → 2.03× → 1.52×) — the "good draft model" insight made quantitative.

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *Guessing two tokens at once (speculative decoding).*
- **Angle:** decode wastes the GPU's parallelism — one token per huge weight-read is the most expensive possible use of a forward pass. Let a small model *guess* K tokens, let the big model *check* all K in one pass (nearly free, because decode is memory-bound), and accept the ones it agrees with. Net: K tokens for ~1 forward, and the output is provably the big model's.
- **Payload:** the memory-bound premise (tie 1.4) · the draft→verify→accept/reject dance · why verifying K costs ~1 forward (intensity ≈ K) · the accept rule + resample-on-mismatch (tie 1.6) · the equivalence proof (output == target, 0 quality loss) · what makes a good draft (α drives the speedup).
- **Gotcha:** the speedup depends entirely on the draft model's accuracy — a bad draft (low α, or a cross-family draft) gives few accepts and little gain, but **still no quality loss** (rejection sampling holds regardless). And don't confuse *effective* tokens/forward (draft treated as free) with *net* speedup (after the draft's own passes erode it).
- **Video (`T2`):** Hook("one token per weight-read is wasteful — decode is memory-bound") → Mechanism(draft proposes K → target verifies all K in one pass → accept the prefix, resample the first mismatch) → Gold(effective tokens/forward = 3 at K=4, A=2; speedup 3.0× vs naive, growing with α) → Gotcha(needs a good draft model; bad draft = few accepts but still 0 quality loss).

### 6. Checklist
- [ ] demo runs, exits 0, ≥2 `[check]` lines
- [ ] output byte-stable on re-run (determinism)
- [ ] gold value reproduced, matches reference
- [ ] ≥2 web sources logged with `Verifies:` lines
- [ ] article is voiced + embeds the diagram/demo + ≥1 gotcha
- [ ] HyperFrames video renders, 6 beats, gold-check badge green
- [ ] **all probe questions answered out loud, no notes** ← the real bar

## 3.16 Advanced serving (CUDA graphs / disaggregated / ktransformers)

> A survey of three tricks for squeezing more out of serving — kept light. After
> this you *know they exist* and the one bottleneck each kills: launch overhead,
> prefill/decode interference, and the GPU-capacity wall for giant MoE. None of the
> three is a default — each is a reaction to a *profiled* bottleneck. **Optional**.
> **Prerequisites:** 1.4 (prefill vs decode), 3.11–3.13 (vLLM / continuous batching /
> paged attention). **Pays off in:** squeezing more out of serving. **Video:** `T3`.

### 1. Read
- **Your bundles:** `llm/CUDA_GRAPHS.md` (§0 the barista analogy; §2 the eager launch tax `72 × 10 µs = 720 µs`; §6 the `144×` launch gold + the batch-scaling insight) · `llm/DISAGGREGATED_SERVING.md` (§0 the two-kitchens lineage; §2 the co-location ITL spike `20→100 ms`; §6 the `transfer ≪ prefill` budget) · `llm/KTRANSFORMERS_OFFLOAD.md` (§0 the weights-stay/activations-fly idea; §2 layer-offload `350 GB / 64 GB/s ≈ 0.18 tok/s`; §3 the `~14 KB` activation transfer; §6 why MoE sparsity enables it).
- **Canonical:** their cited sources — vLLM *CUDA Graphs* design docs (docs.vllm.ai) · NVIDIA Developer Blog, *Constructing CUDA Graphs with Dynamic Parameters* · Zhong et al., *DistServe* (OSDI'24, arXiv:2401.09670) §1–§2 · Chen et al., *KTransformers* (SOSP'25) + the ktransformers repo (github.com/kvcache-ai/ktransformers) · DeepSeek-V3 Technical Report (arXiv:2412.19437).
- **Goal:** state, from memory, the *one problem each technique solves* and *which prior unit it builds on* — and when you would (and would not) reach for each.

### 2. Research (web-verify, ≥2 sources each)
- **(a) CUDA graphs — decode repeats one fixed kernel sequence every step, and in eager PyTorch every kernel launch is a separate CPU→GPU call costing ~5–14 µs of driver overhead that does zero FLOPs; with ~50–100 kernels/step that is a large fixed per-step tax.** A CUDA Graph captures the whole sequence once and replays it with a single `graph.replay()` call, collapsing `N` launches into one. It is applied to the **decode** path specifically because decode is the fixed-shape, repeated phase (one token/seq/step, tie 1.4) where the launch tax is largest; prefill's variable length *cannot* be captured. `Verifies:` NVIDIA Developer Blog, *Constructing CUDA Graphs with Dynamic Parameters* — "A *graph* groups a set of CUDA kernels and other CUDA operations together and executes them with a specified dependency tree. It speeds up the workflow by **combining the driver activities associated with CUDA kernel launches** and CUDA API calls" (https://developer.nvidia.com/blog/constructing-cuda-graphs-with-dynamic-parameters/) + Spheron, *torch.compile and CUDA Graphs for LLM Inference* — "How CUDA Graphs Cut Kernel Launch Overhead. A CUDA graph records every kernel launch in a forward pass during a warm-up phase, then replays" (https://www.spheron.network/blog/torch-compile-cuda-graphs-llm-inference-pytorch-2-6/) · corroborated by the vLLM GitHub issue — "kernel launch adds overhead, especially when you're generating token after token. **CUDA graphs let vLLM 'record' that entire sequence**" (https://github.com/vllm-project/vllm/issues/14632). *(The `72 × 10 µs = 720 µs` per-step figure and the batch-scaling table are in `llm/CUDA_GRAPHS.md` §2/§6; an independent benchmark — Thomas 2025 — measures +12.5% throughput / −12.3% inter-token latency on Qwen2.5-0.5B, i.e. a modest *total* step win because compute dominates.)*
- **(b) Disaggregated serving (DistServe) — prefill (compute-bound) and decode (memory-bound) *fight* when co-located on one GPU: a long prefill serialized ahead of the decode batch stalls every running decode (ITL spikes). The fix is to physically split the two phases onto separate GPU pools, each tuned for its phase, transferring the KV cache the prefill produces to the decode pool over RDMA.** This eliminates the interference outright (neither phase blocks the other) and lets each pool be independently optimized (tensor-parallel for low TTFT, data-parallel for low ITL). `Verifies:` DistServe abstract (verified live) — "DistServe improves… serving by **disaggregating the prefill and decoding computation**. Existing LLM serving systems colocate the two phases… leads to strong **prefill-decoding interferences**… DistServe assigns prefill and decoding computation to **different GPUs**, hence **eliminating prefill-decoding interferences**… **7.4x more requests** or 12.6x tighter SLO" (arXiv:2401.09670, https://arxiv.org/abs/2401.09670) + DistServe §2.1 — prefill "tends to be compute-bound" while decode "is constrained by the GPU's memory bandwidth" (https://arxiv.org/html/2401.09670v3, the 1.4 split — prefill arithmetic intensity ≈ `L`, decode ≈ 1). *(The co-location ITL spike `20 → 100 ms = prefill + decode` and the `transfer ≪ prefill` budget are in `llm/DISAGGREGATED_SERVING.md` §2/§6; both rest on the real roofline arithmetic + published RDMA bandwidths.)*
- **(c) ktransformers — a 671B MoE model (DeepSeek-V3: 671B total / 37B active, 256 routed + 1 shared expert, `k=8`) is ~335 GB even at 4-bit, far beyond a consumer GPU; KTransformers keeps attention + the shared expert on the GPU and parks the routed expert *weights* in CPU DRAM (run via Intel AMX), so per token only the ~14 KB hidden-state *activations* cross PCIe — not the 350 GB weights.** The trick works specifically because **MoE is sparse**: only `k=8` of `E=256` experts run per token, so the CPU executes just 8 small SwiGLU GEMMs, not all 256. A dense FFN (no sparsity) would force *all* expert weights through CPU compute every token and get no such win. `Verifies:` ktransformers GitHub README (verified live) — "KTransformers is a research project focused on efficient inference… through **CPU-GPU heterogeneous computing**"; features "**MoE Optimization**: Efficient Mixture-of-Experts inference with NUMA-aware memory management", "**Heterogeneous expert placement** (hot experts on GPU, cold experts on CPU)", "AMX/AVX Acceleration: Intel AMX and AVX512/AVX2 optimized kernels for INT4/INT8 quantized inference" (https://github.com/kvcache-ai/ktransformers) + the SOSP'25 paper (Chen et al.) — *"KTransformers: Unleashing the Full Potential of CPU/GPU Hybrid Inference for **MoE Models**,"* targeting 671B DeepSeek-V3/R1 (DOI 10.1145/3731569.3764843, cited in `llm/KTRANSFORMERS_OFFLOAD.md` Sources) · the DeepSeek-V3 spec (671B/37B, 256 experts, k=8) is from arXiv:2412.19437. *(The `350 GB / 64 GB/s ≈ 0.18 tok/s` layer-offload ceiling and the `~14 KB` activation figure are in `llm/KTRANSFORMERS_OFFLOAD.md` §2/§3; the weights-vs-activations byte ratio is ~25,000×.)*

### 3. Questions (answer aloud, no notes)
1. In one line each, what problem does **CUDA graphs**, **disaggregated serving**, and **ktransformers** solve — and which prior unit does each build on (1.4 / 3.11–3.13)?
2. When would you reach for **CUDA graphs** — what must profiling show, and why is it decode-only (why can't you graph prefill)?
3. When would you reach for **disaggregated serving**, and what does it cost you (the one thing co-location gives you for free that disaggregation charges for)?
4. Why does disaggregated serving split prefill and decode onto **separate pools** rather than just scheduling them better on one GPU? (tie 1.4 — the interference is physical)
5. Why does **ktransformers** work for **MoE** models specifically — what property of MoE makes CPU offload feasible, and why would it barely help a dense LLM?
6. Why are all three "advanced" — what must be true before you reach for *any* of them, and what goes wrong if you apply them prematurely?

### 4. Demo `.py`
- File: `advanced_serving.py`. A light, **fully deterministic** three-part comparison sketch — one calc per technique. **Determinism:** every shape, latency, and bandwidth is a pinned constant; no `random`/`Date.now()`/network; output byte-stable on re-run.
- **Part A — CUDA graphs (the gold).** Pin `num_layers=24`, `kernels_per_layer=3` → `N_kernels=72`; `launch_us_per_kernel=10.0`, `graph_replay_us=5.0`, `compute_us_per_kernel=8.0`. Eager launch overhead = `72 × 10 = 720 µs/step`; graphed = `5 µs/step` (one replay). Pin a decode rate of **`STEPS_PER_SEC=50`** (~20 ms/step). Recovered launch-overhead = `50 × (720 − 5) = 35750 µs/s`. **Gold value (must match on re-run): overhead saved = `35.75 ms/s`** at 50 steps/s — ~3.6% of wall-clock CPU/GPU bookkeeping reclaimed, every second, for free on the decode path. (Print the launch speedup `720/5 = 144×` and note the *total* step speedup is smaller because compute is identical — `1296 → 581 µs`, the batch-scaling insight from `CUDA_GRAPHS.md` §6.)
- **Part B — disaggregated serving (the contention).** Pin `decode_step_ms=20`, `prefill_ms=80` (a 512-token prefill), a window of `10` steps with prefills arriving at steps 5 and 10. Co-located (1 GPU, prefill priority): decode ITL spikes to `prefill + decode = 100 ms` on those steps → timeline `[20,20,20,20,100,20,20,20,20,100]`, **max 100 ms, avg 36.0 ms**. Disaggregated (2 pools): prefill runs on the *separate* prefill pool, decode ITL stays flat → **max 20 ms, avg 20.0 ms** (spike factor `100/20 = 5.0×`).
- **Part C — ktransformers (the byte contrast, light).** Pin `model_bytes=350 GB` (4-bit DeepSeek-V3 671B) vs `activation_bytes=14336` (~14 KB per expert call). Layer offload streams the *weights* every token (`350 GB / 64 GB/s ≈ 0.18 tok/s`); expert offload streams only activations → byte ratio `3.5e11 / 14336 ≈ 24,414,062×`. MoE sparsity: only `k=8` of `E=256` experts run/token → `256/8 = 32×` less CPU compute than a dense FFN.
- Print ≥2 `[check]` lines: `[check] cuda graph overhead savings > 0: 715 us/step -> 35.75 ms/s recovered at 50 steps/s` · `[check] disaggregated decode ITL flat under prefill (max 20.0 ms == decode_step, no contention)` · `[check] co-located ITL spikes to prefill+decode (max 100.0 ms) -> interference visible` · `[check] ktransformers: weights-stay/activations-fly byte ratio 24414062x > 1000 (MoE sparsity E/k=32x)`.
- **Extension (make it yours):** (1) **Batch sweep on CUDA graphs** — re-print the per-step savings at `steps_per_sec ∈ {10, 50, 100}` and show the launch-overhead *fraction* of the step shrinks as batch grows (the tax is fixed, compute scales with batch → graphs help most at small/latency-sensitive batch). (2) **The disaggregation budget** — compute `Latency_transfer = Size_KV / bandwidth` for Llama-3-8B at `S=512` (`64 MiB`) over 400G RoCEv2 (`~1.34 ms`) and confirm `transfer ≪ prefill` (so the split is worth its ~1.7% TTFT cost). (3) **Dense vs MoE** — re-run Part C with `E=k=1` (dense) and show the CPU must run *all* weights every token → no sparsity win, which is exactly why ktransformers targets MoE.

### 5. What to teach
- **Title:** *Three advanced serving tricks (and when you'd need them).*
- **Angle:** beyond the basics — three techniques, each a reaction to one profiled bottleneck. One paragraph each: **CUDA graphs** cut per-step CPU launch overhead; **disaggregated serving** splits prefill/decode onto separate pools to kill their interference; **ktransformers** runs giant MoE on mixed CPU+GPU by parking expert weights in DRAM.
- **Payload:** the three problems + their one-line fixes · which prior unit each builds on (CUDA graphs ↔ 1.4 decode + 3.11 vLLM; disaggregated ↔ 1.4 interference; ktransformers ↔ 1.4 + MoE sparsity) · the overhead-savings gold (`35.75 ms/s`) · the co-location ITL spike (`20 → 100 ms`) · the weights-stay/activations-fly ratio (`~25,000×`).
- **Gotcha:** all three are advanced — only reach for them when **profiling shows the specific bottleneck they fix**: launch overhead dominating small-batch decode (graphs), prefill/decode interference breaking your ITL SLO (disaggregation), or a 671B MoE that won't fit any other way (ktransformers). Applying them prematurely is engineering theater — the basics (3.11–3.13) already get you most of the way.
- **Video (`T3`):** Hook(when the basics aren't enough) → Mechanism(3 techniques, 1 line each: record-replay / split pools / weights-stay-activations-fly) → Gold(overhead savings `35.75 ms/s`) → Gotcha(premature optimization — profile first).

- [ ] gold value reproduced, matches reference
- [ ] ≥2 web sources logged with `Verifies:` lines
- [ ] article centers on one annotated diagram + ≥1 gotcha
- [ ] HyperFrames video renders, 6 beats, gold-check badge green
